1 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


E & J Equities, LLC v. Bd. of Adjustment of the Tp. of Franklin


A-40 September Term 2014, 075207


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


226 N.J. 549; 146 A.3d 623; 2016 N.J. LEXIS 890


March 1, 2016, Argued 
September 15, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 437 N.J. Super. 490, 100 A.3d 539 (App. Div. 2014) [***1] .
E & J Equities, LLC v. Board of Adjustment of Tp. of Franklin, 437 N.J. Super. 490, 100 A.3d 539, 2014 N.J. Super. LEXIS 143 (App.Div., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Court held that a digital billboard, as a form of communication, is subject to the protections afforded to speech under the First Amendment to the United States Constitution, U.S. Const. amend. I, and N.J. Const. art. I, para. 6 and to the extent that a municipality seeks to restrict billboards, the regulation must find support in the governmental interests that the municipality seeks to protect or advance; [2]-In the case on appeal, the Court held that although the township relied upon aesthetic and public safety concerns in banning digital billboards, while permitting static billboards in designated zones, the record failed to demonstrate that the ban furthered the governmental interests that the township asserts, thus, the ordinance ban on digital billboards was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

 

2 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Meehan v. Antonellis


A-45 September Term 2014, 075265


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


226 N.J. 216; 141 A.3d 1162; 2016 N.J. LEXIS 850


March 15, 2016, Argued 
August 9, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Meehan v. Antonellis, 2014 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2066 (App.Div., Aug. 21, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The enhanced requirements of the Patients First Act, N.J.S.A. § 2A:53A-41, which govern the qualifications of persons permitted to submit an affidavit of merit, or provide expert testimony, in a medical malpractice action, apply only in medical malpractice actions and in all other actions against a licensed professional, N.J.S.A. § 2A:53A-27 of the Affidavit of Merit statute, N.J.S.A. §§ 2A:53A-26 to 2A:53-29, statute prescribes the qualifications of the person who may submit an affidavit of merit against a licensed professional; [2]-As a result, the trial court improperly dismissed plaintiff's complaint because the affidavit of merit that plaintiff submitted in the action, from a licensed dentist with experience in the treatment of sleep apnea, satisfied N.J.S.A. § 2A:53A-27.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed; case remanded to trial court for further proceedings.

 

3 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Lunsford


A-61 September Term 2014, 075691


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


226 N.J. 129; 141 A.3d 270; 2016 N.J. LEXIS 717


February 29, 2016, Argued 
August 1, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Corrected August 3, 2016.
Motion denied by State v. Lunsford, 2017 N.J. LEXIS 249 (N.J., Mar. 7, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Lunsford, 223 N.J. 159, 121 A.3d 384, 2015 N.J. LEXIS 1098 (2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-As a long-standing feature of New Jersey law, telephone billing records are entitled to protection from government access under N.J. Const. art. I, para. 7 because they reveal details of one's private affairs that are similar to what bank and credit card records disclose, those areas of information should receive the same level of constitutional protection and be available based on a showing of relevance; [2]-The Court held that direct judicial oversight of the process is required to guard against the possibility of abuse, and in order to obtain a court order requiring production of telephone billing records, the State must present specific and articulable facts to demonstrate that the records are relevant and material to an ongoing criminal investigation.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

4 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Johnson v. Roselle EZ Quick LLC


A-33 September Term 2014, 075044


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


226 N.J. 370; 143 A.3d 254; 2016 N.J. LEXIS 712


January 5, 2016, Argued 
July 27, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Johnson v. Roselle EZ Quick LLC, 2014 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2089 (App.Div., Aug. 25, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The 2011 amendment to N.J.S.A. § 39:6A-9.1 does not expressly or implicitly present any of the factors necessary to rebut the presumption that, as a newly enacted law, it should be applied prospectively; [2]-As a result, the Court held that the amendment did not apply to plaintiff's claims for personal injuries, thus, the trial court properly granted the insurer's motion for summary judgment on its claim for reimbursement of the Personal Injury Protection benefits that it paid to plaintiff.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

5 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Scriven


A-11 September Term 2015, 075682


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


226 N.J. 20; 140 A.3d 535; 2016 N.J. LEXIS 698


April 26, 2016, Argued 
July 20, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Court held that the lower courts properly upheld the grant of defendant's motion to suppress evidence obtained from a motor-vehicle stop as violative of U.S. Const. amend. IV, and N.J. Const. art. I, para. 7, because the language of the high-beam statute, N.J.S.A. § 39:3-60, was unambiguous and required drivers to dim their high beams only when approaching an oncoming vehicle, thus, neither a car parked on a perpendicular street, such as defendant's, nor an on-foot police officer count as an oncoming vehicle.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

 

6 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


In re Forfeiture of Pers. Weapons and Firearms Identification Card belonging to F.M.


A-60 September Term 2014, 074964


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


225 N.J. 487; 139 A.3d 67; 2016 N.J. LEXIS 688


March 1, 2016, Argued 
June 30, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
In re State for the Forfeiture of Pers. Weapons & Firearms Identification Card Belonging to F.M., 2014 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2092 (App.Div., Aug. 25, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Court held that the Family Part applied an incorrect legal standard and its conclusions were not supported by substantial, credible evidence in the record because the record established that the return of the husband's personal weapon and identification card was inconsistent with the Domestic Violence Act, N.J.S.A. § 2C:58-3(c)(5), and, therefore, his weapon and identification card were forfeited; [2]-The Court found that the Family Part judge's failure to recognize that N.J.S.A. § 2C:58-3(c)(5) did not require that an individual possess a diagnosable disorder to be disqualified from possessing a gun was particularly significant, because the unchallenged expert testimony from two doctors was that the husband was not fit to possess a firearm.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed; case remanded to trial court for entry of an order forfeiting the husband's weapon and firearms purchaser identification card.

 

7 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Berg v. Christie


A-71/72 September Term 2014, 074612


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


225 N.J. 245; 137 A.3d 1143; 2016 N.J. LEXIS 561; 166 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P61,715


March 14, 2016, Argued 
June 9, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 436 N.J. Super. 220, 93 A.3d 387 (App. Div. 2014) [***1] .
Berg v. Christie, 436 N.J. Super. 220, 93 A.3d 387, 2014 N.J. Super. LEXIS 92 (App.Div., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Court held that to construe a statute as creating a contractual right, the New Jersey Legislature's intent to limit the subsequent exercise of legislative power must be clearly and unequivocally expressed concerning both the creation of a contract as well as the terms of the contractual obligation; [2]-The Court found that in the case at bar involving public retirees challenging the State's suspension of pension cost-of-living adjustments (COLAs), proof of unequivocal intent to create a non-forfeitable right to yet-unreceived COLAs was lacking, thus, the Legislature retained its inherent sovereign right to act in its best judgment of the public interest and to pass legislation suspending further COLAs.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the appellate division reversed; trial court's judgment dismissing the complaints reinstated.

 

8 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Feliciano


A-24 September Term 2014, 074395


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


224 N.J. 351; 132 A.3d 1245; 2016 N.J. LEXIS 229


December 2, 2015, Argued 
March 9, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Feliciano, 2014 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1026 (App.Div., May 6, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Court held that when a target purposely changes facilities to avoid detection, law enforcement officers may switch over and begin to monitor a new facility under the State's wiretap law, N.J.S.A. § 2A:156A-9(g)(2), provided they have otherwise fully complied with the statute and going forward, law enforcement must notify a wiretap judge within 48 hours of the switch and obtain authorization to continue monitoring the new facility; [2]-In response to defendant's challenge, the Court found no abuse of discretion occurred by the orders that allowed 24/7 monitoring interception as the orders were consistent with N.J.S.A. § 2A:156A-12 and directed the drug task force to make reasonable efforts to reduce those hours whenever possible and the judge observed that the targets conducted sales at unpredictable times, most occurring outside the given window.


OUTCOME: As modified, judgment affirmed.

 

9 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Major v. Maguire


A-110 September Term 2013, 074345


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


224 N.J. 1; 128 A.3d 675; 2016 N.J. LEXIS 1


September 17, 2015, Argued 
January 12, 2016, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Major v. Maguire, 2014 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 874 (App.Div., Apr. 17, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where plaintiffs, who commenced an action under the Grandparent Visitation Statute, N.J.S.A. § 9:2-7.1, alleged in detail their involvement in their granddaughter's life from birth and contended that their alienation from the child would cause her harm, and, based on these allegations, established a prima facie showing of harm to the child at the pleading stage, as required by Moriarty v. Bradt, the trial court should have denied defendant mother's motion to dismiss and given plaintiffs the opportunity to satisfy their burden of proving harm; [2]-Procedural guidelines were established by the high court for proceedings under § 9:2-7.1; [3]-The trial court erred in holding that the grandparents could not institute litigation before visitation was denied with finality, as no such threshold requirement was imposed by § 9:2-7.1 or the case law construing it.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed as modified.

 

10 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Cashin v. Bello


A-98 September Term 2013, 073215


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


223 N.J. 328; 123 A.3d 1042; 2015 N.J. LEXIS 964


March 3, 2015, Argued 
October 7, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Cashin v. Bello, 2013 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1883 (App.Div., July 22, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Court held that the New Jersey Legislature's use of the word building, in its singular form, to be both deliberate and dispositive under the Anti-Eviction Act, N.J.S.A. §§ 2A:18-61.1 to 2A:18-61.12, and that the term designates a discreet physical structure, not a number of such structures connected by nothing more than the ownership of the land on which they sit; [2]-Applying that interpretation of N.J.S.A. § 2A:18-61.1(l)(3) to the facts, the Court held that plaintiff was within her rights as a landowner to remove defendant from the detached garage that served as a rental apartment because it was a separate building with fewer than three residential units, and plaintiff owner sought to occupy the unit that defendant was renting in that building, therefore, plaintiff could evict defendant under N.J.S.A. § 2A:18-61.1(l)(3).


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

 

11 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Witt


A-9 September Term 2014, 074468


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


223 N.J. 409; 126 A.3d 850; 2015 N.J. LEXIS 890


April 14, 2014, Argued 
September 24, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 435 N.J. Super. 608, 90 A.3d 664 (App. Div. 2014) [***1] .
State v. Witt, 435 N.J. Super. 608, 90 A.3d 664, 2014 N.J. Super. LEXIS 72 (App.Div., 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Court held that the exigent-circumstances test in Cooke and Pena-Flores no longer applies and it returns to the standard set forth in Alston for warrantless searches of automobiles based on probable cause; [2]-As such, the Court held that searches on the roadway based on probable cause arising from unforeseeable and spontaneous circumstances are permissible, however, when vehicles are towed and impounded, absent some exigency, a warrant must be secured; [3]-The Court determined that its decision was a new rule of law and will be given prospective application from the date of the opinion.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed; case remanded to trial court for further proceedings.

State v. Buckner


A-22 September Term 2014, 074390


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


223 N.J. 1; 121 A.3d 290; 2015 N.J. LEXIS 814


April 28, 2015, Argued 
July 30, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Buckner, 437 N.J. Super. 8, 96 A.3d 261, 2014 N.J. Super. LEXIS 60 (App.Div.2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a suit wherein defendant challenged his criminal conviction on the basis that the Recall Statute, N.J.S.A. § 43:6A-13, which allows retired judges to be recalled for temporary judicial service, was unconstitutional, the Court held that defendant failed to show beyond a reasonable doubt that the Recall Statute was clearly repugnant to the New Jersey Constitution, N.J. Const. art. VI, § 6, para. 3; [2]-The Court held that the Recall Statute was consistent with both the language and the history of the modern New Jersey Constitution, N.J. Const. art. VI, § 6, para. 3, and did not violate the separation of powers doctrine, N.J. Const. art. III, para. 1.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

State v. Reece


A-79-13, A-80-13 September Term 2013, 073284


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


222 N.J. 154; 117 A.3d 1235; 2015 N.J. LEXIS 800


April 14, 2015, Argued 
July 20, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from and certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Reece, 2013 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2138 (App.Div., Aug. 28, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Defendant's conviction for resisting arrest was affirmed and his conviction for obstruction was reinstated because the emergency-aid doctrine justified the officers' warrantless entry into defendant's home since a dropped 9-1-1 call originating therefrom permitted the officers to presume that there was an emergency and observations of defendant denying others were in the home, an abrasion on his hand, and his agitation gave an objectively reasonable basis to believe that an emergency existed.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed, in part; defendant's conviction for resisting arrest affirmed; defendant's conviction for obstruction reinstated.

State v. Saavedra


A-68 September Term 2013, 073793


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


222 N.J. 39; 117 A.3d 1169; 2015 N.J. LEXIS 641; 127 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 733


November 10, 2014, Argued 
June 23, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 433 N.J. Super. 501, 81 A.3d 693 (App. Div. 2013) [***1] .
State v. Saavedra, 433 N.J. Super. 501, 81 A.3d 693, 2013 N.J. Super. LEXIS 185 (App.Div., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Court held that the trial court properly denied defendant's motion to dismiss the indictment charging official misconduct and theft by unlawful taking of public documents, in violation of N.J.S.A. § 2C:30-2, because the State presented to the grand jury a prima facie showing with respect to the elements of each offense charged and did not withhold from the grand jury exculpatory information or a charge regarding a defense that it was compelled by law to present; [2]-The Court held that defendant's indictment did not violate due process standards or New Jersey public policy by conflicting with the Court's decision in Quinlan, which did not govern the application of the criminal laws at issue in the appeal.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed; case remanded to trial court for further proceedings.

 

15 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Burgos v. State


A-55 September Term 2014, 075736


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


222 N.J. 175; 118 A.3d 270; 2015 N.J. LEXIS 566; 165 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P61,598


May 6, 2015, Argued 
June 9, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by N.J. Educ. Ass'n v. New Jersey, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 1017 (U.S., Feb. 29, 2016)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Burgos v. New Jersey, 2016 U.S. LEXIS 1140 (U.S., Feb. 29, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Mercer County.
Burgos v. State, 2015 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1786 (Law Div., Feb. 23, 2015)



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the trial court erred in granting plaintiffs summary judgment in their suit alleging the State's failure to appropriate funds to reduce the unfunded liability of state pensions' impaired public employees' contract rights. HOLDINGS: [1]-Chapter 78, L. 2011, c. 78, which amended N.J.S.A. § 43:3C-9.5(c), did not create a legally enforceable contract that was entitled to constitutional protection; the Debt Limitation Clause, N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 2, para. 3, interdicted the creation, in this manner, of a legally binding enforceable contract compelling multi-year financial payments in the sizable amounts called for by Chapter 78; [2]-Under the Appropriations Clause, N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 2, para. 2, the legislature had the sole authority to appropriate funds; therefore, the Judiciary had no authority to redress the legislature's refusal to take action.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

16 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


388 Route 22 Readington Realty Holdings, LLC v. Township of Readington


A-63 September Term 2013, 073322


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


221 N.J. 318; 113 A.3d 744; 2015 N.J. LEXIS 503


December 2, 2014, Argued 
May 5, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
388 Route 22 Readington Realty Holdings, LLC v. Twp. of Readington, 2013 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2194 (App.Div., Sept. 4, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court properly denied a developer's challenge to a township's sewer allocation ordinance because it provided adequate standards to guide the exercise of municipal discretion when considering the repurchase of sewer permits from private entities, and did not delegate to private parties the township's authority to control access to sewer capacity; [2]-The trial court properly ordered the township to provide a reasoned basis for refusing to recapture the unused sewer capacity held by multiple private entities; as a blanket policy of not recapturing unused sewer capacity was the functional equivalent of a moratorium on development, the high court approved of the trial court's approach, requiring the township both to undertake a detailed analysis of the unused capacity in the hands of private parties and to explain whether any of that capacity could be recalled.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed in part and reversed in part.

 

17 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Adkins


A-91 September Term 2013, 073803


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


221 N.J. 300; 113 A.3d 734; 2015 N.J. LEXIS 393


December 3, 2014, Argued 
May 4, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by State v. Adkins, 2016 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 699 (App.Div., Mar. 31, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 433 N.J. Super. 479, 81 A.3d 680 (App. Div. 2013) [***1] .
State v. Adkins, 433 N.J. Super. 479, 81 A.3d 680, 2013 N.J. Super. LEXIS 179 (App.Div., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Court held that the United States Supreme Court's decision in McNeely, which holds that in drunk-driving investigations, the natural dissipation of alcohol in the bloodstream does not constitute an exigency in every case sufficient to justify conducting a blood test without a warrant, applied retroactively to cases that were in the pipeline when McNeely was issued; [2]-As a result, the Court held that the lower court's decision in the case at bar was reversed and the case was remanded to the trial court for the State and defendant to have the opportunity to re-present their respective positions on exigency in a hearing on defendant's motion to suppress the admissibility of the blood test results.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed; case remanded to trial court for further proceedings.

62-64 Main Street, L.L.C. v. Mayor and Council of City of Hackensack


A-19, A-20 September Term 2013, 072699


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


221 N.J. 129; 110 A.3d 877; 2015 N.J. LEXIS 279


October 7, 2014, Argued 
March 23, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
62-64 Main St., L.L.C. v. Mayor & Council of Hackensack, 2013 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1031 (App.Div., May 3, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Court held that N.J.S.A. § 40A:12A-5(a), (b), and (d) do not violate the Blighted Areas Clause of N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 3, para. 1; [2]-The Court further held that a determination that an area is blighted and in need of redevelopment does not require a finding that the area negatively affects surrounding properties, so long as the legislative definitions are met; [3]-The Court held that substantial evidence in the record supported the city planning board's findings, later adopted by the mayor and council, that plaintiffs' lots were part of an area in need of redevelopment based on the buildings being vacant, in a deteriorated condition, substandard and unsafe for occupancy, and untenantable, and the entirety of the property, including the parking lot, suffered from a faulty arrangement or design.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

State v. Pomianek


A-32/33 September Term 2013, 072293


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


221 N.J. 66; 110 A.3d 841; 2015 N.J. LEXIS 275; 126 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 963


October 20, 2014, Argued 
March 17, 2015, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by, Remanded by State v. Pomianek, 2016 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 597 (App.Div., Mar. 18, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 429 N.J. Super. 339, 58 A.3d 1205 (App. Div. 2013) [***1] .
State v. Pomianek, 429 N.J. Super. 339, 58 A.3d 1205, 2013 N.J. Super. LEXIS 10 (App.Div., 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Court held that N.J.S.A. § 2C:16-1(a)(3) fails to give adequate notice of conduct that it proscribes, is unconstitutionally vague, and violates the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. XIV; [2]-The Court noted that defendant was convicted not based on what he was thinking but rather on his failure to appreciate what the victim was thinking.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the Appellate Division reversed; convictions for bias-intimidation and misconduct-in-office dismissed; case remanded to trial court for further proceedings.

 

21 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Perez


A-25 September Term 2013, 072624


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


220 N.J. 423; 106 A.3d 1212; 2015 N.J. LEXIS 59


September 8, 2014, Argued 
February 2, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Perez, 2013 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1055 (App.Div., May 6, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-As defendant's admissions during the colloquy that he knew the victim was under 18 and that he sent text messages to him in an attempt to lure him to a place where they could have sexual relations, in combination with the text messages he sent to the victim, which were introduced at the plea hearing, set forth a sufficient factual basis to support his guilty plea to child luring, the trial court properly denied his motion to withdraw his plea; [2]-However, because community supervision for life (CSL) and parole supervision for life were non-interchangeable, distinct post-sentence supervisory schemes for certain sex offenders, and as defendant was serving a special sentencing condition of CSL at the time of his offenses, defendant's extended-term sentences imposed pursuant to N.J.S.A. § 2C:43-6.4 were illegal as they violated the U.S. and New Jersey Ex Post Facto Clauses.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed in part and reversed in part and the matter was remanded to the trial court for resentencing.

 

22 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Tate


A-46 September Term 2013, 072754


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


220 N.J. 393; 106 A.3d 1195; 2015 N.J. LEXIS 58


November 10, 2014, Argued 
February 2, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Tate, 2013 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1273 (App.Div., May 28, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court accepted defendant's guilty plea to abusing his foster child by the habitual use of profane, indecent, or obscene language, N.J.S.A. § 9:6-1(d), based solely on his admission that he used curse words and off-color language in the child's presence in a way that would debauch his morals; it denied his subsequent motion to vacate the plea, and the intermediate appellate court affirmed. The high court held that factual basis provided by defendant during the plea colloquy was inadequate to support the guilty plea because it did not satisfy the elements of § 9:6-1(d); the trial court did not ask him to repeat the offending language or to state how often he used that language, and did not assess whether his conception of "off-color language" was equivalent to the statutory language prohibition.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was reversed.

 

23 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Griepenburg v. Township of Ocean


A-55 September Term 2013, 073290


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


220 N.J. 239; 105 A.3d 1082; 2015 N.J. LEXIS 40


November 12, 2014, Argued 
January 22, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Griepenburg v. Twp. of Ocean, 2013 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2154 (App.Div., Aug. 29, 2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a suit brought by property owners challenging ordinances that rezoned much of their 34 acres from residential and commercial use to an Environmental Conservation district thereby restricting the future development of their property, the Court reversed the appellate court's reversal of the trial court's grant of summary judgment to the township; [2]-The Court held that the ordinances represented a legitimate exercise of the municipality's power to zone property consistent with its Master Plan and Master Plan and Land Use Law, N.J.S.A. §§ 40:55D-1 to 40:55D-163; [3]-The Court further held that the property owners failed to overcome the ordinances' presumption of validity.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the appellate court reversed; judgment of the trial court reinstated.

May 5, 2014, Reargued

In re Civil Commitment of D.Y., 426 N.J. Super. 436, 45 A.3d 394, 2012 N.J. Super. LEXIS 101 (App.Div., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Court held that the plain language of Sexually Violent Predator Act (SVPA), N.J.S.A. §§ 30:4-27.29(c) to 30:4-27.31(a), requires that there be one of two alternative forms of representation at SVPA commitment hearings: full representation by counsel, or self-representation by an individual who is competent to conduct his or her case, with standby counsel present throughout the hearing to assist if needed; [2]-The Court held that standby counsel may advise the committee, assist the court in expediting the proceedings, and assume an active role if his or her client proves unwilling or unable to participate cooperatively in the hearing; [3]-As such, it was error for the trial judge to refuse the committee's request for self-representation with retained or appointed standby counsel present to assist them if necessary.

James v. New Jersey Mfrs. Ins. Co.

SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
James v. New Jersey Mfrs. Ins. Co., 2012 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1610 (App.Div., July 5, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-As of its effective date of September 10, 2007, N.J.S.A. § 17:28-1.1(f), addressing the step down provisions of uninsured or underinsured motorist (UM/UIM) coverage applied to and prospectively reformed, for employees, a corporation's or business entity's motor vehicle liability policy containing UM/UIM step-down provisions, including policies that were in force at that time; [2]-The Court found no exceptions to the rule favoring prospective application of new legislation pertained to N.J.S.A. § 17:28-1.1(f); [3]-As a result, since the employee's accident preceded N.J.S.A. § 17:28-1.1(f)'s effective date, his claims were governed by the provisions of the insurer's policy that were in existence as of the date of his accident.

In re Plan for the Abolition of the Council on Affordable Housing


A-127 September Term 2011, A-14 September Term 2012, 070426


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


214 N.J. 444; 70 A.3d 559; 2013 N.J. LEXIS 727


January 28, 2013, Argued 
July 10, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to and appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 424 N.J. Super. 410, 38 A.3d 620 (2012).
In re Plan for Abolition of Council on Affordable Housing, 424 N.J. Super. 410, 38 A.3d 620, 2012 N.J. Super. LEXIS 33 (App.Div., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff housing center brought suit against defendants, the Governor and New Jersey Department of Community Affairs (DCA), challenging a Reorganization Plan adopted by the governor that abolished the New Jersey Council on Affordable Housing. The New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, invalidated the Plan. The governor and the DCA petitioned for certification, which was granted.


OVERVIEW: The Legislature created the Council on Affordable Housing (COAH) to ensure that municipalities fulfilled their constitutional obligation to provide affordable housing. The Governor issued a Reorganization Plan that abolished COAH and transferred its responsibilities to DCA, relying on the New Jersey Executive Reorganization Act of 1969, N.J.S.A. §§ 52:14C-1 to 52:14C-11. The high court held that because COAH was "in, but not of," an Executive Branch department, the plain language of the Reorganization Act, which extended the Chief Executive's authority only to agencies that were "of the executive branch," N.J.S.A. § 52:14C-3(a)(1), did not encompass, and thus did not authorize the Governor to abolish, an independent agency like COAH. To abolish independent agencies, the legislative and executive branches had to enact new laws that were passed by the Senate and Assembly and signed by the Governor.


OUTCOME: The high court modified and affirmed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court.

 

40 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


New Jersey Dept. of Envir. Prot. v. Huber


A-116 September Term 2010, 065540


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


213 N.J. 338; 63 A.3d 197; 2013 N.J. LEXIS 216


October 23, 2012, Argued 
April 4, 2013, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
N.J. Dep't of Envtl. Prot. v. Huber, 2010 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 121 (App.Div., Jan. 20, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant homeowners appealed the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which upheld the decision and findings of plaintiff, the New Jersey Department of Environmental Protection (DEP), that the homeowners violated the Freshwater Wetlands Protection Act (FWPA), N.J.S.A. §§ 13:9B-1 to 13:9B-30, and were subject to the administrative penalty and restoration remedy ordered by the DEP.


OVERVIEW: The homeowners owned land that was developed by prior owners subject to conditions of a duly recorded FWPA permit that controlled activities in certain areas of the property. The DEP became involved after a neighbor complained that the homeowners were placing fill and mowing vegetation in restricted areas. A DEP supervisor conducted various inspections and confirmed the activities in the protected areas. The DEP imposed an administrative penalty and ordered restoration. The homeowners filed various appeals and, for the first time in its appeal to the Court, raised the issue that the evidence collected by the DEP supervisor should have been excluded because he had not obtained an administrative search warrant. The court held that under the FWPA, a property owner receives the right to develop restricted land in exchange for giving the right of reasonable entry to the DEP to inspect. As such, the DEP supervisor lawfully entered the property. Further, the violations found were supported by sufficient evidence based on the aerial and other photographs, the homeowners' own admissions, and the disturbance of wetlands and the transition area.


OUTCOME: As modified, the Court affirmed the judgment of the Appellate Division.

 

41 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Jersey Cent. Power & Light Co. v. Melcar Utility Co.


A-96 September Term 2010, 067444


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


212 N.J. 576; 59 A.3d 561; 2013 N.J. LEXIS 86


November 29, 2011, Argued 
January 16, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Jersey Cent. Power & Light Co. v. Melcar Util. Co., 2010 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2907 (App.Div., Dec. 6, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The petition for certification filed by plaintiff, an electrical utility company, was granted allowing it to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed a trial court order dismissing plaintiff's suit against defendants, a utility company and a communications company. Plaintiff brought suit seeking reimbursement for costs incurred in repairing electrical lines damaged by defendants' excavation.


OVERVIEW: While performing excavation work for the purpose of installing underground cables for the communications company, the utility company damaged plaintiff's underground electrical lines. Defendants filed a motion to dismiss plaintiff's suit for lack of jurisdiction, citing the mandatory alternative dispute resolution provision of the Underground Facility Protection Act, N.J.S.A. § 48:2-80(d). The lower courts agreed that the case was subject to dismissal under § 48:2-80(d). The Court found error, concluding that § 48:2-80(d), as written, impinged on the constitutional right to a jury trial for property damages as it compelled parties seeking monetary relief for damages to underground facilities to submit such causes of action, if valued under a specified amount, to the Office of Dispute Settlement (ODS) for unspecified dispute resolution. The Court found that, on its face, the provision failed to preserve a party's ability to have the property damage dispute remain within or return to a judicial setting. The Court noted that neither it, nor the ODS, could write in a right to a trial de novo to save the statute from its constitutional shortcoming.


OUTCOME: The Court reversed the judgment dismissing plaintiff's cause of action and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

43 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Schubert


A-15 September Term 2011, 068149


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


212 N.J. 295; 53 A.3d 1210; 2012 N.J. LEXIS 1060


May 7, 2012, Argued 
October 22, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Schubert, 2011 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1010 (App.Div., Apr. 26, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed a trial court's action in amending defendant's judgment of conviction to include community supervision for life, pursuant to the Violent Predator Incapacitation Act, N.J.S.A. § 2C:43-6.4, after he had fully completed his sentence following his conviction for sexual assault.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had pled guilty in 2000 to sexual assault. During the plea colloquy, he acknowledged he would have to register. He was sentenced to three years probation and penalties, payments, and conditions were imposed. The trial court indicated that if defendant did all of those things, he would have no problem with the court. A judgment of conviction was entered that mirrored the orally-imposed terms. Defendant successfully completed his probationary sentence and was discharged from probation. More than seven years later, defendant was notified by the New Jersey State Parole Board that his sentence omitted the statutorily mandated community supervision for life provision. The trial court amended defendant's judgment of conviction to include community supervision for life. The Appellate Division concluded that amending defendant's judgment of conviction after he completed his sentence violated defendant's double jeopardy rights. The Court agreed, holding that while defendant's sentence was illegal because it omitted the statutorily mandated provision for community supervision for life, his sentence was completely served and was no longer subject to correction.


OUTCOME: The Court affirmed the judgment of the Appellate Division. The Court remanded the case to the trial court with directions to re-enter the original judgment of conviction.

 

44 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Harris


A-111 September Term 2010, 067929


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


211 N.J. 566; 50 A.3d 15; 2012 N.J. LEXIS 836


January 4, 2012, Argued 
August 16, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Harris, 2011 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 593 (App.Div., Mar. 10, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth appealed the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed a trial court's order granting defendant's motion to suppress. At issue was whether weapons recovered from defendant's premises during a search conducted pursuant to a warrant issued under the Prevention of Domestic Violence Act, N.J.S.A. §§ 2C:25-17 to 2C:25-35, could be admitted at a subsequent criminal prosecution.


OVERVIEW: A woman defendant was dating obtained a temporary restraining order against him. The trial court in that matter authorized the officers to search the premises for weapons. The officers conducted the search and found various weapons. A National Crime Information Center database search revealed one of the guns was stolen. Defendant was charged with several crimes, including possession of a loaded assault rifle and a stolen revolver. Defendant moved to suppress, arguing that the items found during the search conducted pursuant to a warrant issued under the domestic violence statute could not be used as evidence in a later criminal case. The lower courts agreed with defendant. The Court found error, concluding that items seized during a search conducted pursuant to a domestic violence warrant can serve as the basis for a subsequent criminal prosecution if their illegal nature is immediately apparent. The Court held that a check of the weapon's serial numbers is neither a seizure nor a search. As such, whether the officers could recognize immediately that the assault rifle and large capacity magazines were illegal to possess were factual determinations that had to be remanded.


OUTCOME: The Court reversed the judgment of the Appellate Division which affirmed the grant of defendant's motion to suppress and otherwise affirmed. The Court remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

45 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Trautmann ex rel. Trautmann v. Christie


A-16 September Term 2011, 067705


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


211 N.J. 300; 48 A.3d 1005; 2012 N.J. LEXIS 827


May 8, 2012, Argued 
August 6, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 418 N.J. Super. 559, 15 A.3d 22 (2011).
Trautmann ex rel. Trautmann v. Christie, 418 N.J. Super. 559, 15 A.3d 22, 2011 N.J. Super. LEXIS 33 (App.Div., 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff infants, through their guardians ad litem, sued defendants, the State of New Jersey and its Governor, challenging the validity of L. 2009, c. 37 (Chapter 37), called Kyleigh's Law, which amended N.J.S.A. § 39:3-13. The New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, upheld Chapter 37. Plaintiffs sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs argued that Chapter 37, which required certain drivers under 21 to display highly visible decals on their vehicles, contravened and was preempted by the Driver's Privacy Protection Act, 18 U.S.C.S. §§ 2721-2725. The high court disagreed. Chapter 37 required the disclosure of nothing more than the fact that a driver was under 21 and was the holder of a special learner's permit, examination permit, or probationary license; the driver's age group was neither "highly restricted personal information" within the meaning of § 2725(4), nor "personal information" within the meaning of § 2725(3). Chapter 37 met federal and state equal protection standards, as it bore a rational relation to a legitimate state interest--ensuring that only those qualified to do so were permitted to operate motor vehicles--with minimal intrusion on the drivers subject to its provisions. Chapter 37 did not give rise to an unreasonable search and seizure under U.S. Const. amend. IV or N.J. Const. art. I, para. 7, because the young drivers subject to Chapter 37 had no reasonable expectation of privacy in their age group, and an officer's review of the decal did not constitute a search.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.

 

46 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Edmonds


A-106 September Term 2010, 067889


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


211 N.J. 117; 47 A.3d 737; 2012 N.J. LEXIS 781


January 18, 2012, Argued 
July 26, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Edmonds, 2011 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 503 (App.Div., Mar. 3, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, charged with the unlawful possession of a gun, moved to suppress it on grounds that the warrantless search yielding the weapon was unconstitutional. The trial agreed and granted the motion. The State appealed; the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, affirmed. The State sought further review.


OVERVIEW: "John Smith" called 9-1-1 from a pay phone and said his sister's boyfriend was beating her and might have a gun. When police responded, a woman told them that there was no problem and that her young son was inside alone. Against her will, police entered her apartment to assure the boy's safety. He was unharmed, and there was no indication of a domestic disturbance. Police removed defendant from an adjoining room and patted him down; they found no weapons. They searched the area where he had been seated and found a handgun. The high court held that in responding to the 9-1-1 report of possible domestic violence, once the police officers found that there was inadequate evidence to corroborate the 9-1-1 report and determined that the parties' safety was not an issue, there was no objectively reasonable basis to search the residence under either the community-caretaking or emergency-aid exceptions to the warrant requirement and the evidence obtained through the warrantless search had to be suppressed. As to the emergency-aid exception, the test of State v. Frankel, 179 N.J. 586, was modified to remove as a factor the officer's subjective motivation for conducting the search.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.

 

47 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


DePascale v. State


A-34 September Term 2011, 069401


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


211 N.J. 40; 47 A.3d 690; 2012 N.J. LEXIS 778


March 26, 2012, Argued 
July 24, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Mercer County.
DePascale v. State, 2011 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2626 (Law Div., Oct. 17, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court granted direct certification to defendant, the State of New Jersey, allowing it to appeal the judgment of the Superior Court, Mercer County (New Jersey), which held the Pension and Health Care Benefits Act (Chapter 78), L. 2011, c. 78, unconstitutional as applied to sitting justices and judges. Plaintiff judge filed a complaint and order to show cause initiating the action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff's suit sought a judgment declaring that Chapter 78 violated N.J. Const. art. VI, § 6, para. 6 because it diminished the salary of justices and judges during their terms of service. Under Chapter 78, sitting justices and judges would be subject to a more than 400 percent increase in their pension contributions and a more than 100 percent increase in their health care contributions. Because the increased contributions were imposed without any corresponding salary increase, the take-home salaries of justices and judges would decrease in the range of $ 17,000 or more, representing a more than 10 percent decline in their disposable income. The trial court determined that Chapter 78 was unconstitutional. The trial court concluded that requiring justices and judges in service to make increased judicial contributions to the pension system, as set forth in Chapter 78, amounted to an unconstitutional diminution in judicial salaries. The trial court reached the same conclusion with respect to health benefits. The Court recognized that Chapter 78 served a legitimate public policy goal, but that goal, as applied to justices and judges, had to be achieved through constitutional means.


OUTCOME: The Court affirmed the judgment.

 

48 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Galicia


A-79 September Term 2010, 067018


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


210 N.J. 364; 45 A.3d 310; 2012 N.J. LEXIS 676


November 30, 2011, Argued 
June 19, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Galicia, 2010 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2330 (App.Div., Sept. 22, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury of aggravated manslaughter, N.J.S.A. § 2C:11-4(a)(1), as a lesser included offense of purposeful and knowing murder; second-degree aggravated assault; disorderly persons theft; and weapons charges. He appealed; his conviction and sentence were affirmed by the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division. He filed a petition for certification, which was granted.


OVERVIEW: After they exchanged blows, defendant drove a car toward his ex-boyfriend, who climbed on the hood. Defendant accelerated, drove several blocks, and abruptly stopped; the ex-boyfriend fell off, sustaining fatal injuries. The trial court charged the jury concerning passion/provocation manslaughter, § 2C:11-4(b)(2). The high court held defendant's romantic involvement with the victim and their fight did not support this instruction, as there was no evidence defendant acted in a jealous rage or in the heat of passion. Section § 2C:11-4(b)(2) did not violate equal protection, as the legislature could rationally conclude that aggravated manslaughter in violation of § 2C:11-4(a)(1), requiring extreme indifference to human life, should be punished more harshly than passion/provocation manslaughter. The verdict sheet improperly directed the jury not to consider the passion/provocation issue unless it found defendant guilty of murder, but as there was no evidentiary foundation for a § 2C:11-4(b)(2) instruction, the error was harmless. As defendant faced no imminent threat from the victim, the trial court's failure to sua sponte instruct the jury on self-defense was not plain error.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.

Mazdabrook Commons Homeowners' Ass'n v. Khan


A-65 September Term 2010, 067094


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


210 N.J. 482; 46 A.3d 507; 2012 N.J. LEXIS 668


October 24, 2011, Argued 
June 13, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Mazdabrook Commons Homeowners' Ass'n v. Wasim Khan, 2010 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2170 (App.Div., Sept. 1, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff homeowners' association sued defendant homeowner to collect maintenance fees and fines. He counterclaimed, alleging violations of his free speech rights. The trial court entered judgment for the association on its claims and the counterclaim; both parties appealed. The New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, reversed in part, finding the association's sign restrictions unconstitutional. The association appealed this ruling.


OVERVIEW: Defendant lived in a planned townhouse community that the association managed; its regulations banned all residential signs except "For Sale" signs. Defendant ran for town council and posted two signs in support of his candidacy at his home--one inside a window and another inside a door. The association notified him that the signs violated its rules, ordered their removal, and assessed a fine. He complied and removed the signs. The association filed the instant suit to collect maintenance fees and fines assessed for an unrelated matter. The high court noted that the free speech and assembly clauses in the New Jersey Constitution could be invoked against private entities because of the public use of their property. Balancing the minimal interference with the association's private property interest against defendant's free speech right to post political signs on his own property, the sign policy in question violated the free speech clause of the New Jersey Constitution, N.J. Const. art. I, para. 6. As the association's policy banning all political signs was unreasonable and unconstitutional, the covenant memorializing it was unenforceable.


OUTCOME: The high court affirmed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court.

US Bank, N.A. v. Hough


A-82/83 September Term 2010 067029


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


210 N.J. 187; 42 A.3d 870; 2012 N.J. LEXIS 587


November 7, 2011, Argued 
May 22, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 416 N.J. Super. 286, 3 A.3d 1251 (2010).
US Bank, N.A. v. Hough, 416 N.J. Super. 286, 3 A.3d 1251, 2010 N.J. Super. LEXIS 189 (App.Div., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff assignee filed a foreclosure action against defendant mortgagor and obtained a default judgment. The trial court denied defendant's motions to void the judgment and to dismiss the complaint. She appealed; the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, reversed the trial court's order denying defendant's motion to dismiss. Both parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: A lender issued a loan to defendant, secured by a mortgage on her affordable housing unit, that was in excess of 95 percent of the allowable resale price of the unit, in violation of N.J.A.C. 5:80-26.8(b). Defendant defaulted on the loan; plaintiff foreclosed. The trial court declined to void either the mortgage or the loan, finding that this would give defendant a windfall. The intermediate appellate court held that N.J.A.C. 5:80-26.18(e) only required the voiding of the mortgage securing the loan and that plaintiff could file a separate action to collect on the unsecured indebtedness. Defendant argued that both the mortgage and loan were void as against public policy pursuant to § 5:80-26.18(e). The appellate court disagreed with both contentions. According to the plain language of § 5:80- 26.18(e), the portion of the loan exceeding the permissible limits of § 5:80-26.8(b) was void and not collectible by the lender; the remainder of the loan was valid and secured by the affordable housing unit.


OUTCOME: The high court reversed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
In re Contest of November 8, 2011 Gen. Election, 427 N.J. Super. 410, 48 A.3d 1164, 2012 N.J. Super. LEXIS 105 (Law Div., Jan. 5, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court granted the motion for emergent relief filed by appellant challenger, who commenced an election challenge against respondent incumbent with respect to respondent's election to a seat in the New Jersey General Assembly. The Court further held that, in construing the vacancy filling provisions, it recognized that respondent would meet the eligibility requirements for appointment as an interim successor, if she were selected by her party.

Borough of Sayreville v. 35 Club L.L.C.


A-66 September Term 2010, 067092


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


208 N.J. 491; 33 A.3d 1200; 2012 N.J. LEXIS 12


September 12, 2011, Argued 
January 19, 2012, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is at 416 N.J. Super. 315, 3 A.3d 1268 (2010).
Borough of Sayreville v. 35 Club, L.L.C., 416 N.J. Super. 315, 3 A.3d 1268, 2010 N.J. Super. LEXIS 192 (App.Div., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff borough sued defendant strip-club owner to enjoin its operations on grounds its business was within 1,000 feet of a public park or residential zone in violation of N.J.S.A. § 2C:34-7(a). The trial court rejected defendant's as-applied challenge to the constitutionality of § 2C:34-7(a) and granted the injunction. Defendant appealed; the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, reversed and remanded the matter. The borough appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's as-applied challenge to § 2C:34-7(a), which limited the sites in New Jersey where sexually oriented businesses could operate, was based on its claim that there were not adequate alternative sites for its business. The intermediate appellate court held that the trial court should not have granted the injunction because 1) the borough failed to meet its burden to identify defendant's relevant market area; 2) the borough failed to identify available alternate sites within the relevant market area; and 3) the trial court erred in including Staten Island, New York, in the relevant market area, pursuant to the testimony of the borough's expert, a licensed professional planner. The high court disagreed with the intermediate appellate court's third holding. In evaluating the adequacy of alternative channels of communication when deciding an as-applied constitutional challenge to the state's statute limiting the places where sexually oriented businessescould operate, trial courts were not precluded from considering the existence of sites that were located outside of New Jersey but that were found within the relevant market area as defined by the parties' experts.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was reversed only to the extent that it precluded consideration of alternative sites in New York; in all other respects its judgment was affirmed.

Fair Share Hous. Ctr., Inc. v. New Jersey State League of Municipalities


A-36 September Term 2010, 066228


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


207 N.J. 489; 25 A.3d 1063; 2011 N.J. LEXIS 925


May 4, 2011, Argued 
August 23, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 413 N.J. Super. 423, 995 A.2d 865 (2010).
Fair Share Housing Center, Inc. v. New Jersey State League of Municipalities, 413 N.J. Super. 423, 995 A.2d 865, 2010 N.J. Super. LEXIS 88 (App.Div., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court granted certification to plaintiff requestor, allowing it to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which upheld a trial court decision dismissing its complaint against defendant league. The requestor filed a complaint in lieu of prerogative writs against the league, alleging a violation of the Open Public Records Act (OPRA), N.J.S.A. §§ 47:1A-1 to 47:1A-13.


OVERVIEW: The league was a nonprofit association representing all of New Jersey's 566 municipalities. It was created in 1915 by an act of the New Jersey Legislature. The requestor had sought particular documents from the league relating to certain fair housing regulations. The league declined to provide the requested documents on the ground that it was not covered by OPRA. Subsequently, the trial court determined that the league was not a public agency under OPRA, and the Appellate Division agreed. The Court held that the lower courts erred by denying the requestor relief because the league was a public agency under OPRA and was required to provide access to government records that were not subject to an exemption under OPRA. The Court determined that both lower courts went astray by importing into OPRA's definition of public agency the definition of a public body found in the Open Public Meetings Act, N.J.S.A. § 10:4-6 et seq., and by misconstruing those two very different terms.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

Whirlpool Properties, Inc. v. Director, Div. of Taxation


A-25 September Term 2010, 066595


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


208 N.J. 141; 26 A.3d 446; 2011 N.J. LEXIS 855


May 4, 2011, Argued 
July 28, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Whirlpool Properties, Inc. v. Director, Div. of Taxation, 25 N.J. Tax 519, 2010 N.J. Tax LEXIS 13 (App.Div., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation was granted leave to appeal the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which upheld an assessment against it by respondent, the Director of the New Jersey Division of Taxation, under the Throw-Out Rule, N.J.S.A. § 54:10A-6(B), as well as upheld the constitutionality of the Throw-Out Rule. The corporation had challenged the constitutionality of the Rule via a summary judgment motion.


OVERVIEW: The corporation was a Michigan based business and conducted all activities outside of New Jersey. The corporation earned income by licensing brand names that it owned and managed. The corporation did not file New Jersey tax returns or pay New Jersey's Corporate Business Tax (CBT) from 1996 to 2003. The Director calculated allocable income using information gleaned from related entities and issued the corporation a CBT deficiency assessment of nearly $ 25 million for those years using the Throw-Out Rule. The Appellate Division held that the Throw-Out Rule was constitutional and did not discriminate against interstate commerce. The Court agreed and held that for corporate taxpayers having a substantial nexus to New Jersey, the Throw-Out Rule may apply constitutionally only to untaxed receipts from states that lack jurisdiction to tax the corporation due to insufficient connection with the corporation or due to congressional action, but not to receipts that are untaxed because a state chooses not to impose an income tax.


OUTCOME: As modified, the Court affirmed the judgment of the Appellate Division.

State v. Hess


A-113 September Term 2009


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


207 N.J. 123; 23 A.3d 373; 2011 N.J. LEXIS 746


January 5, 2011, Argued 
July 21, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Hess, 2010 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 765 (App.Div., Apr. 8, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was granted certification to appeal the denial of her petition for post conviction relief seeking a new sentencing hearing. Defendant was convicted of aggravated manslaughter for the killing of her husband, a police office, following the entry of a negotiated plea agreement.


OVERVIEW: Under the terms of the plea agreement, defendant was required to acknowledge that she would receive a 30-year prison sentence, concede that the aggravating factors outweighed the mitigating factors, and agree that neither she nor her attorney would seek a lesser term of imprisonment. At defendant's sentencing, despite evidence in his possession suggesting that defendant suffered from Battered Women's Syndrome when she killed her husband, defense counsel offered no mitigating evidence in support of a lesser sentence. The court held that defendant was denied her constitutional right to the effective assistance of counsel at sentencing. The court found that defense counsel deprived the court of mitigating evidence that was necessary for a meaningful sentencing hearing. Moreover, the court found that the constraints embedded in the terms of the plea agreement denied the trial court of arguments that may have shed light on relevant sentencing factors and how they should be weighed. Further, defense counsel was constitutionally ineffective for failing to challenge the unduly prejudicial video tribute to the victim scored to popular and religious music.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the Appellate Division, which affirmed the denial of defendant's petition for post-conviction relief. The court ordered the restrictions in the plea agreement void and that the plea agreement could be vacated at the option of the State. The court further ordered a new sentencing hearing for defendant.

Abbott v. Burke


M-1293 September Term 2009


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


206 N.J. 332; 20 A.3d 1018; 2011 N.J. LEXIS 616


April 20, 2011, Reargued 
May 24, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On motion in aid of litigants' rights concerning the judgment of this Court reported at 199 N.J. 140, 971 A.2d 989 (2009). Argued January 5, 2011. Remanded January 13, 2011. Special Master's Report Filed March 22, 2011.
Abbott ex rel. Abbott v. Burke, 199 N.J. 140, 971 A.2d 989, 2009 N.J. LEXIS 420 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff schoolchildren filed a motion under R. 1:10-3, seeking aid of litigants' rights based on defendant, the State of New Jersey, failing to fund the School Funding Reform Act of 2008 (SFRA), N.J.S.A. §§ 18A:7F-43 to 18A:7F-63. The suit was a continuation of the litigation referred to as Abbott district cases wherein plaintiffs challenged the funding of schoolchildren in poorer school districts.


OVERVIEW: The State had applied to the Court to be relieved of the ordered that required parity funding and supplemental funding for children in the so-called Abbott districts in exchange for providing funding to those districts under the SFRA. The Court granted the relief the State sought in Abbott XX. However, plaintiffs brought the underlying motion as a result of the State's failure to provide full funding under SFRA's formula to the Abbott districts. As such, the Court held that the State breached the very premise underlying the grant of relief it secured with Abbott XX. The Court held that the Appropriations Clause, N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 2, para. 2, created no bar to judicial enforcement under the circumstances presented. The Court directed that the funding to the Abbott districts in Fiscal Year 2012 must be calculated and provided in accordance with the SFRA. The Court did limit the relief to the plaintiff class of children from Abbott districts for whom the Court had a historical finding of constitutional violation and for whom the Court has specific remedial orders in place through Abbott XX.


OUTCOME: The Court granted the motion and ordered that the funding to the Abbott districts in Fiscal Year 2012 must be calculated and provided in accordance with the SFRA formula. The Court stated that, based on Office of Legislative Services figures, the best estimated cost of that remedy was $ 500 million.

 

58 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Brown


A-67 September Term 2009, A-17 September Term 2010


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


205 N.J. 133; 14 A.3d 26; 2011 N.J. LEXIS 81


October 12, 2010, Argued 
January 25, 2011, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Brown, 2015 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1097 (App.Div., May 13, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Brown, 2009 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2181 (App.Div., Aug. 7, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court granted defendant's petition for certification, challenging the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed his conviction on multiple counts of armed robbery and upheld the admission of his custodial statements. The State cross-petitioned for certification, challenging the Appellate Division's conclusion that defendant's arrest was unlawful.


OVERVIEW: The police relied on invalid arrest warrants when they set out to arrest defendant at his girlfriend's apartment. Upon hearing the officers knock, defendant fled through a window. After a 20 minute standoff, the police arrested him. Later at headquarters, he made incriminating statements after he was advised of his Miranda rights. Defendant challenged the admission of those statements. The trial court found that the police had sufficient probable cause to arrest him and that his subsequent statements were given voluntarily. The Appellate Division affirmed that issue on different grounds, concluding that although defendant's arrest was unlawful, his statements were attenuated from the arrest. The Court affirmed for different reasons, finding that when defendant fled to a public area when the police arrived at the apartment, the police had sufficient probable cause to believe that he had committed a felony. Therefore, they did not need a warrant to arrest him in public. Further, the police had probable cause to arrest him for his conduct in resisting arrest, which they observed. As such, the defective arrest warrants played no role and the post-arrest statements were admissible.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment by concluding that defendant's arrest was lawful and, as modified, affirmed the judgment of the Appellate Division. The court remanded the case to the trial court for resentencing consistent with the Appellate Division's opinion.

Hopewell Valley Citizens' Group, Inc. v. Berwind Prop. Group Development Co., L.P.


A-83 September Term 2009


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


204 N.J. 569; 10 A.3d 211; 2011 N.J. LEXIS 7


October 26, 2010, Argued 
January 12, 2011, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Hopewell Valley Citizens Group v. Berwind Prop. Group Dev. Co., L.P., 2010 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 37 (App.Div., Jan. 8, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff objectors challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed a trial court order dismissing the complaint in lieu of prerogative writs they filed against defendants, a developer, a township planning board, the township, and the township's committee. The complaint challenged the grant of preliminary site plan approval for a development.


OVERVIEW: The developer had published a notice of the resolution granting the preliminary site plan in a newspaper of general circulation, and notified the board of its action. The board published a second notice in another local newspaper. Plaintiffs contacted the board and was informed that the notice had been published on October 2, 2008. In reliance on that information, plaintiffs calculated the 45 day period within which to bring an action in lieu of prerogative writs and filed the complaint on November 17, 2008, against defendants. The lower courts disagreed with plaintiffs that the objector who contacted the board was misled by the information conveyed by the board and refused to enlarge the limitations period. The court found error, concluding that it was entirely reasonable for plaintiffs to have contacted the board and use the date that was provided to them to calculate the proper time to file the action.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the Appellate Division and remanded the matter to the trial court for the reinstatement of plaintiffs' complaint.

 

60 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Johnson v. Johnson


A-91 September Term 2009


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


204 N.J. 529; 9 A.3d 1003; 2010 N.J. LEXIS 1259


September 28, 2010, Argued 
December 10, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 411 N.J. Super. 161, 984 A.2d 912 (2009).
Johnson v. Johnson, 411 N.J. Super. 161, 984 A.2d 912, 2009 N.J. Super. LEXIS 257 (App.Div., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff father filed a motion to confirm an arbitration award concerning custody and parenting time; defendant mother filed a motion opposing confirmation and requesting modification of custody and visitation. The trial court confirmed the award; the mother appealed. The New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, reversed the confirmation order and remanded the matter for a plenary hearing. The father appealed.


OVERVIEW: The parties had agreed to arbitrate their custody dispute under the New Jersey Alternative Procedure for Dispute Resolution Act (APDRA), N.J.S.A. §§ 2A:23A-1 to 2A:23A-19. After the trial judge confirmed the award, the high court decided Fawzy v. Fawzy, which held that an arbitral award was subject to judicial review if a party established that it threatened harm to a child. Under Fawzy, testimony before the arbitrator had to be recorded verbatim and the award had to including findings of fact and conclusions of law. The intermediate appellate court reversed the confirmation order because of the absence of a verbatim transcript of the arbitration proceedings. The high court held that the principles established in Fawzy were intended to be applicable to all child custody arbitrations, including those conducted under the APDRA. The record created by the arbitrator, which included a recitation of all evidence considered, a recapitulation of every interview and observation he conducted, a full explanation of the underpinnings of the award, and a separate opinion on reconsideration, satisfied the spirit of Fawzy and was an acceptable substitute for a verbatim transcript.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was reversed and the order of the trial judge confirming the arbitration award was reinstated.

 

61 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Henry v. New Jersey Dept. of Human Services


A-69 September Term 2009


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


204 N.J. 320; 9 A.3d 882; 2010 N.J. LEXIS 1260; 110 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 1803


September 28, 2010, Argued 
December 10, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Henry v. New Jersey Dep't of Human Servs., 2009 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 3192 (App.Div., July 21, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former employee sued defendants, her former state employer and its officials, alleging racial discrimination and retaliation in violation of the New Jersey Law Against Discrimination, N.J.S.A. §§ 10:5-1 to 10:5-1-49. The trial court granted defendants summary judgment and dismissed the complaint as time-barred by N.J.S.A. § 2A:14-2. The New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, affirmed. Plaintiff sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff asserted that she was given only an entry level nursing position, despite having a masters degree in nursing, and was not reclassified thereafter due to her race. She claimed she did not learn she was the victim of racial discrimination until two years after she resigned. The trial court held that her cause of action accrued when she resigned because she had a reasonable suspicion of racial discrimination at that time. The high court held that there was no equitable basis on which to extend the two-year statute of limitations on plaintiff's retaliation claim. That cause of action accrued at or before the date she resigned, after being told that if she had not complained, she may have been reclassified. However, a Lopez hearing was required to determine whether the discovery rule applied to the discrimination claim. When plaintiff requested reclassification, she was given a reason that had nothing to do with discrimination, which may have misled her into not pursuing the issue. She was entitled to assert that she had no reasonable suspicion of discrimination until two years after she resigned, when she learned about other claims of racial discrimination by employer.


OUTCOME: The high court affirmed the dismissal of plaintiff's retaliation claim, but remanded the matter for a Lopez hearing with respect to the date of accrual of the racial discrimination claim.

 

62 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Committee to Recall Robert Menendez From the Office of U.S. Senator v. Wells


A-86 September Term 2009


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


204 N.J. 79; 7 A.3d 720; 2010 N.J. LEXIS 1223


May 25, 2010, Argued 
November 18, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Committee to Recall Robert Menendez from the Office of U.S. Senator v. Wells, 413 N.J. Super. 435, 995 A.2d 1109, 2010 N.J. Super. LEXIS 40 (App.Div., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner senator appealed the order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which ordered respondent, the New Jersey Secretary of State, to accept a recall petition of the senator. Respondents, a recall committee and the New Jersey Director of the Division of Elections were also parties. At issue was the Uniform Recall Election Law (UREL), N.J.S.A. §§ 19:27A-1 to 19:27A-18, which implemented N.J. Const. art. I, para. 2.


OVERVIEW: New Jersey voters had approved the constitutional amendment that provided for the recall of any elected official in the State of New Jersey representing New Jersey in the United States Congress. The recall committee filed its recall petition with the Secretary, who rejected it for filing or review. The recall committee filed an application with the Appellate Division, seeking temporary injunctive relief and expedited review. The Appellate Division questioned the constitutionality of the UREL but did not pass on the ultimate validity of the recall process and ordered the Secretary to accept and file the recall petition and to proceed under the UREL. The Court determined that the case was ripe for adjudication and, upon analysis of the text and history of the United States Constitution, as well as the principles of the democratic system it created, held that the United States Constitution did not allow the states the power to recall United States Senators. The Court noted that it was only invalidating as unconstitutional the provisions of the UREL and N.J. Const. art. I, para. 2 that authorized the recall of United States Senators only.


OUTCOME: The Court reversed and vacated the Appellate Division's order directing the Secretary to accept the notice of intention to recall the senator. The Court invalidated as unconstitutional those provisions of the UREL and New Jersey's Constitution that authorized the recall of a United States Senator and struck those portions only.
