In re Civil Commitment of W.X.C.


A-33 September Term 2009


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


204 N.J. 179; 8 A.3d 174; 2010 N.J. LEXIS 1213


April 26, 2010, Argued 
November 9, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by W.X.C. v. New Jersey, 131 S. Ct. 1702, 179 L. Ed. 2d 635, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 2383 (U.S., 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 407 N.J. Super. 619, 972 A.2d 462 (2009).
In re Civil Commitment of W.X.C., 407 N.J. Super. 619, 972 A.2d 462, 2009 N.J. Super. LEXIS 140 (App.Div., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted defendant's petition for certification, allowing him to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which upheld a trial court's rejection of his challenges to the New Jersey Sexually Violent Predator Act (SVPA), N.J.S.A. §§ 30:4-27.24 to 30:4-27.38. Defendant was convicted for numerous sexual assault related offenses. Specifically, defendant challenged the constitutionality of the SVPA.


OVERVIEW: The court noted that defendant's challenge to the SVPA was both a narrow and specific in that he asserted that the SVPA was unconstitutional as applied to individuals, like himself, who were committed pursuant to its terms when they were not provided with sex offender treatment while incarcerated for their underlying criminal sexual offenses. Defendant asserted that the SVPA was punitive, therefore, violating the federal and state ex post facto clauses under U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, cl. 1 and N.J. Const. art. IV, § 7, para. 3, and that it was fundamentally unfair. The court analyzed the statute and various relevant case law and held that the SVPA was neither punitive nor unfair. The court held that the SVPA was remedial and regulatory in nature, and that its incidental effects, including the use of confinement as part of the treatment methodology, did not alter the essential character of the statute. The Court declined to conclude that the SVPA was transformed into a punitive, and therefore unconstitutional, enactment merely because it applied to some individuals, like defendant, who were not provided with specialized treatment prior to civil commitment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the Appellate Division.

 

64 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Davila


A-20 September Term 2009


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


203 N.J. 97; 999 A.2d 1116; 2010 N.J. LEXIS 590


February 2, 2010, Argued 
July 14, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at State v. Davila, 2012 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 920 (App.Div., Apr. 25, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Davila, 2009 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1537 (App.Div., Apr. 16, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to the Fourth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. IV, defendant moved to suppress evidence obtained as a result of a warrantless search of a dwelling. After the trial court denied his motion, he pled guilty to conspiracy to commit robbery and two counts of felony murder. He then appealed; the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, affirmed. Defendant's petition for certification was granted.


OVERVIEW: Investigating a car theft and murders, police found the car. The owner's cell phone was missing; police learned it had been used to call an apartment after the murders. An officer testified that an occupant of the apartment consented to their entry. Police swept the dwelling; seeing drugs in plain view, they arrested defendant and others. The high court held a protective sweep conducted on private property was not per se invalid merely because it did not occur incident to an arrest. Law enforcement officers could conduct a protective sweep only when 1) they were lawfully within private premises for a legitimate purpose, which could include consent to enter; and 2) they had a reasonable articulable suspicion that the area to be swept harbored an individual posing a danger. The sweep would be upheld only if it was conducted quickly and restricted to areas where the person posing a danger could hide. When an arrest was not the basis for entry, police had to point to dangerous circumstances that developed once they were at the scene. The evidence established that the entry was consensual and lawful, but questions remained as to the legitimacy of the investigative technique employed.


OUTCOME: The high court reversed and remanded the matter to the trial court for supplemental proceedings to determine whether the police entry could be shown to meet the directives contained in the high court's opinion.

 

65 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Moran


A-55 September Term 2009


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


202 N.J. 311; 997 A.2d 210; 2010 N.J. LEXIS 588


April 27, 2010, Argued 
July 13, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 408 N.J. Super 412, 975 A.2d 480 (2009).
State v. Moran, 408 N.J. Super. 412, 975 A.2d 480, 2009 N.J. Super. LEXIS 171 (App.Div., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in municipal court of reckless driving; her driving privileges were suspended for 45 days under N.J.S.A. § 39:5-31. The superior court, in a trial de novo, upheld defendant's conviction and imposed the same sentence. The the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, affirmed and set forth standards for the imposition of license suspensions under § 39:5-31. Defendant's petition for certification was granted.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed she did not receive "fair notice" that she was facing a potential license suspension and that § 39:5-31 was unconstitutional. The high court held that the license suspension provision of § 39:5-31, which was published in the Motor Vehicle Code of the New Jersey Statutes Annotated, was not "hidden," and defendant, like all motorists, was presumed to know the law. To ensure that license suspensions meted out pursuant to § 39:5-31 were imposed in a reasonably fair and uniform manner, so that similarly situated defendants were treated similarly, the high court defined the term "willful violation" contained in § 39:5-31 to mean a deliberate violation of certain motor-vehicle statutes, and enunciated sentencing standards to guide municipal court and law division judges in determining whether to impose a license suspension under N.J.S.A. § 39:5-31, and, if so, the length of the suspension. Having defined "willful violation" and having set standards to guide the discretion of judges imposing license suspensions, the high court concluded that § 39:5-31 was neither vague nor overbroad, nor did it give unbridled discretion to sentencing judges.


OUTCOME: The high court reversed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court upholding the superior court's 45-day suspension of defendant's driving privileges. It remanded the case to the municipal court to consider anew whether to impose a suspension and, if so, the length of the suspension, based on the guidelines established in the high court's opinion.

 

66 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Besler v. Board of Educ. of West Windsor-Plainsboro Regional School Dist.


A-81 September Term 2008


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


201 N.J. 544; 993 A.2d 805; 2010 N.J. LEXIS 408


September 29, 2009, Argued 
May 17, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Besler v. Bd. of Educ., 2008 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2693 (App.Div., Aug. 25, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, a high school student and her father and mother, filed a 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 action against appellants, a board of education and its agents, alleging, inter alia, that the board violated the father's rights to free speech under the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I. A jury awarded the father damages. Appellants sought review; the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, affirmed. Appellants sought further review.


OVERVIEW: During the public comment period of a board meeting, the board president denied the father the opportunity to complete a statement critical of both board policy and a high school coach he believed had verbally abused students, including his daughter. A jury found that the board did not have a compelling reason to justify silencing the father and awarded him damages. The high court held that, for purposes of § 1983, the board president was acting as a final policymaker while presiding over the public comment period of the board meeting and therefore the board could be held liable for a violation of the father's First Amendment rights. In addition, the father presented sufficient evidence for the jury to determine that the board silenced him for no reason other than the unpopular viewpoint he expressed, in violation of his free speech rights. However, he offered minimal evidence of emotional distress and the damages award of $ 100,000 was so clearly excessive that it constituted a miscarriage of justice. Such a substantial award of damages for emotional distress had to be based on more than de minimis mental anguish, fleeting embarrassment, or mere shock and bewilderment.


OUTCOME: The high court affirmed that part of the intermediate appellate court's judgment that upheld the jury's finding that the board violated the father's First Amendment rights. It reversed the trial court's denial of a remittitur and remanded the case to that court for a remittitur hearing.

State v. J.G.


A-44 September Term 2008


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


201 N.J. 369; 990 A.2d 1122; 2010 N.J. LEXIS 381


September 30, 2009, Argued 
April 7, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by State v. J.G., 2014 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 306 (App.Div., Feb. 14, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 402 N.J. Super. 290, 953 A.2d 1214 (2008).
State v. J.G., 402 N.J. Super. 290, 953 A.2d 1214, 2008 N.J. Super. LEXIS 180 (App.Div., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, charged with sexually assaulting minor children, moved to exclude statements he made to a pastor. The trial court granted the motion on grounds the statements were protected by the cleric-penitent privilege under N.J.S.A. § 2A:84A-23. The State appealed; the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, reversed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's daughters told their mother that defendant had molested them. The mother called the pastor, who had known defendant for 30 years. The two men spoke in private about the allegations; they did not discuss whether the conversation was to be kept confidential, and each left the session with a very different understanding. The pastor later revealed details of the conversation to defendant's wife and police. In ruling that the privilege applied, the trial court noted that the pastor reached out to defendant, that defendant had known him as a pastor for many years, that defendant desired to be baptized, and that the two had spoken in private. In reversing, the intermediate appellate court noted that the pastor had refused to counsel or baptize defendant. The high court held that the cleric-penitent privilege applied when, under the totality of the circumstances, an objectively reasonable penitent would have believed that a communication was secret, that is, made in confidence to a cleric in the cleric's professional character or role as a spiritual advisor. The record established that defendant's conversation with the pastor was privileged under this newly adopted test.


OUTCOME: The high court reversed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

68 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


1717 Realty Associates, LLC v. Borough of Fair Lawn


A-26 September Term 2009


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


201 N.J. 275; 990 A.2d 636; 2010 N.J. LEXIS 232


February 1, 2010, Argued 
March 17, 2010, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
1717 Realty Assocs., LLC v. Borough of Fair Lawn, 2009 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1136 (App.Div., May 12, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer sued defendant borough, challenging a tax assessment. The tax court affirmed the tax assessor's valuation and assessment of the taxpayer's property. The taxpayer appealed, challenging the constitutionality of N.J.S.A. § 54:4-34. The New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, found § 54:4-34 constitutional and affirmed the assessment. The taxpayer sought further review.


OVERVIEW: As the taxpayer failed to respond to the tax assessor's request for information, he valued its property without its input. The taxpayer argued that the application of § 54:4-34, mandating dismissal of a tax appeal if the taxpayer failed to respond to a request for information, violated its rights under U.S. Const. amend. VIII and N.J. Const. art. I, para. 12, because the difference between the tax bill issued and one informed by the taxpayer's tardy income information constituted an "excessive fine." The intermediate appellate court held that § 54:4-34 as applied did not result in the imposition of an unconstitutional excessive fine as 1) the tax bill was not a punishment, forfeiture, or civil sanction; 2) the tax court found the process of assessing the tax to be reasonable; and 3) the taxpayer was not assessed a penalty or additional tax for failing to comply with the request for information. The high court approved the judgment of the intermediate appellate court substantially for the reasons set forth in its thorough and thoughtful opinion. The statutory-appeal dismissal sanction did not violate the Excessive Fines Clause of the Eighth Amendment or the New Jersey Constitution.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.

Nicastro v. McIntyre Machinery America, Ltd.


A-29 September Term 2008


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


201 N.J. 48; 987 A.2d 575; 2010 N.J. LEXIS 19


January 21, 2009, Argued 
February 2, 2010, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari granted by J. McIntyre Mach., Ltd. v. Nicastro, 131 S. Ct. 62, 177 L. Ed. 2d 1151, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 5747 (U.S., 2010)
Reversed by J. McIntyre Mach. v. Nicastro, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 4800 (U.S., June 27, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 399 N.J. Super. 539, 945 A.2d 92 (2008).
Nicastro v. McIntyre Machinery America, Ltd., 399 N.J. Super. 539, 945 A.2d 92, 2008 N.J. Super. LEXIS 81 (App.Div., 2008)



DISPOSITION:  The court affirmed the judgment, which reinstated the worker's product-liability action, and remanded the matter to the trial court for further proceedings.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted the petition for certification filed by defendant manufacturer, who appealed the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed a trial court judgment dismissing the complaint. Plaintiff worker had brought a product-liability action, pursuant to N.J.S.A. § 2A:58C-2, against the manufacturer and defendant, its American distributor.


OVERVIEW: The worker was employed by a scrap metal company and severed four of his fingers while operating a recycling machine used to cut metal that was made by the manufacturer. The manufacturer was a foreign corporation, incorporated in the United Kingdom, who sold the machine through its American distributor. The trial court had found that the manufacturer did not have sufficient minimum contacts with New Jersey to justify the State's exercise of personal jurisdiction over it. The Appellate Division reversed following discovery, concluding that the exercise of jurisdiction over the manufacturer by New Jersey would not offend traditional notions of fair play and substantial justice and was justified under the stream-of-commerce plus rationale. The court agreed with the Appellate Division and reaffirmed a prior case law holding that a foreign manufacturer that places a defective product in the stream of commerce through a distribution scheme that targets a national market, which includes New Jersey, may be subject to the in personam jurisdiction of a New Jersey court in a product-liability action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which reinstated the worker's product-liability action, and remanded the matter to the trial court for further proceedings.

In re Atty General's "Directive on Exit Polling: Media & Non-Partisan Pub. Interest Groups"


A-47 September Term 2008


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


200 N.J. 283; 981 A.2d 64; 2009 N.J. LEXIS 991


February 17, 2009, Argued 
September 30, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 402 N.J. Super. 118, 952 A.2d 1127 (2008).
In re Attorney General's "Directive on Exit Polling: Media and Non-Partisan Public Interest Groups", 402 N.J. Super. 118, 952 A.2d 1127, 2008 N.J. Super. LEXIS 174 (App.Div., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted petitioner organization's petition for certification, allowing it to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which upheld a 2007 Directive from respondent, the New Jersey Attorney General, allowing exit polling within 100 feet of a polling place and that made other interpretations of New Jersey's elections laws, namely N.J.S.A. §§ 19:34-6, -7, and -15.


OVERVIEW: The organization challenged both the Attorney General directive, which required those conducting exit polling to register with election officials two weeks before an election, and the Attorney General's denial of the organization's request to distribute so-called voting-rights cards within 100 feet of a polling place. The Court disagreed with the Attorney General's analysis that the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, required an exception to be carved out of New Jersey's election-law statutes for exit polling, and only exit polling, in the 100-foot exclusionary zone. The Court held that N.J.S.A. §§ 19:34-6, -7, and -15 direct that voters will have a 100-foot free, unobstructed passage to polling places, without interference from any person, and that the ban applied to all expressive activities within the 100-foot zone, including exit polling and handing out voting-rights cards. The Court held that the laws were constitutional because they were reasonable time, place, and manner restrictions under the First Amendment intended to secure and enhance another vital constitutional right, namely, the right to vote.


OUTCOME: The Court modified and affirmed the judgment of the Appellate Division and held that New Jersey's election laws provided a comprehensive, non-discriminatory scheme for regulating the area within 100 feet of a polling place and banned all expressive activity, including exit polling and the handing out of voting-rights cards.

 

71 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Baum


A-44 September Term 2007


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


199 N.J. 407; 972 A.2d 1127; 2009 N.J. LEXIS 421


March 11, 2008, Argued, September 8, 2008, Re-argued 
June 15, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 393 N.J. Super. 275, 923 A.2d 276 (2007). Argued March 11, 2008.
State v. Baum, 393 N.J. Super. 275, 923 A.2d 276, 2007 N.J. Super. LEXIS 161 (App.Div., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant and co-defendant were charged with drug offenses. The trial court granted their motion to suppress evidence seized from co-defendant's vehicle. The State appealed; the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, reversed. Defendant was granted leave to appeal.


OVERVIEW: An officer stopped a vehicle that had no inspection sticker. After co-defendant (the driver) and defendant gave inconsistent accounts of their itinerary, the officer told co-defendant that he suspected that there was contraband in the car and threatened to summon a drug-sniffing dog. Co-defendant admitted that there was marijuana in the car, but told him it belonged to defendant. The officer arrested her for having a suspended license and Mirandized her. She waived her rights and revealed where the drugs were. The high court held that defendant's motion to suppress evidence found during a warrantless search of the vehicle in which he was riding should have been denied because he did not have standing to argue that co-defendant's right against self-incrimination under U.S. Const. amend. V was violated and because the search was not unreasonable under U.S. Const. amend. IV or N.J. Const. art. I, para. 7. The stop was justified by the absence of an inspection sticker. Defendant and co-defendant's inconsistent stories supported the officer's continued inquiries. The encounter, lasting 26 minutes, was not unreasonably extended or more intrusive than necessary.


OUTCOME: The high court affirmed, as modified, the judgment of the intermediate appellate court that reversed the grant of the motion to suppress, and remanded the matter for further proceedings.

Abbott ex rel. Abbott v. Burke


M-969/1372 September Term 2007


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


199 N.J. 140; 971 A.2d 989; 2009 N.J. LEXIS 420


April 28, 2009, Re-argued 
May 28, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Abbott v. Burke, 2011 N.J. LEXIS 616 (N.J., May 24, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Argued September 22, 2008. Remanded November 18, 2008. Master's Report filed - March 26, 2009. On motion for review of the Constitutionality of the School Funding Reform Act of 2008 and Cross-Motion for an Interim Order Preserving the Status Quo and Clarifying Procedural Protections.
Abbott v. Burke, 196 N.J. 544, 960 A.2d 360, 2008 N.J. LEXIS 1693 (2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant State filed a motion seeking a declaration that the New Jersey School Funding Reform Act of 2008 (SFRA) satisfied the New Jersey Constitution and that, therefore, the State was released from prior remedial orders concerning public school funding in Abbott districts. Plaintiffs filed a cross-motion to preserve the status quo. New Jersey's highest court appointed a special master, who recommended that SFRA be found to be constitutional.


OVERVIEW: In an effort to comply with the Thorough and Efficient Education Clause, N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 4, para. 1, the legislature enacted SFRA, N.J.S.A. §§ 18A:7F-43 to -63, which redesigned the school funding formula. The State asked for elimination of the requirements that Abbott districts be provided parity aid and supplemental funding. Plaintiffs objected and sought to maintain the status quo. At an evidentiary hearing, the State's witnesses explained how the formula was created and how it would work. Plaintiffs' witnesses raised concerns about the adequacy of SFRA funding. The special master opined that SFRA was constitutional but recommended that supplemental funding continue to Abbott districts until the three-year look-back review of SFRA. To the extent that the record permitted its review, the high court held that SFRA was constitutional and could be applied in Abbott districts subject to the State continuing to provide school funding aid during the current and the next two years at the levels required by SFRA's formula each year, and subject further to the mandated review and retooling of the formula's weights and other operative parts after three years of implementation.


OUTCOME: The State's motion, seeking declarations that SFRA constitutional and seeking an order relieving the State from prior remedial orders concerning funding to the Abbott districts, was granted. Plaintiffs' cross-motion seeking an order preserving and continuing the status quo concerning enforcement of the prior remedial orders was denied.

 

73 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


New Jersey Shore Builders Ass'n v. Township of Jackson


A-83 September Term 2007


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


199 N.J. 38; 970 A.2d 992; 2009 N.J. LEXIS 264


September 9, 2008, Argued 
May 13, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
New Jersey Shore Builders Ass'n v. Township of Jackson, 193 N.J. 586, 940 A.2d 1218, 2008 N.J. LEXIS 29 (2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff builders association sued defendant township, challenging a tree removal ordinance that required landowners to obtain a permit before removing certain trees on private land; to replant removed trees; and, if that was not feasible, to pay money into an escrow fund to be used to plant trees on public property. The trial court found the ordinance invalid; the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, affirmed. The township appealed.


OVERVIEW: The township adopted the ordinance under the general police power statute, N.J.S.A. § 40:48-2. The intermediate appellate court agreed with the trial court that ordinance was not a valid exercise of police power; it held that the payment of a fee to plant new trees on public land did not further the ordinance's expressed objective of ameliorating the negative effects of removing trees on private property. It also affirmed the trial court's finding that the ordinance was impermissibly vague. The high court held that the ordinance was a valid exercise of police power because the details of the ordinance, including the tree replacement fee, the escrow fund, and the planting of trees and shrubs on public property when replanting at the original location was not feasible, were rationally related to the broad environmental goals that informed the ordinance. As the ordinance was an environmental regulation, not a planning or zoning initiative, it was not subject to the New Jersey Municipal Land Use Law, N.J.S.A. §§ 40:55D-1 to -163. The association did not prove that the fees assessed under the ordinance exceeded the township's administrative costs and thus were impermissible taxes.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was reversed to the extent it held that the ordinance was not a valid exercise of the police power. However, the ordinance remained in limbo because the intermediate appellate court's affirmance of the trial court's ruling that the ordinance was impermissibly vague was not challenged on appeal.

 

74 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Fortin


A-27 September Term 2008


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


198 N.J. 619; 969 A.2d 1133; 2009 N.J. LEXIS 261


January 20, 2009, Argued 
May 12, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by State v. Fortin, 2015 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2392 (App.Div., Oct. 20, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 400 N.J. Super. 434, 948 A.2d 160 (2008).
State v. Fortin, 400 N.J. Super. 434, 948 A.2d 160, 2008 N.J. Super. LEXIS 121 (App.Div., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of capital murder. The State filed a motion to sentence him under amended N.J.S.A. § 2C:11-3 to life without parole. The trial court denied the motion on the grounds that this would violate the federal and state Ex Post Facto Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, §, 10. cl. 1; N.J. Const. art. IV, § 7, para 3. The New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, affirmed. The State sought further review.


OVERVIEW: At the time of the offense, under former N.J.S.A. § 2C:11-3(c), a defendant convicted of capital murder was subject to the death penalty if the jury found that one or more statutory aggravating factors were present and that they outweighed the mitigating factors. However, before the penalty phase of the trial, § 2C:11-3 was amended to provide for a mandatory life-without-parole sentence in place of the death penalty if the State proved a single statutory aggravator. The high court held that because defendant had prior notice of the death penalty procedures in former § 2C:11-3, there was no impediment to proceeding to the penalty phase under the former statute. If the jury concluded that the State proved beyond a reasonable doubt that the aggravating factors outweighed the mitigating factors, rendering defendant subject to a death sentence under the former law, then imposing a life-without-parole sentence under the new law would not violate the Ex Post Facto Clause. If the jury found in favor of a non-death sentence, defendant had to be sentenced under the law as it existed at the time of the offense, to a term of 30 years to life with a 30-year parole disqualifier.


OUTCOME: To the extent the intermediate appellate court's holding entailed that defendant could not be sentenced to life without parole under any circumstances, even if the jury found that the aggravating factors outweighed the mitigating factors, its judgment was reversed. The judgment was otherwise affirmed and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

State v. Byrd


A-105 September Term 2007


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


198 N.J. 319; 967 A.2d 285; 2009 N.J. LEXIS 168


January 5, 2009, Argued 
April 2, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 393 N.J. Super. 218, 923 A.2d 242 (2007).
State v. Byrd, 393 N.J. Super. 218, 923 A.2d 242, 2007 N.J. Super. LEXIS 155 (App.Div., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court granted the State's petition for certification, allowing it to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed defendants' convictions for felony murder, in violation of N.J.S.A. § 2C:11-3(a)(3), and other crimes. Defendants were tried together before a jury during a four-week trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendants' convictions arose from their actions in killing a known drug dealer during a robbery. At the heart of the case was the trial court allowing the out-of-court statement of a witness to be introduced based upon it concluding that he was unavailable due to intimidation by defendants. The witness had been incarcerated and was taken to the courthouse in the same van as one defendant. When brought before the trial court to testify, he refused. Later, without placing the witness under oath, the trial court questioned him in camera, on the record, regarding the threats made to him. The court held that defendants' convictions required reversal based on the trial court improperly introducing the witness's statement. The court found that the trial court improperly examined the witness outside the presence of defendants and their counsel, took testimony without placing the witness under oath, and denied defendants the opportunity to present evidence to rebut the evidence of intimidation. The Court forwarded a recommendation that the New Jersey Legislature adopt a forfeiture-by-wrongdoing exception to the hearsay rule.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the Appellate Division reversing defendants' convictions. The court forwarded a forfeiture-by-wrongdoing exception to the hearsay rule for inclusion in the New Jersey Rules of Evidence to the New Jersey Senate and the New Jersey General Assembly, for their approval by resolution, and to the New Jersey Governor for his signature.

DEG, LLC v. Township of Fairfield

In re Commitment of J.M.B.


A-79 September Term 2007


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


197 N.J. 563; 964 A.2d 752; 2009 N.J. LEXIS 67


September 8, 2008, Argued 
February 23, 2009, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by J.M.B. v. New Jersey, 558 U.S. 999, 130 S. Ct. 509, 175 L. Ed. 2d 361, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 7682 (Nov. 2, 2009)
Related proceeding at State v. J.B., 2017 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1478 (App.Div., June 19, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 395 N.J. Super. 69, 928 A.2d 102 (2007).
In re Civil Commitment of J.M.B., 395 N.J. Super. 69, 928 A.2d 102, 2007 N.J. Super. LEXIS 260 (App.Div., July 20, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted appellant inmate certification to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed a trial court order civilly committing him as a sexually violent person under New Jersey's Sexually Violent Predator Act (SVPA), N.J.S.A. §§ 30:4-27.24 to -27.38. The State petitioned for civil commitment under the N.J.S.A. § 30:4-27.26(b) standard. Defendant had been convicted of eight prior offenses.


OVERVIEW: Appellant's convictions involved a pattern of unusual behavior toward his victims, including bondage. The trial court determined that six of the eight offenses were sexually violent offenses under § 30:4-27.26(b). The State petitioned under § 30:4-27.26(b) since none of appellant's convictions were for offenses listed in the SVPA. The case presented the first time the Court was being asked to approve the continued involuntary commitment of an inmate under § 30:4-27.26(b). The court upheld appellant's commitment and concluded that, when faced with an application under § 30:4-27.26(b), a court may consider the circumstances that led to the qualifying prior conviction and when that conduct was substantially equivalent to the sexually violent conduct encompassed by the offenses in § 30:4-27.26(a), then that prior conviction might provide the predicate for a civil commitment application under § 30:4-27.26(b). The court held that § 30:4-27.26(b) must be read to confer additional authority to a court to determine an offense, not listed in § 30:4-27.26(a) and which does not have substantially the same elements as an enumerated offense, to be, nevertheless, a sexually violent offense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

State v. DeAngelo


A-73 September Term 2007


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


197 N.J. 478; 963 A.2d 1200; 2009 N.J. LEXIS 10; 49 A.L.R.6th 729; 185 L.R.R.M. 3057; 157 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P60,747


September 23, 2008, Argued 
February 5, 2009, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On appeal from and certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 396 N.J. Super. 23, 930 A.2d 1236 (2007).
State v. DeAngelo, 396 N.J. Super. 23, 930 A.2d 1236, 2007 N.J. Super. LEXIS 304 (App.Div., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of violating a township's sign ordinance based on his role in the display of an inflatable rat. He appealed, alleging the ordinance violated his free speech rights under the First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, and N.J. Const. art. I, § 6. The New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, affirmed the conviction. Defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The ordinance banned most signs, including balloon signs or other inflated signs. Defendant, a union official in charge of a labor protest who ordered the display of the inflatable rat (a symbol of labor unrest) was convicted of violating the ordinance. The intermediate appellate court held the ordinance's ban on all inflatable signs other than grand opening signs was not a restraint on free speech, the ordinance was content-neutral, and it did not entirely prevent the union's message. The high court held the ordinance violated defendant's First Amendment right to free speech and was overbroad. As it prohibited the union from displaying a rat balloon, but authorized a similar display as part of a grand opening, it was a content-based restriction of non-commercial speech, and thus was subject to the most exacting scrutiny. Its goals--aesthetics, safety, and protection of property values--did not justify the restriction on speech. It was not shown to be necessary to serve a compelling state interest or to have been narrowly drawn to achieve that end. The ordinance's virtual elimination of an entire medium of expression without a readily available alternative rendered it overbroad.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was reversed and the case was remanded to the trial court for the entry of an order dismissing the summons issued to defendant.

Abbott v. Burke


M-969/M-1372 September Term 2007


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


196 N.J. 544; 960 A.2d 360; 2008 N.J. LEXIS 1693


September 22, 2008, Argued 
November 18, 2008, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by, Motion denied by Abbott v. Burke, 2009 N.J. LEXIS 420 (N.J., May 28, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On motion for review of the Constitutionality of the School Funding Reform Act of 2008 and Cross-Motion for an Interim Order Preserving the Status Quo and Clarifying Procedural Protections.
Abbott v. Burke, 196 N.J. 451, 956 A.2d 923, 2008 N.J. LEXIS 1322 (2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the New Jersey Commissioner of Education, the New Jersey Director of Budget and Accounting, the New Jersey State Treasurer, and the New Jersey State Board of Education, filed a motion seeking a declaration that the New Jersey School Funding Reform Act of 2008 (SFRA), L. 2007, ch. 260, satisfied N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 4. Plaintiffs, minors and their guardians ad litem, filed a cross motion seeking an order to preserve the status quo.


OVERVIEW: The high court had held that public school funding under N.J.S.A. ch. 212, as applied to "special needs" school districts (Abbott districts), violated the "thorough and efficient education clause" of N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 4. The State was ordered to provide students attending Abbott districts with a constitutionally compliant education supported by adequate funding. Defendants sought a declaration that SFRA was constitutional, and that they could be relieved from the high court's remedial orders. Plaintiffs sought a declaration that those prior remedial orders remained in force. The high court held that the matter could not be resolved on an undeveloped record. The matter had to be remanded for further proceedings. In view of the history behind the enactment of SFRA, the burden would be on defendants to prove that SFRA's funding formula provided sufficient resources to enable the Abbott districts to deliver a thorough and efficient education, as defined by the New Jersey Core Curriculum Content Standards. Plaintiffs would have the burden of proof on factual issues relating to their specific claims of current year inadequate school funding.


OUTCOME: The matter was remanded to a trial court judge, sitting as a special master, to conduct a plenary hearing to develop a full and complete evidential record that addressed the factual contentions raised by the parties and amici curiae before the high court. The master was ordered to file a report on his factual findings and conclusions with the high court within 60 days of the completion of the hearings.

In re Taylor


A-30 September Term 2007


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


196 N.J. 162; 952 A.2d 1025; 2008 N.J. LEXIS 891


March 26, 2008, Argued 
July 28, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 393 N.J. Super. 213, 923 A.2d 239 (2007).
In re Taylor, 393 N.J. Super. 213, 923 A.2d 239, 2007 N.J. Super. LEXIS 140 (App.Div., 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county's petition for certification was granted allowing it to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed an Assignment Judge's decision granting respondent prosecutor's requests for additional funds for the Prosecutor's Office beyond the amount initially approved by the county. The prosecutor petitioned the Assignment Judge for the additional appropriations under N.J.S.A. § 2A:158-7.


OVERVIEW: The funds sought by the prosecutor were mostly for salary increases with some exceeding the previously agreed-to amounts in certain collective bargaining agreements. The Assignment Judge rejected the county's argument that the prosecutor must establish that the additional expenditures were necessary and concluded that the prosecutor needed only to show that his requests for the additional funds were appropriate. The main issue on appeal was what standard the Assignment Judge was required to apply, namely whether a reasonably necessary standard applied or an appropriate standard. The court held that the plain words of § 2A:158-7 authorized the Assignment Judge to approve expenses of the prosecutor that exceed the funds appropriated by the county only when those expenses were reasonably required by the prosecutor to fulfill the statutory responsibilities. As such, the Assignment Judge erred when he approved the salary increases after the prosecutor failed to establish that they were reasonably necessary or essential.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the Appellate Division and remanded the case to the Assignment Judge to determine whether the salary requests for the First Assistant Prosecutor and the Chief of Detectives were reasonably necessary.

State ex rel. J.A.


A-2 September Term 2007


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


195 N.J. 324; 949 A.2d 790; 2008 N.J. LEXIS 788


February 5, 2008, Argued 
June 23, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 385 N.J. Super. 544, 897 A.2d 1119 (2006).
State v. Sweet, 195 N.J. 357, 949 A.2d 809, 2008 N.J. LEXIS 787 (2008)
State ex rel. J.A., 385 N.J. Super. 544, 897 A.2d 1119, 2006 N.J. Super. LEXIS 156 (App.Div., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted the petition for certification filed by defendant juvenile, permitting defendant to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which upheld a family court's delinquency adjudication of defendant for an act, which if committed by an adult, would have constituted a second-degree robbery under N.J.S.A. § 2C:15-1.


OVERVIEW: The adjudication arose from defendant being identified as the juvenile who grabbed a woman's purse. An eyewitness spoke to an officer after the robbery. At trial, over defendant's objection, the family court permitted the officer to testify to the eyewitness's account as a present sense impression under N.J.R.E. 803(c)(1). The State explained that the witness refused to come to court. The Appellate Division affirmed defendant's adjudication of delinquency, finding that the family court properly admitted the non-appearing eyewitness's out-of-court statements through the officer as a present sense impression and as an excited utterance exception upon concluding that the witness had observed a startling event, a robbery, and reacted spontaneously and coincidently with the occurrence when he spoke to the officer. The court noted that the dispatcher to whom the eyewitness first had spoken was not called as a witness. The court held that the eyewitness's account was made approximately 10 minutes after the robbery and was not made while he was perceiving the event, as required by Rule 803(c)(1). As such, the family court erred by admitting the eyewitness's statements.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the Appellate Division and remanded the case to the family court for a new trial.

Oberhand v. Director, Div. of Taxation


A-106 September Term 2006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


193 N.J. 558; 940 A.2d 1202; 2008 N.J. LEXIS 110


October 9, 2007, Argued 
February 27, 2008, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 388 N.J. Super. 239, 23 N.J. Tax 431, 907 A.2d 428 (2006).
Oberhand v. Director, Div. of Taxation, Dept. of Treasury, 388 N.J. Super. 239, 23 N.J. Tax 431, 907 A.2d 428, 2006 N.J. Super. LEXIS 274 (App.Div., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Director of the New Jersey Division of Taxation imposed estate taxes on two decedents' estates under the amended version of N.J.S.A. § 54:38-1. Plaintiffs, the executor and executrix of the estates, sought review. The tax court held that the doctrine of manifest injustice precluded applying the amendment version retroactively. The Director appealed; the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, reversed. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: Congress amended the federal estate tax law effective January 1, 2002. To avoid revenue loss, New Jersey Legislature amended N.J.S.A. § 54:38-1 on July 1, 2002, to provide that the New Jersey estate tax would continue to be computed in accordance with the federal state tax credit in effect on December 31, 2001. Section 54:38-1 was made retroactive to January 1, 2002. Both decedents died after January 1, 2002, and before the amendments to § 54:38-1. Under the prior version of § 54:38-1, the decedents' estates would have owed no taxes. The tax court and the intermediate appellate court both agreed that the amendment applied to the estates; the latter disapproved the former's application of the doctrine of manifest injustice to a tax statute. The high court held that the July 2002 amendment to § 54:38-1 applied to the estates, but under the circumstances presented, the doctrine of manifest injustice barred retroactive application of the amendment to plaintiffs. The decedents reasonably relied on the previous law, and there was no showing that denial of retroactive application of the amendment to their estates would seriously impact New Jersey's revenues.


OUTCOME: The high court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's ruling that the amendment applied to the estates, reversed its ruling that the doctrine of manifest injustice did not bar the retroactive application § 54:38-1(a)(2), and remanded the matter to the tax court for further proceedings.

State v. O'Neill


A-79 September Term 2006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


193 N.J. 148; 936 A.2d 438; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 1507


September 10, 2007, Argued 
December 20, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 388 N.J. Super. 135, 906 A.2d 495 (2006).
State v. O'Neill, 388 N.J. Super. 135, 906 A.2d 495, 2006 N.J. Super. LEXIS 272 (App.Div., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted defendant's petition for certification, allowing him to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed his convictions for felony murder, in violation of N.J.S.A. § 2C:11-3(a)(3); first-degree robbery, in violation of N.J.S.A. § 2C:15-1; first-degree aggravated manslaughter, in violation of N.J.S.A. § 2C:11-4(a)(1); and other crimes.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's convictions arose from the killing of a taxi cab driver. While defendant was incarcerated for another crime, two homicide detectives subjected him to a 95-minute interrogation, eliciting from him incriminating statements that linked him to the killing. Only then, for the first time, did the detectives advise defendant of his Miranda rights. Without any significant break, the detectives resumed the interrogation, questioning defendant nearly five more hours, taking two taped statements, which singly and together, directly connected him to the shooting death of the taxi cab driver. At defendant's trial, the State only sought to admit defendant's statements made after the initial 95-minute unwarned interrogation. The trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress the statements he made after receiving the Miranda warnings. The court held that the question-first, warn-later interrogation procedure in the case violated defendant's State law privilege against self-incrimination. That privilege was rendered illusory because the detectives exploited defendant's admissions from the initial unwarned questioning.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court upholding defendant's convictions and remanded the case to the trial court for a new trial.

State v. Froland


A-8 September Term 2006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


193 N.J. 186; 936 A.2d 947; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 1437

December 12, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Clarified by, Modified by State v. Froland, 195 N.J. 413, 949 A.2d 844, 2008 N.J. LEXIS 438 (2008)
Related proceeding at State v. Kindt, 2008 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1123 (2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 378 N.J. Super. 20, 874 A.2d 568 (2005).
State v. Froland, 378 N.J. Super. 20, 874 A.2d 568, 2005 N.J. Super. LEXIS 170 (App.Div., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted defendant's petition for certification, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed defendant's conviction for non-consent kidnapping, in violation of N.J.S.A. § 2C:13-1. Defendant was sentenced to an aggregate custodial term of seven years.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was the stepmother of two children. The father, her husband, had devised a scheme to essentially disappear with the children and defendant to keep the children away from his ex-wife, with whom he had shared legal custody and, for a time, shared physical custody. At issue on appeal was whether defendant, as a stepmother, was guilty of non-consent kidnapping by removing the stepchildren from the state with the consent of their father, her husband, but without the consent of their mother. Defendant asserted that under the plain language of § 2C:13-1(d), a parent cannot be guilty of kidnapping his own children and she, therefore, could not be guilty of kidnapping since she acted with her husband's permission. The court agreed with defendant that she was not subject to conviction under § 2C:13-1(d) since her acts in removing the stepchildren were all pursuant to the consent of her husband. Further, since no allegations of force, threat, or deception were made against defendant, the conviction could not stand. The court noted that the interference with custody statute, N.J.S.A. § 2C:13-4(a), was applicable to the facts.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and remanded the case to the trial court for proceedings consistent with the principles to which the court adverted.

In re Contest of the November 8, 2005 General Election for Office of Mayor of Tp. of Parsippany-Troy Hills


A-90 September Term 2006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


192 N.J. 546; 934 A.2d 607; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 1255


May 2, 2007, Argued; September 11, 2007, Re-argued  
November 8, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 388 N.J. Super. 663, 909 A.2d 1199 (2006).
In re Contest of November 8, 2005 General Election for Office of Mayor for Tp. Parippany-Troy Hills, 388 N.J. Super. 663, 909 A.2d 1199, 2006 N.J. Super. LEXIS 316 (App.Div., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certification to respondent, an election winner, to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed a trial court judgment dismissing the petition contesting the mayoral election of a township filed by petitioner challenger, pursuant to N.J.S.A. §§ 19:29-1 to -14, the election contest statute.


OVERVIEW: The parties were opponents in the mayoral election. The challenger initially filed a verified petition, alleging that there were persons who were entitled to vote whose ballots had been rejected and that illegal votes had been permitted and counted. The challenger also alleged irregularities among the absentee ballots, mishandling of the same, and that some voters were provided wrongful assistance or were subjected to intimidation. The trial court had dismissed the petition upon the motion of the election winner, upon concluding that the challenger's amended complaint was insufficient to state a claim. The Appellate Division reversed, concluding that the election contest statute required interpretation in light of the modern rules of pleading. The court agreed with the Appellate Division's decision as to the sufficiency of the petition but disagreed as to its rationale. The court held that because N.J. Stat. Ann. § 19:29-7 empowered only a court to compel a challenged voter to reveal his vote, no challenger would ever be able to overcome the inherent vagueness that existed until discovery was conducted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the Appellate Division, as modified, by not adopting the Appellate Division's view that modern pleading rules were to be engrafted onto the election statute's requirements so as to test the petition against liberal notice pleading concepts. The court remanded the case to the assignment judge for further proceedings.

 

87 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Acuna v. Turkish


A-15 September Term 2006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


192 N.J. 399; 930 A.2d 416; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 1058


February 20, 2007, Argued 
September 12, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Acuna v. Turkish, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 6990 (U.S., Oct. 6, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinions are reported at 384 N.J. Super. 395, 894 A.2d 1208 (2006) and 354 N.J. Super. 500, 808 A.2d 149 (2002).
Acuna v. Turkish, 384 N.J. Super. 395, 894 A.2d 1208, 2006 N.J. Super. LEXIS 102 (App.Div., 2006)



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff patient sued defendant doctor, asserting claims of negligent infliction of emotion distress (NIED) and negligence based on lack of informed consent. The trial court granted the doctor summary judgment on both claims. The New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, reversed the dismissal of these claims and remanded for further consideration. The doctor appealed.


OVERVIEW: The patient alleged the doctor breached a duty owed to her by failing to inform her that her six- to eight-week-old embryo was a unique human being and that an abortion would result in killing an existing human being. She signed a form giving her consent to an abortion, which was duly performed. The trial court held a physician was not required to advise a woman that her non-viable embryo was a living human being to obtain her informed consent for an abortion and dismissed the lack-of-informed consent and NIED claims. The intermediate appellate court held that a jury issue remained concerning whether the doctor accurately answered the patient's question, "Is the baby already there?" The high court disagreed. Although the doctor had a common law duty to provide the patient with information about the medical risks of an abortion, there was no common law duty requiring him to instruct her that the embryo was an existing human being, and to suggest that an abortion was tantamount to murder. Further, there was no basis to create a new duty that had no broad support in either the medical community or society.


OUTCOME: The high court reversed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court and reinstated the trial court's order dismissing the patient's informed consent and NIED claims.

State v. Elders


A-42 September Term 2006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


192 N.J. 224; 927 A.2d 1250; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 925


February 13, 2007, Argued 
July 30, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 386 N.J. Super. 208, 899 A.2d 1037 (2006).
State v. Elders, 386 N.J. Super. 208, 899 A.2d 1037, 2006 N.J. Super. LEXIS 175 (App.Div., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were granted leave to appeal the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed a trial court ruling that had granted defendants' motion to suppress evidence seized from defendants' disabled vehicle, which was stopped on the side of a highway. The State had initiated the appeal of the trial court's suppression order.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were travelling in two separate vehicles when one became disabled. On the basis of the nervousness of some defendants, the absence of a registered owner, and the suspicion aroused by the gas tank falling off, the troopers sought consent to search the disabled vehicle and found drugs under the hood. The trial judge determined that the troopers engaged in an unconstitutional investigatory detention and search as they were not premised on a reasonable and articulable suspicion and because a knowing and voluntary consent to search was not given. The appellate court, in deciding not to give any special deference to the trial judge's factfinding, reversed the suppression upon relying on the videotape and concluding that the troopers had the requisite suspicion. The court held that the appellate court improperly substituted its own factfindings for those of the trial judge, particularly based on the availability of the videotape, which did not extinguish the deference owed to the trial court's findings. Sufficient credible evidence was found to have supported the trial court's finding that the State fell short of showing that there was an articulable suspicion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment and reinstated the trial judge's order suppressing the evidence.

Committee for a Better Twin Rivers v. Twin Rivers Homeowners' Ass'n


A-118/122 September Term 2005


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


192 N.J. 344; 929 A.2d 1060; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 911; 51 A.L.R.6th 741


January 4, 2007, Argued 
July 26, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On appeal from and certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 383 N.J. Super. 22, 890 A.2d 947 (2006).
Committee for a Better Twin Rivers v. Twin Rivers Homeowners' Ass'n, 383 N.J. Super. 22, 890 A.2d 947, 2006 N.J. Super. LEXIS 29 (App.Div., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certification to defendant homeowners' association to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed a trial court decision that held that defendant's rules and regulations did not violate New Jersey's constitutional guarantees of free expression. Plaintiffs, a committee of residents, brought suit seeking injunctive relief to invalidate various rules and regulations.


OVERVIEW: The rules and regulations enacted by defendant at issue governed the posting of signs, the use of a community room, and access to its newsletter. Plaintiffs asserted that the rules and regulations violated New Jersey's constitutional guarantees of free expression. The trial court concluded that the restrictive covenants at issue regarding the posting of signs were reasonable, that plaintiffs were not denied access to defendant's newspaper, and that the rate charged for the community rule was impermissibly vague but upheld the amended policy of a unified rate charged for the community room. The lower court reversed upon concluding that defendant was subject to state constitutional standards with respect to its internal rules and regulations. The court, in applying the balancing test between expressional rights and privacy interests, held that the minor restrictions on plaintiffs' expressional activities were not unreasonable nor oppressive and that defendant was not acting as a municipality. The court held that defendant's restrictions were reasonable concerning the time, place, and manner of such restrictions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court and reinstated the judgment of the trial court.

Stomel v. City of Camden


A-45/46 September Term 2006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


192 N.J. 137; 927 A.2d 129; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 909; 26 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 705; 154 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P60,458


January 4, 2007, Argued 
July 25, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at Stomel v. City of Camden, 2010 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1930 (App.Div., Aug. 10, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 383 N.J. Super. 615, 893 A.2d 32 (2006).
Stomel v. City of Camden, 383 N.J. Super. 615, 893 A.2d 32, 2006 N.J. Super. LEXIS 71 (App.Div., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, a former public defender, sued defendants, a city, its mayor, and its council, asserting claims under New Jersey's Conscientious Employee Protection Act (CEPA), N.J.S.A. §§ 34:19-1 to -8, and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The trial court dismissed all but the § 1983 claim against the mayor. The New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, affirmed dismissal of the § 1983 claims but reversed dismissal of the CEPA claims. All parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff testified as a witness for the U.S. government in a political corruption trial of the city's municipal prosecutor; his testimony implicated the mayor in unlawful activity. The case ended in a mistrial; three days later, the mayor fired him as municipal public defender after more than 17 years of service. Plaintiff alleged this violated CEPA; his § 1983 claim was based on his allegation that he was removed from office in violation of his First Amendment rights. The intermediate appellate court held that plaintiff was an "employee" for purposes of his CEPA claim and reversed the entry of summary judgment in favor of the city on that claim. It also held that the city could not be held vicariously liable for the mayor's actions on the § 1983 claim. The high court held that under New Jersey's Faulkner Act, the mayor was the "policy-maker" for the city in respect of plaintiff's removal as municipal public defender; therefore, the § 1983 claim against the city based on his actions should not have been dismissed. Also, plaintiff set forth a prima facie case that, as municipal public defender, he was an "employee" of the city for purposes of advancing his CEPA claim.


OUTCOME: The high court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's reversal of the entry of summary judgment in favor of the city on plaintiff's CEPA claim, but reversed its decision affirming the trial court's dismissal of plaintiff's § 1983 claim against the city. It remanded the matter to trial court for further proceedings.

State v. Williams

SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


192 N.J. 1; 926 A.2d 340; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 719


March 6, 2007, Argued 
July 12, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at, Remanded by State v. Williams, 2007 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 192 (2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 381 N.J. Super. 572, 887 A.2d 190 (2005).
State v. Williams, 381 N.J. Super. 572, 887 A.2d 190, 2005 N.J. Super. LEXIS 360 (App.Div., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted the State's petition for certification to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed a trial court's denial of defendant's motion to suppress, vacated the judgments of convictions, and remanded the case to the trial court in light of the suppression of the handgun seized from defendant. Defendant was convicted on a weapons possession charge.


OVERVIEW: The jury acquitted defendant on an obstruction charge. The convictions against defendant arose from a call reporting possible drug activity and the subsequent investigatory stop of defendant from which he fled. The pursuit that ensured did not last long since defendant stumbled and fell. After arresting defendant, a handgun was found tucked in his waistband. The appellate court also determined that defendant had a right to refuse to participate in the State's unlawful dominion over him. The court found error, concluding that defendant's resistance to the pat down and flight from the police was an intervening act, particularly the crime of obstruction, that completely purged the taint from the unconstitutional investigatory stop.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court, which ordered suppression of the handgun, and reinstated defendant's judgments of conviction. The court remanded the case to the appellate court for that panel to consider defendant's other arguments raised on direct appeal that were not addressed in the panel's opinion.

Gallenthin Realty Development, Inc. v. Borough of Paulsboro


A-51 September Term 2006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


191 N.J. 344; 924 A.2d 447; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 694


April 26, 2007, Argued 
June 13, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Gallenthin Realty Development, Inc. v. Borough of Paulsboro, 188 N.J. 492, 909 A.2d 727, 2006 N.J. LEXIS 1542 (2006)



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent borough classified appellant property owners' land as "in need of redevelopment" under N.J.S.A. § 40A:12A-5(e), which subjected the land to taking by eminent domain. The owners appealed; the trial court and the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, upheld the borough's redevelopment designation. The owners sought further review, challenging the constitutionality of § 40A:12A-5(e) as applied to their land.


OVERVIEW: The land was mostly undeveloped open space and protected wetlands. It was zoned as a marine industrial business park, and portions of it were used for river access, employee parking, storage, and for growing cattle feed. At a hearing held by the borough's planning board, one of the owners testified that they also sought to resume using the land as a dredging depot. As part of its master plan, the borough classified the land as "in need of redevelopment" under N.J.S.A. § 40A:12A-5(e), on the basis that its unimproved condition rendered it "not fully productive." The high court held that because N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 3, para. 1, authorized government redevelopment of only "blighted" areas, the legislature did not intend N.J.S.A. § 40A:12A-5(e) to apply in circumstances where the sole basis for redevelopment was that the property was "not fully productive." Rather, § 40A:12A-5(e) applied only to areas that, as a whole, were stagnant and unproductive because of issues of title, diversity of ownership, or other similar conditions. Moreover, the record was silent as to whether the borough considered the benefits the land's freshwater wetlands status conferred on the public.


OUTCOME: The high court reversed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court and invalidated the borough's redevelopment designation.

Cooper University Hosp. v. Jacobs


A-88/89 September Term 2006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


191 N.J. 125; 922 A.2d 731; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 600


March 20, 2007, Argued  
May 31, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Cooper University Hosp. v. Jacobs, 193 N.J. 271, 937 A.2d 975, 2007 N.J. LEXIS 1522 (2007)
Related proceeding at Cooper Univ. Hosp. v. Jacobs, 2009 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2493 (App.Div., Sept. 10, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Cooper Univ. Hosp. v. Jacobs, 189 N.J. 429, 915 A.2d 1051, 2007 N.J. LEXIS 52 (2007)
Cooper Univ. Hosp. v. Jacobs, 2006 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1844 (App.Div., Oct. 25, 2006)
Cooper Univ. Hosp. v. Jacobs, 189 N.J. 429, 915 A.2d 1051, 2007 N.J. LEXIS 51 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The petition for certification of appellants, various cardiac surgery centers, was granted to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which concluded that respondent, the Commissioner of the Department of Health and Senior Services, was authorized to issue a call inviting health care facilities without a cardiac surgery facility on site to apply for a certificate of need (CN) to conduct elective angioplasty.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, various community hospitals, were also parties to the action. The call stated that six successful applicants would be granted a CN to participate in the Atlantic C-PORT Trial, Elective Angioplasty Study (Atlantic C-PORT-E), a planned multi-state demonstration project to test the safety, quality, and cost of elective angioplasty offered at community hospitals that do not also offer cardiac surgery services on site. Appellants argued that the call under N.J.A.C. 8:33-3.11(e) was ultra vires principally because it authorized, as a demonstration project, a procedure that was expressly prohibited by other regulations. The court determined that although § 8:33-3.11(e) authorized the call, the regulation, as applied, violated fundamental principles relating to the regulatory process. As such, the court was unable to sustain the grant of the CNs for the Atlantic C-PORT-E study. The court directed that the projects could continue through November 30, 2007. The court further directed that a proper regulation must be promulgated, after appropriate adherence to the principles of rulemaking, before any such demonstration project could be continued beyond that date.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court and remanded the case to the Commissioner for further proceedings, including the promulgation of proper regulations addressing calls involving demonstration projects.

State v. Wakefield


A-37 September Term 2004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


190 N.J. 397; 921 A.2d 954; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 454


October 12, 2005, Argued  
May 7, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Wakefield v. New Jersey, 552 U.S. 1146, 128 S. Ct. 1074, 169 L. Ed. 2d 817, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 1040 (2008)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Wakefield, 2015 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1256 (App.Div., May 29, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Atlantic County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to N.J.S.A. § 2C:11-3(e) and R. 2:2-1(a)(3), defendant filed a direct appeal of his death sentence imposed by the Superior Court, Law Division, Atlantic County (New Jersey). Defendant had pled guilty to two counts of capital murder. The court also granted leave to the Association of Criminal Defense Lawyers of New Jersey to appear as amicus curiae.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's convictions arose from the home invasion and murder of an elderly couple. The State had served notice of aggravating factors as a prerequisite for the imposition of the death penalty, asserting that the murder of the male victim was committed while defendant was engaged in robbery and/or burglary and that it was committed for the purpose of escaping detection. The same aggravating factors were alleged as to the female victim. The court rejected defendant's claims that the State was permitted to introduce vast amounts of inflammatory evidence since the evidence, such as defendant's statements to the police, a picture of the murder scene, defendant's underlying crimes, and defendant's past criminal history, were all relevant in the penalty phase trial regarding aggravating and mitigating factors. The court found no merit with regard to defendant's assertions of prosecutorial misconduct throughout the penalty phase trial as the trial court immediately issued a curative instruction when necessary and defendant failed to show any prejudicial effect on his right to a fair trial.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the proceedings were fair, that defendant's death sentence was properly imposed, and that his death sentence was not disproportionate. The court affirmed the judgment of conviction and sentence, including defendant's death sentence.

State v. Drury


A-110 September Term 2005


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


190 N.J. 197; 919 A.2d 813; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 447


November 29, 2006, Argued  
April 24, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Drury, 2012 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 790 (App.Div., Apr. 12, 2012)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Drury v. New Jersey, 2017 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 165025 (D.N.J., Oct. 5, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 382 N.J. Super. 469, 889 A.2d 1087 (2006).
State v. Drury, 382 N.J. Super. 469, 889 A.2d 1087, 2006 N.J. Super. LEXIS 16 (App.Div., Jan. 27, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first-degree aggravated sexual assault (N.J.S.A. § 2C:14-2(a)), aggravated assault, theft, terroristic threats, and four counts of first-degree carjacking (N.J.S.A. § 2C:15-2) and first-degree kidnapping. The Superior Court, Appellate Division, modified the first-degree sexual assault conviction to second-degree but rejected defendant's Natale challenge to his carjacking and kidnapping sentences. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered a car occupied by four teenagers trying to buy marijuana. He directed them to a house, which he and a passenger entered. He raped her in the house. They returned to the car; he ordered the driver to let him drive, and drove around for about 45 minutes. He refused the victims' requests to leave the car, but they escaped after he stopped the car and left. The high court noted that carjacking was not an offense listed in N.J.S.A. § 2C:14-2(a)(3) that elevated a sexual assault to a first-degree aggravated sexual assault. Although robbery was such a predicate offense, robbery and carjacking were dissimilar in many respects. Further, the legislative history indicated that carjacking was not intended to be a predicate offense under § 2C:14-2(a)(3). Therefore, the intermediate appellate court properly reduced defendant's conviction to second-degree sexual assault (albeit for a different reason). When defendant was sentenced, former N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:44-1f(1)(a) provided for a 20-year presumptive term for kidnapping. As his 25-year sentences for kidnapping exceeded the formerly-established presumptive term for that crime, his sentence was illegal under State v. Natale.


OUTCOME: The high court affirmed modification of the first degree sexual assault conviction to second-degree sexual assault. It reversed the ruling that the kidnapping sentence was not entitled to a Natale remand. It ordered the trial court to impose a sentence for second-degree, not first-degree, sexual assault; to convict defendant of third-degree aggravated assault; and to resentence him under Natale for kidnapping and third-degree terroristic threats.

Township of Holmdel v. New Jersey Highway Authority


A-64 September Term 2006


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


190 N.J. 74; 918 A.2d 603; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 341


January 30, 2007, Argued  
April 4, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 388 N.J. Super. 36, 23 N.J. Tax 426, 905 A.2d 900 (2006).
Township of Holmdel v. New Jersey Highway Authority, 388 N.J. Super. 36, 23 N.J. Tax 426, 905 A.2d 900, 2006 N.J. Super. LEXIS 261 (App.Div., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the New Jersey Highway Authority, and respondent, a township, both were granted interlocutory leave to appeal the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which summarily upheld a tax court ruling that determined that the Authority's reception center and amphitheater were both subject to real property taxation for 1997, 1998, 2000, 2001, 2002, and 2004, and that, for 1996, only the reception center was taxable.


OVERVIEW: The Authority completed constructed of the PNC Bank Arts Center, formerly known as the Garden State Arts Center, in 1968, which consisted of an amphitheater. The New Jersey Legislature investigated the operation of the Authority, which resulted in the Legislature amending the definition of a highway project under L. 1968, c. 348, § 1, to include only those facilities directly related to the use of highways. The continued operation of existing facilities or activities by the Authority were not affected by the provisions of the amendment, therefore, the operation of the amphitheater was statutorily established as a highway purpose. However, the reception center did not exist in 1968 but was established in 1972 as a for-profit facility. The court held that because the operation of the amphitheater furthered the Arts Center's original purposes of providing public access to performing arts and generating revenue, it was exempt from local property taxation. However, because the construction and privatization of the reception center were dramatic, unanticipated departures from the Authority's statutory mandate, the reception center was subject to taxation for all years under appeal.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, in part, the judgment holding that the amphitheater and attendant facilities were subject to taxation. The court affirmed, in part, the judgment holding the reception center was taxable. The court remanded the case for an assessment of the Authority's tax liability consistent with the court's opinion.

Simon v. Cronecker


A-105/A-106 September Term 2005


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


189 N.J. 304; 915 A.2d 489; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 22


September 12, 2006, Argued  
January 29, 2007, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to Superior Court, Chancery Division, Cape May County.
Simon v. Cronecker, 188 N.J. 259, 905 A.2d 869, 2006 N.J. LEXIS 464 (2006)
Simon v. Rando, 189 N.J. 339, 915 A.2d 509, 2007 N.J. LEXIS 21 (2007)



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, two tax sale certificate holders, were granted certification to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Chancery Division, Cape May County (New Jersey), which permitted respondent investor to intervene and approved the redemption of the tax sale certificates. The holders had each filed an action to foreclose on their respective tax certificates against respondent property owners.


OVERVIEW: The holders had purchased their respective tax sale certificates. After a search of public records, the investor learned of the foreclosure complaints and contracted with the property owners to purchase the respective lots at issue. The property owners delivered a cashier's check to the tax collector's office to redeem the tax sale certificates after closing on the lots with the investor. The tax collector accepted the redemption checks in each case but the holders refused to surrender their respective certificates and filed motions to bar redemption. In response, the investor sought to intervene in the actions. The court held that the Tax Sale Law, N.J.S.A. §§ 54:5-1 to -137, did not prohibit a third-party investor from redeeming a tax sale certificate after the filing of a foreclosure action, provided that the investor timely intervened in the action and paid the property owner more than nominal consideration for the property. The court did not find that the investor's actions violated public policy as well as agreed that the investor paid more than nominal consideration to the property owners. However, by failing to intervene, the investor violated N.J.S.A. §§ 54:5-89.1 and -98.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings. The court imposed constructive trusts in favor of the property owners, granting the tax certificate holders the opportunity to assume the investor's contractual rights.
