State v. O'Hagen


A-70 September Term 2005


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


189 N.J. 140; 914 A.2d 267; 2007 N.J. LEXIS 16


September 26, 2006, Argued  
January 24, 2007, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at A.A. v. Attorney General of New Jersey, 189 N.J. 128, 914 A.2d 260, 2007 N.J. LEXIS 15 (N.J., Jan. 24, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 380 N.J. Super. 133, 881 A.2d 733 (2005).
State v. O'Hagen, 380 N.J. Super. 133, 881 A.2d 733, 2005 N.J. Super. LEXIS 271 (App.Div.2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After pleading guilty to possession of a controlled dangerous substance, defendant was ordered to submit a biological sample for DNA testing and storage under N.J.S.A. § 53:1-20.20(g) of the New Jersey DNA Database and Databank Act of 1994, N.J.S.A. §§ 53:1-20.17-20.28. He appealed, arguing the Act was unconstitutional. The New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, found the Act constitutional. Defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the Act, which required all persons convicted of a crime to give a DNA sample, violated the Fourth and Fourteenth Amendments and N.J. Const. art. I, paras. 1 and 7. The high court disagreed. Applying the special needs test, it found that mandatory DNA testing extended beyond ordinary law enforcement and presented a "special need" that justified privacy intrusions absent individualized suspicion. Therefore, requiring defendant to provide a DNA sample without a warrant did not violate the Fourth Amendment or N.J. Const. art. I, para 7. As to defendant's equal protection challenge, the Act was rationally related to a legitimate governmental interest of having a data bank of DNA to solve future crimes, exonerate the wrongfully convicted, and deter crime. All similarly situated individuals -- those convicted of a crime -- were required to provide a DNA sample. Due to the impracticality of imposing a warrant requirement, the overriding public need for DNA data, a convicted felon's lessened expectation of privacy, and the minimal nature of the physical intrusion (a swab of the person's cheek), defendant's constitutional rights to equal protection were not violated.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.

Lewis v. Harris


A-68 September Term 2005


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


188 N.J. 415; 908 A.2d 196; 2006 N.J. LEXIS 1521


February 15, 2006, Argued  
October 25, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by Lewis v. Harris, 202 N.J. 340, 997 A.2d 227, 2010 N.J. LEXIS 699 (2010)
Related proceeding at Garden State Equality v. Dow, 2012 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 360 (Law Div., Feb. 21, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinions are reported at 378 N.J. Super. 168, 875 A.2d 259 (2005).
Lewis v. Harris, 378 N.J. Super. 168, 875 A.2d 259, 2005 N.J. Super. LEXIS 186 (App.Div., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff same-sex couples sued defendant state officials seeking a declaration that New Jersey laws banning same-sex marriage violated the liberty and equal protection guarantees of N.J. Const. art. I, para. 1, and injunctive relief compelling the officials to grant them marriage licenses. The trial court granted the officials summary judgment. The New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, affirmed. The couples sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The couples, who had been in permanent, committed relationships for more than 10 years, were denied marriage licenses. The high court agreed with the lower courts that New Jersey's marriage statutes did not violate N.J. Const. art. I, para. 1's substantive due process guarantees, but found that they violated its equal protection provisions. While age-old traditions, beliefs, and laws defined marriage to be between a man and a woman, attitudes had changed, as evidenced by statutes and court rulings banning differential treatment based on sexual orientation. Even after enactment of the New Jersey Domestic Partnership Act, L. 2003, c. 246, many of the rights and benefits accorded to married couples were denied to committed same-sex couples. The officials did not articulate any legitimate public need for depriving same-sex couples of the host of benefits and privileges of marriage. To remedy the constitutional violation, the legislature could either amend the marriage statutes to include same-sex couples or enact a parallel statutory structure by another name, in which same-sex couples would enjoy the rights and benefits and bear the burdens and obligations of civil marriage.


OUTCOME: The high court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's ruling that New Jersey's marriage laws did not contravene N.J. Const. art. I, para. 1's substantive due process guarantee, but reversed its ruling that the laws did not violate the equal protection guarantee. Within 180 days, the legislature had to either amend the marriage statutes or enact a statutory structure that afforded committed same-sex couples the same rights of married couples.


 

100 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Jimenez


A-50 September Term 2005


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


188 N.J. 390; 908 A.2d 181; 2006 N.J. LEXIS 1520


November 29, 2005, Argued  
October 24, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by State v. Jimenez, 190 N.J. 397, 921 A.2d 954, 2006 N.J. LEXIS 1842 (N.J., 2006)
Clarified by, Remanded by New Jersey v. Jimenez, 191 N.J. 453, 924 A.2d 513, 2007 N.J. LEXIS 700 (N.J., June 18, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 380 N.J. Super. 1, 880 A.2d 468 (2005).
State v. Jimenez, 380 N.J. Super. 1, 880 A.2d 468, 2005 N.J. Super. LEXIS 251 (App.Div., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a prosecution for capital murder under N.J.S.A. § 2C:11-3(c), defendant claimed he was mentally retarded and thus ineligible for the death penalty under Atkins v. Virginia. After the trial court devised procedures and burdens of proof for evaluating this claim, New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, reversed the trial court's pre-trial procedures but affirmed its order regarding the guilt phase proceedings. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant confessed to sexually assaulting and murdering a 10-year-old boy; his DNA matched DNA found on the victim's clothes. The trial court ruled that if defendant proved at a pretrial hearing, by clear and convincing evidence, he was retarded, he could not be executed. If he established his retardation by a preponderance and was found guilty of murder, the State had to disprove retardation beyond a reasonable doubt at a sequential trial in order for defendant to be eligible for execution. The intermediate appellate court held that the State had the initial burden to prove to the jury, beyond a reasonable doubt, that defendant was not retarded. The high court disagreed. The jury had to first consider whether defendant was guilty of capital murder beyond a reasonable doubt; he could raise the issue of retardation to negate an element of the crime. If the jury found him guilty, it considered whether a statutory capital trigger existed beyond a reasonable doubt. If so, defendant had to prove he was retarded by a preponderance of the evidence to avoid execution under Atkins. If he failed to do so, he could still raise his mental retardation as a mitigator during the penalty phase.


OUTCOME: The high court reversed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court and remanded the case to the trial court. The high court referred to the New Jersey Trial Judges Committee on Capital Causes the task of developing rule recommendations implementing the decision. In the interim, trial courts had to follow the general procedures set forth in the high court's opinion.

American Fire and Cas. Co. v. New Jersey Div. of Taxation


A-134 September Term 2004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


189 N.J. 65; 912 A.2d 126; 2006 N.J. LEXIS 1512


November 30, 2005, Argued  
October 19, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion has been reported at 375 N.J. Super. 434, 868 A.2d 346 (2005).
American Fire and Cas. Co. v. New Jersey Div. of Taxation, 375 N.J. Super. 434, 868 A.2d 346, 2005 N.J. Super. LEXIS 75 (App.Div., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Certification was granted to petitioner, the Director of the Division of Taxation, to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed a judgment in the Director's favor interpreting the retaliatory tax statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 17:32-15, and the premium tax cap statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 54:18A-6. Plaintiffs, three foreign insurance companies, filed suit challenging the Director's interpretation.


OVERVIEW: The suit arose from the Director rejecting the insurance companies' respective applications for taxes paid when the premium cap was not applied originally in their calculations. The Director had interpreted the two statutes based on a plain reading of their language and independently of each other. Under the Director's interpretation, although an insurer's payment of the premium tax was capped, the Director's application of retaliatory tax raised the insurer's total tax obligation to a point that equated to a foreign state's rate. Therefore, a foreign insurer effectively received no benefit from the premium tax cap. The court held that the Director's interpretation lead to an unreasonable result that the New Jersey Legislature could not have intended. The court found that the Director's interpretation of the premium tax cap provided no incentive for foreign insurers to conduct business in New Jersey because the retaliatory tax fully recaptured any benefit provided by the premium tax cap. Likewise, the Director's interpretation failed to promote the retaliatory tax statute's purpose of encouraging even-handed treatment of insurers between states.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court on statutory grounds and instructed the Director to recalculate refunds due to the insurance companies in accordance with its opinion.

Hardwicke v. American Boychoir School


A-17 September Term 2004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


188 N.J. 69; 902 A.2d 900; 2006 N.J. LEXIS 1153


November 29, 2004, Argued  
August 8, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 368 N.J. Super. 71, 845 A.2d 619 (2004).
Hardwicke v. Am. Boychoir Sch., 368 N.J. Super. 71, 845 A.2d 619, 2004 N.J. Super. LEXIS 113 (App.Div., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted defendant school's motion for leave to appeal the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed the trial court's dismissal of the claims of plaintiffs, a former student and his wife. Plaintiffs brought suit against the school and defendants, its musical director and others, based on the Child Sexual Abuse Act (CSAA), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:61B-1, negligent hiring/supervision, and other claims.


OVERVIEW: The former student asserted that when he was a 12-year-old residential student at the school, its musical director repeatedly sexually abused him and that the school knew, or should have known, of the abuse. Plaintiffs also sought damages based on intentional and negligent infliction of emotional distress, breach of the duty to disclose the abuse, active concealment of the abuse, assault and battery, false imprisonment, and loss of consortium. The trial court granted summary judgment dismissal to the school, finding that plaintiffs' claims were barred by the Charitable Immunity Act (CIA), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2A:53-7 to -11. The lower court reversed upon concluding that the CIA did not bar suits based on intentional conduct. The court agreed and held that, under the CSAA, a school was a person standing in loco parentis within a household and that the CIA did not bar statutory or common-law claims based on willful, wanton, or grossly negligent conduct. The discovery rule provisions of the CSAA were held to apply to common-law claims based on sexual abuse, and the court also held that an employer could be held vicariously liable for the acts of its employees for child abuse claims.


OUTCOME: Modifying the lower court judgment by holding that plaintiffs were permitted to pursue a statutory cause of action and any common-law claims of sexual abuse based on intentional conduct, the court affirmed the judgment, as modified. The court remanded the case to the trial court for it to determine whether the two-year statute of limitations under the CSAA was tolled.

State v. Pierce


A-18 September Term 2005


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


188 N.J. 155; 902 A.2d 1195; 2006 N.J. LEXIS 1151


November 30, 2005, Argued  
August 2, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected August 10, 2006.
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Pierce, 2010 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2859 (App.Div., Dec. 1, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Pierce, 186 N.J. 241, 892 A.2d 1288, 2005 N.J. LEXIS 1625 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of robbery, unlawful possession of a weapon, possession of a weapon for an unlawful purpose, and possession of a weapon by a prohibited person. He was designated a persistent offender, under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:44-3, and sentenced to 40 years with a 16-year parole ineligibility component. The Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), affirmed the convictions and sentence, and defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant said his persistent offender sentencing involved impermissible judicial fact-finding. The supreme court held the sentencing court did not engage in impermissible fact-finding by assessing a record of convictions and finding him statutorily eligible for a discretionary extended term as a persistent offender. Required findings of a "need to protect the public," before imposing an extended term, had to be made after he was found to be a persistent offender, which exposed him to a range from the minimum ordinary sentence to the maximum enhanced sentence. Considering objective facts about his prior convictions did not violate the Sixth Amendment. He was subject to the extended-term range under Blakely's prior-conviction exception. His sentence had to be reconsidered due to the expanded range he was exposed to, considering aggravating and mitigating factors. A "need to protect the public" could be judicially found once defendant was eligible for an enhanced sentence. He was not subjected retroactively to a "new" statutory maximum that only a jury could impose. He could not receive a sentence in excess of the prior one.


OUTCOME: Defendant's sentence was reversed, and the matter was remanded to the trial court for resentencing.

State v. Crawley


A-52 September Term 2005


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


187 N.J. 440; 901 A.2d 924; 2006 N.J. LEXIS 1141


February 15, 2006, Argued  
July 24, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Crawley v. New Jersey, 127 S. Ct. 740, 166 L. Ed. 2d 563, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 9271 (U.S., 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Crawley, 185 N.J. 297, 884 A.2d 1266, 2005 N.J. LEXIS 1372 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted defendant's petition for certification to challenge the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed defendant's conviction for obstruction, in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:29-1. Defendant was sentenced to a one-year probationary term, along with appropriate fines, assessments, and court costs.


OVERVIEW: Two police officers on patrol received a dispatch from headquarters that a person was armed with a gun outside a bar. Minutes later, near the bar, the officers sighted a man matching the description given in the dispatch and ordered him to stop for questioning. Instead, the man, later identified as defendant, ran. After an intense pursuit, the officers arrested defendant. Defendant claimed on appeal that, because the officers engaged in an unconstitutional investigatory stop, the officers were not lawfully performing an official function and, therefore, he should have been found not guilty. The court disagreed and held that substantial credible evidence existed in the record to support the findings of the trial court that the officers ordered defendant to stop for questioning and that defendant clearly understood that command, but ran. Upon examining the language of the sister statutes involving resisting arrest, eluding, and escape, the court determined that the New Jersey Legislature intended that, when a police officer was acting in good faith and under color of his authority, a person had to obey the officer's order to stop and could not take flight without violating § 2C:29-1.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which upheld defendant's conviction of obstruction.

State v. DiFrisco


A-77 September Term 2005


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


187 N.J. 156; 900 A.2d 820; 2006 N.J. LEXIS 1070


January 31, 2006, Argued  
July 5, 2006, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at N.J. v. Anthony Difrisco, 2009 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 381 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County.
State v. DiFrisco, 174 N.J. 195, 804 A.2d 507, 2002 N.J. LEXIS 1110 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to capital murder and was sentenced to death. The Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County (New Jersey), denied defendant's second post-conviction relief petition. Defendant appealed, arguing that his death sentence should be vacated because, had the penalty and the proportionality reviews been combined, a majority of the state supreme court would have voted to overturn his death sentence and impose a life sentence.


OVERVIEW: The state supreme court concluded that although defendant's petition would be barred under the five-year limitation of N.J. Ct. R. 3:22-12 because he filed it on August 1, 2003, which was more than 10 years after the trial court's February 5, 1993, death sentence, defendant's petition was not barred because his failure to file it before the expiration of the time limit was due to excusable neglect and barring his petition would result in an injustice under N.J. Ct. R. 1:1-2. Also, because both the penalty and proportionality reviews determined whether a death sentence had been properly imposed, the two reviews were not distinct but rather different aspects of the same consideration--whether there was error in the imposition of the death penalty. Defendant's death sentence had to be vacated and a life sentence imposed because the death sentence pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3 was upheld only because the penalty review was separated into two proceedings, not because a majority of the state supreme court believed that imposition of the death sentence was proper. Four Justices found that the death sentence was improperly imposed and that defendant should receive a life sentence.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed the trial court's denial of defendant's petition for post-conviction relief, vacated defendant's death sentence, and remanded the case to the trial court for imposition of a life sentence.

In re P.L. 2001, Chapter 362.


A-129/130 September Term 2004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


186 N.J. 368; 895 A.2d 1128; 2006 N.J. LEXIS 389


January 4, 2006, Argued  
April 19, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 375 N.J. Super. 485, 868 A.2d 1034 (2005).
Williams v. State, 375 N.J. Super. 485, 868 A.2d 1034, 2005 N.J. Super. LEXIS 59 (App.Div., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Both certification petitions filed by appellant, the State of New Jersey, and intervenor, a probation association union, were granted with regard to the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division decision declaring the Probation Officer Community Safety Unit Act (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2B:10A-1 to -3 and 2C:39-6(c)(17), unconstitutional. Respondent, the Administrative Director of the Courts, had commenced the suit.


OVERVIEW: Intervenor, the Probation Association of New Jersey, was also a party. The Act authorized probation officers to be armed and make arrests and effectively changed their status from judicial personnel to law enforcement officers. The Administrative Director's complaint sought a judgment declaring the Act violated the New Jersey Constitution by infringing on the exclusive powers of the judiciary under N.J. Const. art. VI and by breaching the separation of powers under N.J. Const. art. III. The complaint alleged that the Act invested probation officers with law enforcement powers, which were incompatible with their judicial roles and placed them under the dual supervision of the Court and the Attorney General. The Court wholly agreed with the lower court's decision that the Act violated its constitutional authority over the administration of the courts and breached the separation of powers between the judicial and legislative branches of government since it directed the judiciary to assign probation officers to serve in a law enforcement capacity, subjected those officers to the supervision of the executive branch, and required the Administrative Director to report to the legislature.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court and declared the Act void.

Loigman v. Twp. Comm.


A-99 September Term 2004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


185 N.J. 566; 889 A.2d 426; 2006 N.J. LEXIS 34


October 12, 2005, Argued  
January 18, 2006, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Loigman v. Twp. Comm. of Middletown, 183 N.J. 213, 871 A.2d 91, 2005 N.J. LEXIS 486 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted the petition for certification filed by appellant township with regard to the township's challenge of the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed a trial court decision finding the township had violated 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. Respondent activist had brought the § 1983 suit against the township and appellant township attorney, challenging a sequestration motion.


OVERVIEW: During a case before an administrative law judge (ALJ), the township attorney filed a motion for sequestration, seeking to have the activist removed from the proceeding. The attorney asserted that the activist was a potential witness, although neither party had listed him on their witness lists nor subpoenaed him. The ALJ issued a sequestration order against the activist. The activist voluntarily withdrew upon the threat of the attorney seeking a trial court order to enforce the sequestration ruling. The lower courts held that intentional lying to the ALJ to exclude the activist was not privileged conduct. No damages were awarded to the activist, but the township was permanently enjoined from interfering with the activist's attendance at future public hearings and the activist was awarded attorney fees as the prevailing party. The court held that the sequestration motion was a communication within a proceeding that was covered by the litigation privilege afforded to attorneys and parties. In addition, the attorney's role as special counsel for the township at the administrative hearing did not transform him into a municipal policymaker under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the lower court and remanded the case to the trial court for entry of a judgment dismissing the § 1983 complaint and the injunction against the township and its attorney. The court also stated that the activist was not a prevailing party and was, therefore, not entitled to attorney's fees.

State v. Badessa


A-94 September Term 2004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


185 N.J. 303; 885 A.2d 430; 2005 N.J. LEXIS 1338


September 13, 2005, Argued  
November 10, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 373 N.J. Super. 84, 860 A.2d 962 (2004).
State v. Badessa, 373 N.J. Super. 84, 860 A.2d 962, 2004 N.J. Super. LEXIS 403 (N.J. Super. Ct. App. Div., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The trial court denied defendant's motion to suppress based on the claim of an illegal stop, acquitted him of driving while intoxicated, and convicted him of refusal to take a breathalyzer test in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:4-50.4a. Defendant appealed; the law division affirmed, as did the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division. Defendant's petition for certification was granted.


OVERVIEW: The supervisor of a sobriety checkpoint told officers to stop any motorist making a turn within the checkpoint zone; defendant was stopped after making such a turn. His eyes were glassy and his speech was slurred, he failed two out of three psycho-physical sobriety tests, and he said he had two glasses of wine. He refused to take a breathalyzer test. The appellate division held that while police failed to provide adequate warning to motorists that a turn would provide cause for a stop, defendant's refusal to submit to the test was an independent act free of any taint from the invalid stop. The State did not appeal the ruling that the stop of defendant's car was illegal but argued that the refusal offense was so attenuated from the unconstitutional stop that the exclusionary rule did not apply. The supreme court disagreed. The police officer's observations at the scene of the illegal stop were necessary to prove an essential element of the crime of refusal to take a breathalyzer test: that the officer had probable cause to believe defendant drove while intoxicated. Because this evidence had to be suppressed, the State could not prove a violation of the refusal statute.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the appellate division and remanded to the law division for the entry of an order consistent with the instant opinion.

R.M. v. Supreme Court of N.J.


A-89 September Term 2004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


185 N.J. 208; 883 A.2d 369; 2005 N.J. LEXIS 1140


May 2, 2005, Argued  
October 19, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Amended December 22, 2005
Remanded by R.M. v. Supreme Court of N.J., 2007 N.J. LEXIS 330 (N.J., Mar. 26, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division, Mercer County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a client, challenged the decision of appellee, the District XIII Ethics Committee (DEC), that mandated that a grievance filed by the client against appellee attorney had to remain confidential. The client brought suit against the court, the DEC, the Office of Attorney Ethics, and the attorney. The client challenged the constitutionality of N.J. Ct. R. 1:20-9, which mandated confidentiality of a grievance.


OVERVIEW: The client had filed a grievance against the attorney. During the DEC investigation, the attorney admitted specific acts of misconduct in connection with her representation of the client. Pursuant to established procedures, the DEC Chair reviewed the matter and determined that the attorney was eligible for diversion because her ethical violations were minor. Thereafter, the DEC informed the client that, although the attorney had accepted diversion, the matter remained confidential under Rule 1:20-9(a). The client contended that the rule was an impermissible restraint on free speech because it prevented her from making truthful statements about the ethics process, including the fact that she filed a grievance, and unduly suppressed criticism of the system of attorney discipline. The court agreed that Rule 1:20-9 violated the First Amendment because it was not narrowly tailored to serve a compelling interest. As such, the court held that a grievant was permitted to discuss publicly the fact that he or she filed a grievance, the content of that grievance, and the result of the process. The court also applied its holding retroactively to all grievances being processed.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the matter to the Professional Responsibility Rules Committee to draft implementing amendments to Rule 1:20-9.

State v. Franklin


A-64 September Term 2004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


184 N.J. 516; 878 A.2d 757; 2005 N.J. LEXIS 937


March 14, 2005, Argued  
August 2, 2005, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at State v. Abdullah, 184 N.J. 497, 878 A.2d 746, 2005 N.J. LEXIS 939 (2005)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Franklin, 182 N.J. 147, 862 A.2d 56, 2004 N.J. LEXIS 1415 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The New Jersey Appellate Court entered a judgment that affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion for post-conviction relief where a jury convicted defendant of second-degree passion/provocation manslaughter and acquitted him of various gun-related offenses but the trial court nevertheless enhanced defendant's sentence after finding he committed the crime with a handgun, a fact not found by the jury. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of second-degree manslaughter and acquitted him of various gun-related offenses. The trial court found that defendant committed the crime with a handgun. Because that finding made defendant a second-time offender under the Graves Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-6(c),(d) (Graves Act), the trial court imposed on him an extended sentence of 20 years in prison. As a result, he received a sentence twice as long as the maximum 10-year second-degree sentence. Eventually, the appellate court affirmed the denial of his post-conviction relief petition. On appeal, he claimed that the imposition of a sentence beyond the statutory maximum based on judicial factfinding violated his Sixth Amendment jury trial and Fourteenth Amendment due process rights. The state supreme court agreed. It found that the second-offender provision of the Graves Act, which permitted the imposition of the extended term based on judicial factfinding that he committed the crime with a handgun even though the jury did not make that specific finding, violated Apprendi because it allowed the sentencing judge to enhance his sentence based on something other than the required prior conviction.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's judgment that affirmed the denial of defendant's postconviction relief petition was reversed, and the case was remanded to the trial court for resentencing.

State v. Natale


A-82/A-83 September Term 2004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


184 N.J. 458; 878 A.2d 724; 2005 N.J. LEXIS 938


March 14, 2005, Argued  
August 2, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 373 N.J. Super. 226, 861 A.2d 148 (2004).
State v. Abdullah, 184 N.J. 497, 878 A.2d 746, 2005 N.J. LEXIS 939 (2005)
State v. Natale, 373 N.J. Super. 226, 861 A.2d 148, 2004 N.J. Super. LEXIS 407 (App.Div., 2004)



DISPOSITION: The court affirmed the intermediate court's holding of unconstitutionality as to sentencing procedures but reversed its determination that the statutory scheme was altogether unconstitutional, salvaging the statute by eliminating presumptive sentences. It gave its holding "pipeline retroactivity," directing new sentencing hearings, in which only statutory maximums would be considered as limits, as to all affected cases.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted the State's petition and defendant's cross-petition for certiorari after the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, reversed a sentence imposed on defendant convicted of aggravated assault after remand that equaled his originally imposed sentence and held that the sentencing process in which aggravating and mitigating factors were balanced under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:44-1(f)(1) violated the Sixth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: New Jersey's sentencing scheme provided for statutory maximums and minimums but also referenced presumptive sentences, which fell somewhere in between. After defendant was convicted of beating his girlfriend nearly to death, the trial court, following statutory procedures, applied § 2C:44-1's aggravators and mitigators to the presumptive sentence and sentenced defendant to a long term. Since the trial court had based its determination on something other than the statutory maximum, the court held that the intermediate court had properly held the process violative of the Sixth Amendment as articulated in Blakely. Nonetheless, the intermediate court erred in holding the whole scheme unconstitutional when it was possible to salvage the statute by eliminating one provision and thereby effectuate the legislative intent of assuring uniformity in sentencing. The court eliminated presumptive sentences and directed that the maximum statutory sentences should guide courts in applying aggravating and mitigating factors. This new rule was to apply prospectively, to all cases pending appeal, and to any cases in which Blakely concerns had been raised.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the intermediate court's holding of unconstitutionality as to sentencing procedures but reversed its determination that the statutory scheme was altogether unconstitutional, salvaging the statute by eliminating presumptive sentences. It gave its holding "pipeline retroactivity," directing new sentencing hearings, in which only statutory maximums would be considered as limits, as to all affected cases.

State v. Hrycak


A-18 September Term 2004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


184 N.J. 351; 877 A.2d 1209; 2005 N.J. LEXIS 926


May 3, 2005, Argued  
July 20, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Hrycak, 181 N.J. 285, 854 A.2d 919, 2004 N.J. LEXIS 955 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted defendant's petition for certification to reconsider a caselaw decision that dictated the maximum jail sentence a third-time offender under the Driving While Intoxicated (DWI) statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:4-50(a)(3), was subject to when one of the prior convictions was uncounseled. Defendant was convicted for DWI for the third time.


OVERVIEW: With regard to her second conviction, defendant claimed that her first offense was uncounseled and moved to limit any period of incarceration to that of a first-time offender in accordance with the caselaw. The trial court in that case granted her motion and sentenced her as a first-time offender without any period of incarceration. In the present case, defendant moved for sentencing as a second-time offender since she was sentenced as a first-time offender with regard to her second conviction. Defendant was sentenced as a third-time offender to 90 days in jail with 90 days of community service, fines, surcharges, penalty costs, and a 10-year suspension of her driving privileges. The court examined the caselaw at issue and held that, to fulfill the legislative intent, the DWI statute for a third-time offender with one prior uncounseled DWI conviction allowed for the imposition of incarceration no greater than that for a second-time offender, namely 90 days, and allowed for the imposition of the second-time offender requirement of 30 days of community service.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of conviction and remanded defendant's case to the trial court for a determination of whether defendant's first DWI conviction was uncounseled, and if so, the maximum jail sentence was directed not to exceed the maximum jail sentence permitted for a second-time DWI offender.

 

114 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


LaManna v. Proformance Ins.


A-101 September Term 2003


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


184 N.J. 214; 876 A.2d 785; 2005 N.J. LEXIS 819


November 9, 2004, Argued; March 28, 2005, Reargued  
July 14, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 364 N.J. Super. 473, 837 A.2d 384 (2003).
Lamanna v. Proformance Ins. Co., 364 N.J. Super. 473, 837 A.2d 384, 2003 N.J. Super. LEXIS 368 (N.J. Super. Ct. App. Div., 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), upheld a jury verdict against appellant injured party and found N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2B:23-17 was constitutional. It also held that because the injured party did not object to a less than five-sixths vote, she waived her right to argue on appeal that the verdict violated the New Jersey Constitution. The injured party appealed.


OVERVIEW: At issue was the constitutionality of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2B:23-17, which authorized parties in a civil action to stipulate that a smaller majority of jurors than five-sixths could render a verdict. The injured party argued that § 2B:23-17 was unconstitutional because it conflicted with N.J. Const. art. I, para. 9, and acknowledged that while she did not raise that argument below, the argument was raised sua sponte by the dissent in the appellate division. She further alleged the 75 percent jury verdict was plain error within the meaning of N.J. Ct. R. 2:10-2. The instant court disagreed. Both the right to a jury trial and the right to a jury verdict of not less than five-sixths majority were provided for in N.J. Const. art. I, para. 9, and there was no reason to distinguish between the right to waive a jury trial entirely and the right to waive a jury verdict by five-sixths majority. Further, civil parties could waive their constitutional right to at least a five-sixths verdict and agree to accept a smaller percentage verdict. Thus, the injured party's plain error argument was rejected.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate division was affirmed.

 

115 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Cummings


A-65 September Term 2004


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


184 N.J. 84; 875 A.2d 906; 2005 N.J. LEXIS 617


March 15, 2005, Argued  
June 23, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Cummings, 182 N.J. 148, 862 A.2d 56, 2004 N.J. LEXIS 1430 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In municipal court, defendant entered a conditional guilty plea to refusing to submit to a breathalyzer test, in violation of the New Jersey Refusal Statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:4-50.4; he appealed. The superior court, law division, affirmed, as did the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division. Defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: An officer stopped defendant for illegally crossing the center line of traffic. Based on his observations, the officer arrested defendant for driving under the influence of an intoxicant. The officer read to defendant the statement required under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:4-50.2(e) and twice requested that he submit to a breathalyzer test. Defendant twice refused. Defendant argued that the Refusal Statute's preponderance-of-the-evidence burden of proof violated his due process rights and was unconstitutional. The lower courts disagreed, but the supreme court did not. It had previously held that, for double jeopardy purposes, a prosecution under the Refusal Statute was quasi-criminal in nature. It extended that reasoning; in prosecutions under § 39:4-50.2(e), the State had to prove every element of the offense beyond a reasonable doubt. The new rule was to be given "pipeline retroactivity," that is, it applied in the instant case, in future cases, and in any case still on direct appeal at the time the new rule was set forth.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate division was reversed and the matter was remanded the municipal court, where defendant could elect either to withdraw his plea and proceed to trial under the proof beyond a reasonable doubt standard of proof, or to accept his earlier conviction and sentence.

State v. McAllister


A-87 September Term 2003


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


184 N.J. 17; 875 A.2d 866; 2005 N.J. LEXIS 611


January 4, 2004, Argued  
June 20, 2005, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 366 N.J. Super. 251, 840 A.2d 967 (2004).
State v. McAllister, 366 N.J. Super. 251, 840 A.2d 967, 2004 N.J. Super. LEXIS 51 (N.J. Super. Ct. App. Div., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certification to the State's request for further review of a judgment of a panel of the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, that affirmed defendant's theft and forgery convictions but held that harmless error had occurred and N.J. Const. art. I, para. 7 had been violated when defendant's bank records were released to investigators pursuant to a grand jury subpoena without a showing of probable cause.


OVERVIEW: Investigators had already been alerted by a check register found in an inventory search of defendant's purse to the likelihood that her bank records would provide evidence that she had stolen from her elderly employers. Her bank records were obtained by grand jury subpoena for which the prosecutor needed only to show relevance to the investigation. The court agreed with an intermediate court that N.J. Const. art. I, para. 7 was more protective than the Fourth Amendment of financial records in the possession of third parties. Even though New Jersey law recognized that defendant had a reasonable expectation of privacy, the court held that her expectation was adequately protected by the requirement that relevancy (rather than probable cause) support issuance of a subpoena. Although the constitution did not require it, the court recognized that some targets of investigation would not be adequately protected if their bank records could be seized without their knowledge. Since it had supervisory powers over grand juries, it directed the appropriate committee to study whether it would be appropriate to include a requirement that targets be notified of third-party subpoenas.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and the intermediate court's holding that defendant had a reasonable expectation of privacy in her bank records but reversed its holding that a probable cause standard applied, holding instead that grand jury procedures were adequate. Finally, it referred the issue of the desirability of requiring that investigative targets receive notice of third-party subpoenas to its Criminal Practice Committee.

State v. Fuller


A-76 September Term 2002


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


182 N.J. 174; 862 A.2d 1130; 2004 N.J. LEXIS 1523


December 2, 2003, Argued  
December 22, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 356 N.J. Super. 266, 812 A.2d 389 (2002).
State v. Fuller, 356 N.J. Super. 266, 812 A.2d 389, 2002 N.J. Super. LEXIS 514 (App.Div., 2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury in the trial court of first-degree robbery and fourth-degree possession of an imitation firearm for unlawful purposes. The trial court denied defendant's motion for a new trial. Defendant appealed. A divided panel of the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division affirmed the judgment. Defendant appealed as of right.


OVERVIEW: The prosecutor used peremptory challenges to excuse two prospective jurors because one was a minister or missionary and the other was, apparently, Muslim, based on his dress and his name. The prosecutor took the Muslim garb and missionary activity as signs of religious devotion. The prosecutor explained that, in his experience, people who tended to be demonstrative about their religions tended to favor defendants to a greater extent than did persons who were not as religious. The prosecutor argued that he lacked motive to discriminate against particular religious beliefs as evidenced by his having made no effort to learn either the religious affiliations of any potential juror or the religions composition of the jury ultimately empaneled. Rather, he took exhibitions of religious devotion as an indication of lenient tendencies towards the defense, which he argued was a permissible basis for exclusion. The trial court accepted the prosecutor's explanation. On appeal, the court found that the jurors who were excluded were members of a cognizable group, and the prosecutor failed to present sufficient evidence of situation-specific bias to justify the peremptory challenges.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was reversed, and the matter was remanded to the trial court for a new trial.

Related proceeding at In re Mathesius, 188 N.J. 496, 910 A.2d 594, 2006 N.J. LEXIS 1657 (2006)

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Superior Court, Law Division, Mercer County (New Jersey), which denied his petition for post-conviction relief (PCR). Defendant had been convicted of capital murder and sentenced to death, which was affirmed on appeal.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the PCR trial court's statements that defendant would never face the death penalty, and its opinion as to its role in determining the appropriateness of the death sentence, impinged on the necessary meaningful appellate review. Accordingly, the court nullified the PCR court's findings and conclusions, based on support from Caldwell and on N.J. Const. art. I, para. 12. The court undertook a de novo review, finding that there were no credibility issues and that it had original jurisdiction under N.J. Const. art. VI, § 5, para. 3 and N.J. Ct. R. 2:10-5. Counsel's performance in the penalty phase was not ineffective on issues such as mitigating factors and a reporter's contact with jurors. The State's discovery violation did not create manifest injustice. Defendant did not present a bona fide claim of mental retardation, although if he had, the trial court's procedure would have denied his due process rights.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's application for post-conviction relief.

Lance v. McGreevey


A-103 September Term 2003


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


180 N.J. 590; 853 A.2d 856; 2004 N.J. LEXIS 915


July 22, 2004, Argued  
July 26, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Law Division, Mercer County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Law Division, Mercer County (New Jersey), ruling against them where they sought a declaration that the proceeds of an intended bond sale were not revenue pursuant to N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 2, para. 2 and that, absent voter approval, the contract or appropriation debt expected to be generated by bonds was unconstitutional under N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 2, para. 3.


OVERVIEW: New Jersey adopted an annual budget in the form of the New Jersey Appropriations Act of 2004, 2004 N.J. Laws 71, and related legislation for the 2005 fiscal year. Two related measures made available bond proceeds in excess of $ 1.9 billion to be appropriated by the legislature for any lawful purpose including the operating expenditures of state government. The appropriations clause of the New Jersey Constitution, N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 2, para. 2, required the state to have a balanced budget each fiscal year. The issue was whether the appropriations clause allowed the state to rely upon $ 1.9 billion in borrowed monies to fund general expenses in order to balance the budget. The Appropriations Act was contrary to the purpose of the appropriations clause, which barred the state from adopting an annual budget in which expenditures exceeded revenues. Bond proceeds did not constitute state revenue and could not be used for the purpose of funding or balancing any portion of the budget pertaining to general costs without violating the appropriations clause. Because the court's holding would have greatly disrupted the operations of the government, it was given prospective effect only.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, although the holding was to be applied on a prospective basis only.

Am. Trucking Ass'ns v. State


A-15/16 September Term 2003


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


180 N.J. 377; 852 A.2d 142; 2004 N.J. LEXIS 910


February 2, 2000, Argued  
July 19, 2004, Decided  
June 20, 2000, Remanded


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Remanded June 20, 2000. Re-argued November 3, 2003. On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 324 N.J. Super. 1, 734 A.2d 314 (1999).
American Trucking Ass'ns v. State, 324 N.J. Super. 1, 734 A.2d 314, 1999 N.J. Super. LEXIS 207 (N.J. Super. Ct. App. Div., 1999)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs challenged the validity of the hazardous waste transporter registration fee regulation, N.J. Admin. Code § 7:26G-3.3(g), under the Commerce Clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3. The tax court granted partial summary judgment to plaintiffs, and the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) reversed. Plaintiffs and the State challenged the appellate decision, and the State also challenged further proceedings of the tax court.


OVERVIEW: The regulation was promulgated pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 13:1E-18, a provision of New Jersey's Solid Waste Management legislation, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 13:1E-1 to -223. The appellate court had directed the State to apply for a "fairness" ruling from the United States Department of Transportation (USDOT) pursuant to 49 U.S.C.S. § 5125(g), and on appeal by both parties, the court summarily remanded to the tax court for further development of the record. The court agreed with the tax court's conclusion that the fee mechanism violated the Commerce Clause. The court held that (1) nothing in the language of 49 U.S.C.S. § 5125(g) made "unmistakably clear" Congress's intent to remove transport fees from Commerce Clause scrutiny; (2) plaintiffs established that the fee mechanism was structurally discriminatory, but the State failed to make a persuasive showing that the fees were adequately apportioned in a way that bore a sufficiently direct relationship to state services rendered to those subject to the charges; and (3) the transporter fees failed the internal consistency test, as they discriminated against interstate commerce, and they imposed an undue burden on interstate commerce.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment, reinstated the tax court's grant of partial summary judgment to plaintiffs, and remanded to the tax court for further proceedings on reserved issues.

State v. Milne


A-61 September Term 2002


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


178 N.J. 486; 842 A.2d 140; 2004 N.J. LEXIS 127


November 17, 2003, Argued  
March 2, 2004, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus denied Milne v. Burns, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 18429 (D.N.J., Aug. 24, 2005)
Related proceeding at State v. Milne, 2009 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1051 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 355 N.J. Super. 355, 810 A.2d 588 (2002).
State v. Milne, 355 N.J. Super. 355, 810 A.2d 588, 2002 N.J. Super. LEXIS 462 (App.Div., 2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed from a decision of the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, that reversed a trial court's denial as procedurally barred by N.J. Ct. R. 3:22-12(a) of defendant's petition for post-conviction relief from a judgment, entered a decade and a half earlier, convicting defendant of sexually assaulting and murdering a child.


OVERVIEW: At defendant's trial, counsel did not offer a diminished capacity defense because at that time, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:4-2 required that the defense be proven by clear and convincing evidence, and she doubted that such a standard could be met. The standard was later held unconstitutional, and even later, in the Reyes case, the state supreme court held that a claim based on preclusion from raising a diminished capacity defense could be raised retroactively in a post-conviction relief petition. Nonetheless, N.J. Ct. R. 3:22-12(a) set a five-year limitations period for filing. That deadline could be relaxed in cases of excusable neglect or for other compelling reasons, but the high court found no reason for relaxation in defendant's case. He knew he had a possible claim based on diminished capacity for years, yet he waited until more than five years after the Reyes decision to seek relief. When that unreasonable delay was combined with the questionable merit of the underlying claim, the court declined to relax its procedural rules.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the intermediate court and reinstated the order denying the petition.

Caviglia v. Royal Tours of Am.


A-72 September Term 2002


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


178 N.J. 460; 842 A.2d 125; 2004 N.J. LEXIS 123


October 21, 2003, Argued  
February 19, 2004, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 355 N.J. Super. 1, 809 A.2d 146 (2002).
Caviglia v. Royal Tours of Am., 355 N.J. Super. 1, 809 A.2d 146, 2002 N.J. Super. LEXIS 427 (N.J. Super. Ct. App.Div.,2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a bus operator and the bus owner, appealed from an order of the Superior Court, Appellate Division, New Jersey, which affirmed a decision of the trial court, reversing its grant of summary judgment to defendants and reinstating a personal injury and property damage action by plaintiffs, an injured driver and his injured passenger, arising from a vehicle collision. N.J. Stat. Ann. § 39:6A-4.5a was held unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The bus collided with the uninsured driver's car, and plaintiffs sued. The passenger settled her action. Defendants were granted summary judgment by the trial court pursuant to § 39:6A-4.5a. However, the trial court then reversed itself and reinstated the driver's action. In doing so, the trial court held § 39:6A-4.5a unconstitutional as violating the equal protection and due process guarantees of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and N.J. Const. art. I, para. 1. The appellate division affirmed, finding that the absolute bar to recovery of noneconomic damages was arbitrary and irrational. On further appeal, the court reviewed the history of the No Fault Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 39:6A-1 to -35, and found that the limitation on suing was a reasonable attempt by the legislature to have drivers maintain insurance. The court found that there were no constitutional violations by such enactment, as the legislature had the power to create preconditions to filing lawsuits. As to substantive due process, the statute was rationally related to, and suitably furthered, a legitimate state interest. Equal protection was not violated, as disparate treatment of uninsured and insured drivers was justified.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate division and held the statute constitutional, and remanded the matter for further proceedings.

Motion granted by, Clarified by State v. Fortin, 178 N.J. 540, 843 A.2d 974, 2004 N.J. LEXIS 21 (2004)

DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Superior Court, Law Division, Middlesex County (New Jersey), which, in a bifurcated jury trial, convicted him of capital murder and then sentenced him to death under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-3(c). Defendant contended that he was denied a fair trial as a result of various rulings of the trial court in the guilt and penalty phases of the trial.


OVERVIEW: The centerpiece of the State's case was defendant's assault on a state trooper that occurred after the charged crime and had unique similarities to the sexual assault of the victim. OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction and sentence and remanded for further proceedings.

Ferreira v. Rancocas Orthopedic Assocs.


A-151 September Term 2001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


178 N.J. 144; 836 A.2d 779; 2003 N.J. LEXIS 1536


March 3, 2003, Argued; September 22, 2003, Reargued  
November 24, 2003, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Ferreira v. Rancocas Orthopedic Assocs., 174 N.J. 193, 803 A.2d 1164, 2002 N.J. LEXIS 1251 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Certification was granted to plaintiff patient for the appeal of a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed a trial court's order dismissing the patient's medical malpractice action against defendants, a doctor and an orthopedic group.


OVERVIEW: The patient's counsel filed the patient's action and obtained an affidavit of merit as required by the Affidavit of Merit statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2A:53A-26 to -29. Defendants filed an answer. Counsel had the affidavit of merit 10 days after the answer was filed but because of a misfiling of the answer within his office, counsel did not timely serve the affidavit under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2A:53A-27. Defendants' motion to dismiss was filed after they received the untimely affidavit. The trial court dismissed for failure to comply with the Affidavit of Merit statute. The court fashioned an equitable remedy to save the action. The court noted past decisions in which it had allowed dismissals without prejudice where there was substantial compliance with the Affidavit of Merit statute. Inadvertence of counsel to timely file an affidavit was not a circumstance that could entitle the patient to dismissal without prejudice. The key in the patient's case was the fact that although the affidavit was provided outside the statutory time frame, it was provided before the motion to dismiss was filed. In such a case, defendants were to be precluded from filing motions to dismiss.


OUTCOME: The decision of the appellate division was reversed and the matter was remanded for further proceedings.

Sojourner A. v. N.J. Dep't of Human Servs.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff welfare recipients appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment dismissal of the recipients' claims to defendants, the New Jersey Department of Human Services and its Commissioner, in the class action attacking N.J. It was also held that the cap did not improperly single out classes of poor children based on their parents' reproductive choices and the timing of their birth. The court also noted that the Department of Human Services provided ample justification for the family cap.

McNeil v. Legislative Apportionment Comm'n


A-73 September Term 2002


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


177 N.J. 364; 828 A.2d 840; 2003 N.J. LEXIS 865


April 28, 2003, Argued  
July 31, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by McNeil v. Legislative Apportionment Comm'n, 540 U.S. 1107, 157 L.Ed.2d 893, 124 S.Ct. 1068, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 88 (2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at
McNeil v. Legislative Apportionment Comm'n of N.J., 357 N.J.Super. 74, 813 A.2d 1264, 2003 N.J. Super. LEXIS 27 (N.J.Super. Ct.App.Div., 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the Legislative Apportionment Commission of the State of New Jersey, appealed a judgment from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey). The appellate division reversed the judgment of the trial court, which ruled that the Commission was not bound by the restrictions set forth in N.J. Const. art. IV, § 2, para. 3 when redrawing Senate and Assembly districts and apportioning Senators and Assembly persons among those districts.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff political party members filed several lawsuits, including one in which they alleged that the Commission's plan violated art. IV, § 2, para. 3 because it carved Newark and Jersey City each into three legislative districts. The trial court granted summary judgment for the Commission, and the appellate division reversed. The court reinstated the trial court's judgment. The political boundary requirement of art. IV, § 2, para. 3 with respect to Newark and Jersey City could not be enforced without violating the Supremacy Clause. To adhere to the one-person, one-vote doctrine, the boundaries of larger municipalities would have to be breached at times, and the Commission properly divided the two cities into more than two districts each to comply with federal law. In addition, "unpacking," or distributing the voting strength of minority groups into majority districts and coalition districts, was a tool of legislative apportionment that satisfied the mandate of the Voting Rights Act (VRA), 42 U.S.C.S. § 1973. In contrast, creating only two districts each would have constituted "packing," or the dilution of racial minority group voting strength, in violation of § 1973.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate division and reinstated the judgment of the trial court.

 

128 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Moriarty v. Bradt


A-145 September Term 2001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


177 N.J. 84; 827 A.2d 203; 2003 N.J. LEXIS 699


January 21, 2003, Argued  
July 14, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Moriarty v. Bradt, 540 U.S. 1177, 124 S. Ct. 1408, 158 L. Ed. 2d 78, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 1047 (U.S., Feb. 23, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
Moriarity v. Bradt, 174 N.J. 189, 803 A.2d 1161, 2002 N.J. LEXIS 1119 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted certification to an appeal by intervenor grandparents from a Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), reversal of a trial court's grant of visitation to them after plaintiff father and defendant mother were divorced, the mother died, and the father sought to limit previous visitation arrangements. The father challenged the constitutionality of the New Jersey Grandparents Visitation Statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 9.2-7.1.


OVERVIEW: After their parents divorced and remarried, the children continued to enjoy frequent visitation with their maternal grandparents. After the mother died of a drug overdose, the father, who blamed the grandparents for the mother's problems, sought to greatly limit their visitation. The grandparents sued under the Statute. After noting that in the Troxel case another state's grandparent visitation statute had been invalidated on substantive due process grounds for unduly interfering with parents' rights to raise their children, the high court held that if properly interpreted, to require an initial finding based on the preponderance of the evidence that the children would be harmed if grandparental visitation were not permitted, before any consideration of the children's best interests occurred, the New Jersey statute would pass constitutional muster. In the case before it, the court held that the trial court had in fact analyzed the situation according to this approach, and found that continued visitation was the one way the children would be able to stay in touch with the memory of their mother and with her family. Therefore, the statute had been properly applied.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the intermediate appellate court and reinstated the visitation order entered by the trial court.

Joye v. Hunterdon Cent. Reg'l High Sch. Bd. of Educ.


A-27 September Term 2002


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


176 N.J. 568; 826 A.2d 624; 2003 N.J. LEXIS 687


February 19, 2003, Argued  
July 9, 2003, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at
Joye v. Hunterdon Cent. Reg'l High Sch. Bd. of Educ., 353 N.J. Super. 600, 803 A.2d 706, 2002 N.J. Super. LEXIS 384 (N.J. Super. Ct. App. Div., 2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, three sets of parents on behalf of themselves and their minor children, appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed the trial court and upheld the constitutionality of a random drug and alcohol testing program by defendants, a high school board of education and the Superintendent of Schools.


OVERVIEW: The testing program applied to all students who participated in athletic and non-athletic extracurricular activities, as well as those who possessed school parking permits. The parents contended that the testing constituted a violation of their children's right to privacy. The trial court invalidated the program, finding that it violated the prohibition against unreasonable searches and seizures under N.J. Const. art. I, para. 7. On appeal, the appellate division reversed that judgment and upheld the program. On further appeal, the court initially noted that it was clearly constitutional under the U.S. Constitution and accordingly, it was analyzed under the special-needs framework within the N.J. Constitution. The court found the program constitutional, noting that it was reasonable. The court stated that the students' expectations of privacy were reduced in a public-school setting; the degree of obtrusiveness was minimal because the manner in which the urine testing was done limited the intrusion on students' privacy and protected their personal dignity; and the need for conducting the testing in order to attempt to reduce the major drug problem in schools was strong.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the Appellate Division.

Warren County Cmty. College v. Warren County Bd. of Chosen Freeholders


A-121/122 September Term 2001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


176 N.J. 432; 824 A.2d 1073; 2003 N.J. LEXIS 667


January 22, 2003, Argued  
June 19, 2003, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at
Warren County Cmty. College v. Warren County Bd. of Chosen Freeholders (In re Warren County Bd. of Chosen Freeholders Charged with Contempt), 350 N.J. Super. 489, 796 A.2d 257, 2002 N.J. Super. LEXIS 85 (N.J. Super. Ct. App. Div., 2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff college appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed the trial court order that directed defendant county board of freeholders (CBF) to issue bonds in order to construct a college facility. The appellate court also held that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 18A:64A-18 was unconstitutional, and it invalidated contempt orders against various members of the CBF.


OVERVIEW: The CBF changed the status of a county community college agency into a county college with the assistance and advice of the Board of Higher Education (BHE). However, based on the BHE's view that the conversion was not the creation of a new entity, compliance with N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 18A:64A-2 to -6 was not had. The college's board of school estimate (BSE) certified an amount necessary for construction of a building to the CBF. In an action by the college due to the CBF's failure to fund, the CBF was ordered to appropriate the funds. On appeal by the CBF, the appellate division held that the college status was not properly statutorily achieved, and accordingly, there was no authority to compel appropriation of funds. The appellate court held N.J. Stat. Ann. § 18A:64A-18 unconstitutional as applied in the instant case because the county citizens had not agreed to subject themselves to such appropriations, which was a violation of popular representation under N.J. Const. art. IV, § 6, para. 1. On further review, the court agreed with the appellate court's reasoning, but found that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 18A:64A-18 was constitutional as long as there was an opportunity for public input.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court, but modified it to the extent of holding that the statute at issue herein was constitutional.

State v. Stanton


A-7 September Term 2001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


176 N.J. 75; 820 A.2d 637; 2003 N.J. LEXIS 458


January 3, 2002, Argued; November 18, 2002, Reargued  
April 17, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by STANTON, TRACI E. v. NEW JERSEY, 540 U.S. 903, 124 S. Ct. 259, 157 L. Ed. 2d 187, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 6013 (U.S., Oct. 6, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 339 N.J. Super. 1, 770 A.2d 1198 (2001).
State v. Stanton, 339 N.J. Super. 1, 770 A.2d 1198, 2001 N.J. Super. LEXIS 136 (N.J. Super. Ct. App.Div.2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State of New Jersey sought review by certification from a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which reversed the trial court's imposition of a three-year parole disqualifier on defendant's conviction for second-degree vehicular homicide in violation of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-5b, which was based on defendant's reckless operation of her motor vehicle.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was driving after admittedly drinking beer, lost control on a curve in the road, and struck a tree. Defendant's passenger died as a result of the accident. She was indicted for second-degree vehicular homicide and was charged with various motor vehicle offenses. A jury found her guilty of the charge and the trial judge found her guilty of all the motor vehicle offenses except that of driving while intoxicated (DWI), which was reserved until sentencing. At that time, the judge found defendant guilty of DWI and sentenced her on the homicide charge as if it were a third-degree crime, imposing a three-year parole ineligibility term pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:11-5b(1) based on the intoxication. On appeal, the appellate division held that the intoxication finding should have been made by the jury and although the conviction stood, the parole disqualifier was vacated. On certification by the State, the court held that intoxication was not an element of the crime, and accordingly a finding on that by the judge was sufficient for sentence enhancement. Although the charges were consolidated to avoid a double jeopardy problem, that did not change the jury right on each charge.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate division's judgment and ordered that the trial court's judgment was to be reinstated.

In re Police Sergeant (PM3776V)


A-138 September Term 2001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


176 N.J. 49; 819 A.2d 1173; 2003 N.J. LEXIS 343


February 3, 2003, Argued  
April 16, 2003, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner promotional consideration candidates sought review of an order from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed the determinations of the New Jersey Department of Personnel (DOP), an administrative law judge, and respondent Merit System Board, all holding that make-up civil service exams did not violate the New Jersey Constitution as conducted.


OVERVIEW: The DOP administered a civil service promotional exam for police sergeant positions which was taken by petitioner candidates. They contended that after the exam, candidates discussed the questions and answers, typed out almost all of them, and disseminated that information throughout the police department. They claimed that candidates who took the make-up exam accordingly had an unfair advantage due to the breach of security, which violated N.J. Const. art. VII, § 1, para. 2. The Board rejected the constitutional challenge and on further review of the factual allegations relating to promoted candidates' knowledge due to their having taken make-up tests, the administrative law judge found no proof that they had access to the original examination. The appellate court affirmed the decision of the Board and on appeal by certification the court found no constitutional infirmity with the testing procedure itself. However, it found that the way that the make-up tests were administered was unfair and not competitive. Accordingly, those candidates who had been promoted and had taken the make-up exam were removed because their test results were held invalid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the finding that the testing practice was not per se unconstitutional. However, it found that administration of the make-up exams violated petitioner candidates' right to a fair and competitive examination and accordingly, remanded for appropriate relief, including removal of those candidates who had been promoted due to having scored high on make-up exams.

 

133 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Lonegan v. State


A-15 September Term 2002


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


176 N.J. 2; 819 A.2d 395; 2003 N.J. LEXIS 337


October 21, 2002, Argued  
April 9, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended June 23, 2003.
Related proceeding at Lonegan v. Corzine, 2009 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2763 (App.Div., Nov. 5, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at .
Lonegan v. State, 341 N.J. Super. 465, 775 A.2d 586, 2001 N.J. Super. LEXIS 266 (App.Div., 2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, private citizens and the group to which they belonged, appealed from a decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), that affirmed a summary judgment in favor of defendants, the State and various independent state authorities, declaring that New Jersey statutes authorizing contract bond financing did not violate the Debt Limitation Clause of N.J. Const. art. VIII, § 2, cl. 3.


OVERVIEW: The Clause placed strict limits on the extent to which the State could pledge its full faith and credit in support of general obligation bonds. Over many decades, numerous legislative schemes financed particular projects by authorizing independent state authorities to contract with New Jersey's treasurer to issue bonds with the proviso that any payment was subject to legislative appropriations. The citizens group attacked all such legislation as an effort to evade the limits of the Clause by issuing bonds that did not legally obligate the State, but that in fact did bind the State, in that it could not afford potential harm to its bond ratings. The high court surveyed case law from the many jurisdictions that had experienced similar challenges, and determined to remain aligned with the majority of states that had found contract bonds not violative of debt limitation clauses. This approach to New Jersey's Clause had been taken in the case law for too long to be disturbed--with consequent economic disruption--without action by the legislature. Therefore, if the citizens group wanted change, it would have to address the legislature.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Lonegan v. State


A-23 September Term 2001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


174 N.J. 435; 809 A.2d 91; 2002 N.J. LEXIS 1261


January 2, 2002, Argued  
August 21, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion denied by, Motion granted by Lonegan v. State, 174 N.J. 354, 807 A.2d 189, 2002 N.J. LEXIS 1293 (2002)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 341 N.J.Super. 465, 775 A.2d 586 (2001).
 Lonegan v. State, 341 N.J.Super. 465, 775 A.2d 586, 2001 N.J.Super. LEXIS 266 (N.J.Super.Ct.App.Div.2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, interest group and individual, sued respondents, the State of New Jersey, its treasurer, and several state agencies, seeking injunctive relief and a declaratory judgment that certain statutes authorizing contract bond financing violated N.J. Const. art. VIII, § II, para. 3 (the Debt Limitation Clause). The Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), granted summary judgment to respondents on all claims. Appellants sought review.


OVERVIEW: Appellants challenged the constitutionality of New Jersey's use of contract debt without voter approval, arguing that the legislature would always make the necessary appropriations to prevent a default. The supreme court had repeatedly rejected challenges under the Debt Limitation Clause to contract bonds issued by independent authorities when the payment on the bonds was made subject to future legislative appropriations. Appellants urged the supreme court to reconsider its precedents that had sustained contract debt or debt subject to future appropriations. The supreme court was open to the suggestion, but required further briefing and argument. It agreed with the appellate division that the Education Facilities Construction and Financing Act (EFCFA), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 18A:7G-1 to -44, was constitutional, not only because of supreme court precedent, but because the EFCFA was enacted in furtherance of the mandate of N.J. Const. art. VIII, § IV, para. 1 (the Education Provision), which authorized state-backed school bonds without reference to the Debt Limitation Clause.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate division upholding the EFCFA was affirmed. Appellants' general challenge to contract debt was set down for reargument.

State v. Difrisco


A-30 September Term 2001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


174 N.J. 195; 804 A.2d 507; 2002 N.J. LEXIS 1110


March 26, 2002, Argued  
August 14, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief granted at, Remanded by State v. DiFrisco, 2006 N.J. LEXIS 1070 (N.J., July 5, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County.
State v. DiFrisco, 142 N.J. 148, 662 A.2d 442, 1995 N.J. LEXIS 526 (1995)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of capital murder and received a death sentence. In an earlier appeal, the conviction and sentence were upheld. Defendant then filed a petition for post-conviction relief, which was denied by the Superior Court, Law Division, Essex County (New Jersey). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that he was denied effective assistance of counsel in the penalty phase, and that the trial court erred in excluding expert testimony, refusing to consider the opinion of a capital case expert, and denying his motion to interview jurors. The appellate court ruled that a capital defendant needed to establish prejudice by showing that, but for counsel's errors, the penalty-phase deliberations would have been affected substantially. Reasonable probability was a probability sufficient to undermine confidence in the result. The appellate court found that defendant failed to show that his counsel was ineffective in several areas he raised, including, inter alia, presentation of evidence of his remorse. The record showed that defendant's confession was voluntary. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in excluding expert testimony. The trial court did err when it refused to consider the opinion of a capital case expert, but the error was harmless. Calling back jurors for interrogation was an extraordinary procedure to be invoked only on a strong showing that a litigant may have been harmed by jury misconduct, and defendant failed to show such misconduct.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

In re Fanelli

The physician's wife handled all financial matters for the practice, and, purportedly while suffering from mental illness, she dissipated the plan's assets, allegedly without the physician's knowledge.
