Ann. § 52:14B-11, the physician had a right to a plenary hearing, at which he would be allowed to present evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of affirmance and remanded the matter to the administrative board for a plenary hearing.

OVERVIEW: New Jersey's Mount Laurel line of cases obliged New Jersey municipalities to provide, in their land use planning, for housing for a range of income groups. Eventually, when municipalities remained still resistant, and the high court devised a builder's remedy as an incentive for builders to pursue Mount Laurel violators, the legislature enacted the New Jersey Fair Housing Law, N.J. After a resolution of its original Mount Laurel lawsuit, a township let its certification as compliant lapse, without building the units of affordable housing to which it committed. The developer sued the town again, arguing that, by not authorizing the building of modest one-family detached dwellings, the town assured that little affordable housing would be built at all and that the township's expensive sewerage requirements also made development unlikely. The high court held that it was proper to consider market demand in such circumstances, and that substantial evidence indicated that the actions in question were taken to prevent development of affordable housing.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Camden County.
State v. Nelson, 155 N.J. 487, 715 A.2d 281, 1998 N.J. LEXIS 647 (1998)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who previously pled guilty to two counts of murder and related offenses, appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court, Law Division, Camden County (New Jersey), after a second penalty-phase jury trial, sentencing her to death for one of the murders.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a transsexual person with a history of psychological problems, killed two police officers who were investigating her possible commission of another offense. OUTCOME: The court left defendant's life sentence in place, but vacated the death sentence, and remanded the matter for further consistent proceedings.

State v. Neulander


A-67 September Term 2001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


173 N.J. 193; 801 A.2d 255; 2002 N.J. LEXIS 1074; 30 Media L. Rep. 2281


March 25, 2002, Argued  
July 18, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Sub nomine at Phila. Newspapers, Inc. v. New Jersey, 537 U.S. 1192, 123 S. Ct. 1281, 154 L. Ed. 2d 1027, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 1178 (2003)
Subsequent appeal at State v. Neulander, 2006 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2278 (App.Div., Dec. 28, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.
State v. Neulander, 171 N.J. 332, 793 A.2d 713, 2002 N.J. LEXIS 149 (2002)



DISPOSITION: Modified and affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), the court granted the motion of intervenor newspaper for leave to appeal from an opinion affirming interlocutory orders. At issue was the power of a court, following a hung jury in a capital murder trial that would result in defendant's imminent retrial for capital murder, to prohibit representatives of the press from attempting to conduct juror interviews after a mistrial.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court vacated the contested order to the extent that it restrained the use of juror identification information that was part of the public record of the proceedings but expanded the trial court's restraint to include juror-initiated interviews. Unlike other cases in which juror identities were anonymous during trial in order to insulate jurors from corrupting influences or to protect them from harassment and intimidation, the names of the jurors at issue were publicly identified throughout the voir dire proceeding. However, the potential for affording the prosecution an inappropriate advantage in a retrial could have been increased if media interviews of members of the hung jury revealed heretofore undisclosed details about evidentiary facts that were pivotal to the jury's deliberations. The restriction on post-verdict interviews of the hung jury imposed expired on conclusion of the retrial and return of a verdict, and properly addressed the unique concern presented that media interviews of jurors should not encroach on defendant's Sixth Amendment right to a fair trial.


OUTCOME: The supreme court modified and affirmed the order.

State v. Josephs


A-113 September Term 1999


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


174 N.J. 44; 803 A.2d 1074; 2002 N.J. LEXIS 1070


October 23, 2001, Argued  
July 15, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at State v. Josephs, 2006 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2356 (App.Div., June 26, 2006)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Josephs, 2009 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 355 (App.Div., Feb. 23, 2009)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Josephs v. Kirkpatrick, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 15594 (D.N.J., Feb. 19, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Law Division, Camden County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought direct review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Law Division, Camden County (New Jersey), convicting him of purposeful or knowing murder by his own conduct and related offenses, and sentencing him to death, after a separate proceeding before a different jury.


OVERVIEW: Circumstantial evidence strongly indicated that defendant and another had killed several fellow drug dealers. The high court held that the evidence was legally sufficient to support the determination of guilt, but that the trial court had impermissibly prejudiced defendant by directing the guilt-phase jury to determine whether the murder occurred through defendant's own conduct before it considered whether he was guilty as an accomplice rather than a principal. This error required reversal of the death-eligibility determination. The high court found no plain error in any of the guilt phase jury instructions, and that the evidence did not support defendant's requests for instructions on self-defense and manslaughter. It found one serious error in penalty phase voir dire, a suggestion that jurors had less than total responsibility for their death penalty decisions, and that jurors were clearly confused regarding the unanimity requirement for aggravating factors. The elements of capital murder had to be found again at the penalty phase. Finally, the court upheld the death penalty statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:3-11c, against a challenge based on evolving standards of decency.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed all of defendant's convictions, but reversed the determination that the murders occurred by defendant's own conduct and the death sentence imposed thereon. If the State still wished to pursue a death sentence, a new penalty phase would have to be held, in accordance with the court's instructions. Finally, the court upheld the constitutionality of New Jersey's death penalty statute.

In re Commitment of W.Z.


A-17 September Term 2001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


173 N.J. 109; 801 A.2d 205; 2002 N.J. LEXIS 905


January 28, 2002, Argued  
July 11, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 339 N.J. Super. 549, 773 A.2d 97 (2001).
In re R.S., 173 N.J. 134, 801 A.2d 219, 2002 N.J. LEXIS 906 (2002)
In re Commitment of W.Z., 339 N.J. Super. 549, 773 A.2d 97, 2001 N.J. Super. LEXIS 172 (App.Div., 2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the trial court found that defendant, a thrice-convicted sex offender, was a sexually violent predator under the Sexually Violent Predator Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 30:4-27.24 to -27.38, and ordered his civil commitment, he sought review, challenging the Act's constitutionality. The Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), affirmed. Defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The experts agreed that defendant did not suffer from a paraphilia or a sexual compulsion, but his antisocial personality disorder, along with his alcohol abuse and intermittent explosive disorder, predisposed him to commit acts of sexual violence and made it likely he would reoffend. He argued that the Act's "likely to engage in acts of sexual violence" standard did not comply with the Crane rule, under which only those persons who had "serious difficulty in controlling behavior" could be committed. The state supreme court disagreed. The Act did not violate substantive due process as long as the State proved by clear and convincing evidence that the person had serious difficulty controlling his or her harmful sexual behavior such that it was highly likely that he or she would reoffend. Because this standard had not previously been enunciated, a remand to the trial court for a determination of whether defendant's mental condition justified his commitment under the Act was necessary. The supreme court rejected the contention that actuarial instruments used to assess a sex offender's risk of reoffense were unreliable.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate division finding the Act constitutional was affirmed, but the case was remanded to the trial court for a determination of whether defendant's mental condition caused the required degree of inability to control sexually violent behavior to justify his commitment under the Act.

McKelvey v. Pierce


A-48 September Term 2001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


173 N.J. 26; 800 A.2d 840; 2002 N.J. LEXIS 904


February 26, 2002, Argued  
July 10, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 342 N.J. Super. 399, 776 A.2d 903 (2001).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff former seminarian appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), affirming the trial court's dismissal, for lack of jurisdiction, of his action against defendants, archdiocese and various individuals, based in contract and tort, in which he argued that he had been driven from the seminary by acts of sexual harassment.


OVERVIEW: The former seminarian's college and seminary education was paid for in large part by the archdiocese in which he had hoped to serve. Discouraged by homosexual advances made to him by fellow seminarians and priests, and by hostile reactions of other supervising priests after his unsuccessful complaints became known, the seminarian withdrew, and was then billed by the archdiocese for educational expenses. He responded by suing the archdiocese and certain priests, not for federal civil rights violations, as some had done, but for breach of implied contract and tortious interference with his vocation. The high court held that despite the ministerial exception under the Establishment Clause of the First Amendment, the trial court should not have assumed that it lacked jurisdiction to hear the seminarian's claims without examining each one to determine whether it could be heard without excessive entanglement in religious affairs. It suggested, without deciding, that the seminarian's claims for breach of an implied covenant of good faith and fair dealing and for violation of a fiduciary relationship were among those that might survive constitutional analysis.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded the matter to the trial court for detailed analysis of the former seminarian's various claims.

 

143 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Parolin


A-6 September Term 2001


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


171 N.J. 223; 793 A.2d 638; 2002 N.J. LEXIS 352


January 15, 2002, Argued  
March 27, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 339 N.J. Super. 10, 770 A.2d 1204 (2001).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), affirming a trial court's determination that defendant who pled guilty to fourth-degree aggravated assault and second-degree weapon possession was ineligible for sentencing under the New Jersey No Early Release Act, specifically N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-7.2.


OVERVIEW: At his plea entry, defendant admitted having pointed a loaded gun at his former girlfriend in a purported effort to scare her away. The sentencing court held that although the facts admitted to would support a sentence under the version of New Jersey's No Early Release Act, specifically N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-7.2 then in effect, because the conduct involved was a violent crime, it believed that it could not impose an enhanced sentence because possession of a weapon for an illegal purpose, to which defendant pled guilty, had been held not an offense covered by the statute. The high court distinguished previous cases on the facts, and held that the instant defendant's offense fell within the statute's reach. Although the statute had later been amended to list covered offenses, and they did not include weapon possession, the high court held that the change applied prospectively only.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the intermediate court's judgment and remanded to the trial court for merger of defendant's convictions and imposition of an enhanced sentence under the statute.

Abbott ex rel. Abbott v. Burke


M-1131 September Term 2000


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


170 N.J. 537; 790 A.2d 842; 2002 N.J. LEXIS 52


September 25, 2001, Argued  
February 21, 2002, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at, Remanded by In re 1999-2000 Abbott v. Burke Implementing Regulations, 348 N.J. Super. 382, 792 A.2d 412, 2002 N.J. Super. LEXIS 96 (App.Div., 2002)


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] On motion in aid of litigants' rights from the judgments of this Court, whose opinions are reported at 153 N.J. 480, 710 A.2d 450 (1998) and 163 N.J. 95, 748 A.2d 82 (2000).
Abbott by Abbott v. Burke, 163 N.J. 95, 748 A.2d 82, 2000 N.J. LEXIS 141 (2000)
Abbott v. Burke, 164 N.J. 84, 751 A.2d 1032, 2000 N.J. LEXIS 532 (2000)
Abbott by Abbott v. Burke, 153 N.J. 480, 710 A.2d 450, 1998 N.J. LEXIS 451 (1998)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff advocacy group and individual parents on behalf of their children moved for relief in aid of litigants' rights announced by the instant court in five earlier decisions regarding the obligations of defendant public officials to effectuate New Jersey's statutorily mandated preschool programs for certain districts.


OVERVIEW: For several years, beginning with the first of several decisions in the ongoing Abbott litigation, an educational advocacy group pursued its goal, on behalf of disadvantaged New Jersey parents and children, of assuring that state agencies and officials fulfilled their obligations under the state constitution, statutes, regulations, and court decisions, to assure that local school districts had the resources to offer quality preschool experiences to all interested families. The court had previously issued numerous orders and timetables, but the advocacy group argued that funding and other progress was too slow, that sufficient funds had still not been made available, and that appointment of a standing master to monitor progress was required. The court reiterated its commitment to timely compliance, but held that under the circumstances, a standing master would be a less effective means of assisting the school districts than effective pursuit of administrative and, only when appropriate, judicial remedies.


OUTCOME: The court reiterated its commitment to enforcement of timetables for decision making by officials and any necessary reviewing courts, as announced in an earlier order, but denied the motion to the extent that it requested appointment of a standing master.

 

145 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Papasavvas


A-2 September Term 2000


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


170 N.J. 462; 790 A.2d 798; 2002 N.J. LEXIS 51


October 24, 2000, Argued  
February 14, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On proportionality review of a death sentence imposed in the Superior Court, Law Division, Middlesex County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a death sentence imposed in the Superior Court, Law Division, Middlesex County (New Jersey), and requested a proportionality review by the Supreme Court of New Jersey.


OVERVIEW: Defendant ran from police and entered the victim's house. The victim was an elderly woman who lived alone. He was surprised by her, asphyxiated her, sexually abused her, and threw her down the stairs, causing her death. After his conviction and sentence were affirmed, he requested a proportionality review. The state supreme court assigned his case to the murder committed during a robbery in a home category. It considered defendant's criminal history, his mental condition, and abuse as a child. It determined that his moral blameworthiness was moderate. Although terrible, the victim's injuries were not among the most egregious. Defendant expressed remorse, which indicated that he had the potential for rehabilitation. After reviewing 19 comparison cases, the state supreme court determined that defendant's death penalty was disproportionate.


OUTCOME: The death sentence was vacated and remanded for resentencing.

 

146 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Miller


A-94 September Term 2000


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


170 N.J. 417; 790 A.2d 144; 2002 N.J. LEXIS 4


October 10, 2001, Argued  
January 23, 2002, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with instructions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury convicted defendant of possession of cocaine. The trial court had admitted into evidence, over defendant's objections, a laboratory certificate without the presence of the laboratory employee. The Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) affirmed. Certification was granted, and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: A police lab found that an item seized from defendant was cocaine, and issued a lab certificate saying that. The State notified defense counsel that it intended to proffer the certificate at trial in place of a witness, pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-19c. Defendant filed a timely notice of objection, saying that "the composition, quality and quantity of the substance submitted to the lab for analysis will be contested at trial." The intermediate appellate court affirmed, holding that absent a demonstration of specific grounds for objection showing that there was a bona fide challenge to the composition, quality or quantity of the substance, the certificate was to be admitted. The appellate court held that the language of N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-19 was satisfied if an accused timely objected to the certificate and asserted that the lab results would be contested. As it was the State and not defendant that bore the burden of justification, imposition of a barrier, beyond notice, to defendant's right to confrontation was not to be countenanced. A defendant's objection, under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:35-19, was a vehicle for notice that was quite general.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed. The case was remanded for a hearing where the State bore the burden of proving the reliability of the methodology underlying the report. If the trial court determined that the lab employee should have been produced, the judgment affirming the conviction was to be reversed and a new trial ordered. If the trial court ruled that the report was properly admitted, the judgment was to be affirmed.

BTD-1996 NPC 1 L.L.C. v. 350 Warren L.P.


A-62 September Term 2000


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


170 N.J. 90; 784 A.2d 1214; 2001 N.J. LEXIS 1278


September 17, 2001, Argued  
November 14, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at BTD-1996, NPC 1, 333 N.J.Super 476, 755 A.2d 1236 (2000).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sheriff instituted proceedings through an order to show cause to compel payment under N.J. Stat. Ann. § 22A:4-8 for services in respondent creditor's canceled foreclosure proceeding. The trial court dismissed the sheriff's application, and the Appellate Division (New Jersey) affirmed that judgment for different reasons. Appellant challenged that judgment, and certification was granted.


OVERVIEW: The reviewing court held that the record was clearly inadequate to support the trial court's conclusion that the charge authorized by § 22A:4-8 was intended primarily to raise revenue, and not to compensate the governmental entity for the cost of providing the service. Absent any procedural challenge to the statute's enactment, the reviewing court disagreed with the holding of the appellate court that the statute, if determined to be a tax, was unenforceable absent a legislative intent to impose a tax.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's judgment was reversed.

 

148 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


J.B. v. M.B.


A-9 September Term 2000


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


170 N.J. 9; 783 A.2d 707; 2001 N.J. LEXIS 955


February 26, 2001, Argued  
August 14, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 331 N.J. Super. 223, 751 A.2d 613 (2000).
J.B. v. M.B., 331 N.J. Super. 223, 751 A.2d 613, 2000 N.J. Super. LEXIS 216 (N.J. Super. Ct. App. Div., 2000)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The parties' judgment of divorce resolved all issues except the disposition of seven remaining preembryos that were in storage. Both parties moved for summary judgment, and the trial court granted plaintiff wife's motion. The Appellate Division (New Jersey) affirmed. The state supreme court granted certification.


OVERVIEW: The cryopreserved preembryos were created in connection with an in vitro fertilization procedure which resulted in the birth of the parties' daughter. Defendant husband contended that the wife had stated her intention to either use or donate the preembryos. The state supreme court held that the parties never entered into a separate binding contract providing for the disposition of the preembryos. The state supreme court reasoned that the husband's right to procreate was not lost if he was denied the opportunity to use or donate the preembryos, and it would not force the wife to become a biological parent, with all its attendant consequences, against her will. It was represented at oral argument, however, that the wife did not object to the continued storage of the preembryos.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was modified such that, if the husband wished to pay any fees associated with the continued storage of the preembryos, he had to inform the trial court whether he would do so. Otherwise, the preembryos were to be destroyed. The judgment was affirmed as modified.

 

149 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Borough of Princeton v. Bd. of Chosen Freeholders of Mercer


A-42/A-43/A-44 September Term 2000


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


169 N.J. 135; 777 A.2d 19; 2001 N.J. LEXIS 809


March 12, 2001, Argued  
July 23, 2001, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Waste Mgmt. of N.J., Inc. v. Mercer County Improvement Auth., 2014 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2714 (App.Div., Nov. 18, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 333 N.J. Super. 310, 755 A.2d 637 (2000).
Borough of Princeton v. Board of Chosen Freeholders, 333 N.J. Super. 310, 755 A.2d 637, 2000 N.J. Super. LEXIS 312 (App.Div., 2000)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, city and company, filed a complaint in lieu of prerogative writs against defendants, counties, alleging that garbage disposal contracts were invalid as they were not bid publicly, Local Public Contracts Law (LPCL), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40A:11-1 to -50. The trial court transferred the case. The Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) held the bidding requirements applied. The counties and an intervenor appealed.


OVERVIEW: The counties argued that public bidding was not required, as the contracts granted the counties easement rights on landfill space. The New Jersey Supreme Court held that for purposes of the LPCL, the contracts were service contracts and not contracts for real property or any interest therein. Although the counties retained interests in the property, the agreements were not merely contracts for the acquisition of property rights. They dealt predominantly with solid waste disposal services, both in terms of the obligations undertaken by an intervenor, a waste management company, and the compensation provided in return by the respective counties. The terminology in the contracts granting the counties a license and easement over the landfill did not alter the overriding purpose of the agreements. Therefore, the contracts were subject to public bidding under the LPCL. The supreme court held that the holding concerning the contracts should be applied retrospectively. Although the plaintiffs engaged in significant delay in bringing the action, the prospect of prejudice to the public, combined with the duration of the contracts, required re-bidding of the contracts.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed and remanded to the trial court with instructions to set appropriate deadlines for re-bidding of the solid waste disposal contracts.

Roman Check Cashing v. N.J. Dep't of Banking & Ins.


A-62 September Term 1999


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


169 N.J. 105; 777 A.2d 1; 2001 N.J. LEXIS 806


September 25, 2000, Argued  
July 18, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 324 N.J. Super. 58, 734 A.2d 346 (1999).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to N.J. Ct. R. 2:2-1(a)(1), appellant Department of Banking and Insurance challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division, which held that the distance requirement contained within the New Jersey Check Cashers Regulatory Act of 1993 (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 17:15A-30 to -52, constituted a violation of substantive due process and was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The applicant filed an application for a check cashing license from the department. Later, when the department had not yet rendered a licensing decision, the applicant brought a mandamus proceeding to compel the department to take action on the license, and to obtain a ruling declaring the distance requirement, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 17:15A-41(e), unconstitutional. When the application was denied because of location, the matter was removed to the appellate court as an appeal from a final agency decision. The appellate court held the distance requirement was unconstitutional. The application was approved pending the outcome of the appeal. On appeal, the applicant argued that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 17:15A-41(e) violated its substantive due process rights. The supreme court determined the record demonstrated that the legislature's enactment of the 2,500 foot distance requirement, the means chosen, was rationally related to maintaining the statutory fee cap, a legitimate state objective. In addition, the supreme court found that the distance requirement was rationally related to the health and stability of the industry and the supreme court could not say the choice was arbitrary.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment of the appellate court, rejected the applicant's constitutional challenge, and sustained the validity of the applicant's check cashing license.

 

151 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


In re Registrant J.G.


A-18 September Term 2000


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


169 N.J. 304; 777 A.2d 891; 2001 N.J. LEXIS 805


January 30, 2001, Argued  
July 17, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a petition for certification challenging: (1) the judgment of the Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division, which classified defendant as a Tier 2 sex offender; (2) the application of the Registration and Community Notification Law, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:7-1 to 7-11, also known as Megan's Law, to defendant; and (3) the constitutionality of Megan's Law as applied to defendant. The supreme court granted certification.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a juvenile who pled guilty to conduct that, if committed by an adult, would constitute a sexual assault. The trial court accepted the State's contention that based on defendant's Tier 2 classification, Megan's Law notification should be provided. The appellate court affirmed defendant's classification but limited the notification requirement. On appeal, defendant asserted that Megan's Law should not apply to him because he was only 10-years-old when the underlying offense occurred. Based on the conclusion that the trial court's finding on penetration was not sustainable, the supreme court reduced defendant's score, resulting in a Tier 1 classification. However, the supreme court did not disturb the notification requirement imposed by the appellate court. Finally, the supreme court rejected that contention that Megan's Law constituted cruel and unusual punishment in view of its determination to terminate registration and notification requirements at age 18 for adjudicated delinquents whose sex offenses were committed prior to age 14 and who proved by clear and convincing evidence that they were not likely to pose a threat to the safety of others.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the judgment in part, reversed the judgment in part, and modified the judgment in part.

 

152 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Do-Wop Corp. v. City of Rahway


A-25 September Term 2000


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


168 N.J. 191; 773 A.2d 706; 2001 N.J. LEXIS 784


March 27, 2001, Argued  
June 29, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant city filed a petition for certification requesting review of the judgment of the Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division, affirming the order of the trial court which concluded that plaintiff, a non-conforming adult business, was protected from application of a local zoning ordinance by the Municipal Land Use Law (MLUL). The supreme court granted the petition for certification.


OVERVIEW: In 1993, the city passed amendments to its code establishing minimum distance requirements with respect to adult entertainment centers. In 1995, upon denial of its license-renewal application, the business brought an action seeking a declaratory judgment that the amendments violated the MLUL. The trial court determined the amendments were invalid, and the appellate court affirmed the decision. On appeal, the city argued it properly denied the business's license-renewal application because the business's operation became an illegal establishment after N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:34-7 became effective, because that statute trumped the nonconforming use protection found in the MLUL. The supreme court concluded that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:34-7 gave the city the authority to deny the business's license-renewal application. In addition, the supreme court determined that because the preexisting use provision of the MLUL, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40:55D-68, protected nonconforming uses only from subsequent municipal zoning ordinances, and not subsequent statutory changes, the prohibition in N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:34-7 applied to the business's facility.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the judgment.

State v. Koskovich


A-22/23 September Term 1999


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


168 N.J. 448; 776 A.2d 144; 2001 N.J. LEXIS 662


September 26, 2000, Argued  
June 7, 2001, Decided


NOTICE:

[EDITOR'S NOTE: PART 2 OF 3. THIS DOCUMENT HAS BEEN SPLIT INTO MULTIPLE PARTS ON LEXIS TO ACCOMMODATE ITS LARGE SIZE. EACH PART CONTAINS THE SAME LEXIS CITE.]


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at State v. Vreeland, 2005 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 822 (App.Div., Jan. 21, 2005)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Koskovich, 2009 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 2983 (App.Div., Dec. 8, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal and cross-appeal from judgment of conviction and sentence of death in the Superior Court, Law Division, Sussex County.
State v. Koskovich, 161 N.J. 144, 735 A.2d 570, 1999 N.J. LEXIS 1648 (1999)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of conviction and sentence of death in the Superior Court, Law Division, Sussex County (New Jersey) for the murder of two pizza delivery persons. Defendant challenged numerous evidentiary rulings as well as the constitutionality of the death sentence and the penalty phase.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and an accomplice killed two pizza delivery persons after luring them to a secluded area in order to experience the "thrill" of killing. Defendant was convicted of numerous crimes resulting from that act as well as other related acts. In the sentencing phase, the jury found defendant's felony-murder aggravating factor outweighed the mitigating factors. Thus, defendant was given the death penalty. The appellate court held evidence obtained from defendant's bedroom was admissible since it was not admitted to show defendant was a bad person. Rather, it showed motive and that defendant killed the victims purposefully. The appellate court held the prosecutor's remark about a third victim did not prejudice defendant. However, the appellate court held the trial court erred in the sentencing phase by asking the jury to balance defendant's background with that of the victim. In addition, the trial court failed to inform the jury that defendant would likely spend his life in prison if they did not give the death penalty.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed. The trial court's imposition of consecutive sentences in respect to the non-capital convictions was affirmed except as to his sentence for possession of a firearm for an unlawful purpose which should have been merged with the murder conviction. The constitutionality of the death sentence was affirmed. Defendant's death sentence was set aside and the matter was remanded for a new penalty phase.

156 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


Nobrega v. Edison Glen Assocs.


A-130 September Term 1999


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


167 N.J. 520; 772 A.2d 368; 2001 N.J. LEXIS 527


January 16, 2001, Argued  
May 22, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 327 N.J. Super. 414, 743 A.2d 864 (2000).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant developers appealed the Superior Court, Appellate Division's (New Jersey), judgment for plaintiff owners. The New Jersey Supreme Court granted certification to decide if the New Residential Real Estate Off-Site Conditions Disclosure Act (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 46:3C-1 to -12, was retroactive, and if it allowed other statutory suits.


OVERVIEW: Owners purchased condominium units from developers. Developers knew the units were located near toxic waste sites, but did not tell owners. Owners had difficulty selling their units and discovered market values were declining. They sued for damages. Developers' motion to dismiss was granted. The appellate court reversed, and the supreme court granted certification. The supreme court held the Act barred all claims except those based on statutes imposing specific off-site disclosure obligations. The Act specified retroactive application. The supreme court declined to find the Act unconstitutional. It adopted a manifest injustice analysis, and held that retroactive application would harm owners. Because some owners had bought their units prior to the Act's passage, the matter was remanded to find which owners had claims under the Act, and which had claims under common law and prior laws.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was modified, and the matter was remanded for further proceedings.

 

157 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


McCann v. Clerk of Jersey City


A-92 September Term 2000


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


167 N.J. 311; 771 A.2d 1123; 2001 N.J. LEXIS 657


April 5, 2001, Argued Telephonically  
April 5, 2001, Decided  
June 1, 2001, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at     N.J. Super.     (2001).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff candidate appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) which reversed a decision of the Law Division allowing defendant city clerk to put candidate's name on a mayoral ballot. The Supreme Court of New Jersey granted certification.


OVERVIEW: Candidate, a former mayor, petitioned to be placed again on the ballot for mayor. The city clerk refused to process his petition because of his prior convictions. He sued, and the trial court ordered his name be placed on the ballot. The appellate court reversed after reviewing both the criminal forfeiture statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 51-2d, and the disqualification sections of the Faulkner Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §40:69-A-166. The matter was certified to the state supreme court. The supreme court reviewed both statutes. It found that candidate's convictions did not involve or touch on his prior position as mayor. Therefore, he was not subject to permanent disqualification under § 51-2d. The Faulkner Act was rationally related to the governmental purpose of public officials having high ethical standards. Candidate did not meet those standards, and was disqualified from running for office.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed as modified.

 

158 of 1283 DOCUMENTS


State v. Rumblin


A-89 September Term 1999


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


166 N.J. 550; 766 A.2d 1141; 2001 N.J. LEXIS 178


October 11, 2000, Argued  
February 28, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 326 N.J. Super. 296, 741 A.2d 138 (1999).
State v. Rumblin, 326 N.J. Super. 296, 741 A.2d 138, 1999 N.J. Super. LEXIS 397 (App.Div., 1999)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pled guilty to robbery, and other related charges. He was sentenced to 13 years, and was required to serve 85 percent before becoming eligible for parole. Defendant appealed the sentence asserting that N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-7.2 of the No Early Release Act (NERA) did not apply to him. The superior court, appellate division (New Jersey), affirmed. The state's highest court granted the petition for certification.


OVERVIEW: Defendant provided a factual basis to support his guilt on each of the six counts to which he entered guilty pleas. He admitted that his role was to assist in planning a robbery of the hotel, and to provide transportation by driving the stolen vehicle to and from the scene. The court found that it was undisputed that defendant acted purposely in planning, promoting, or facilitating the robbery. The court then determined that the state legislature did not intend the word "actor" to apply solely to principals. It intended to impose a greater punishment upon violent criminals. NERA did not differentiate among perpetrators of violent crimes based upon principal and accomplice classifications. Thus, although an accomplice could be found guilty of a lower degree of a violent offense than a principal if the two did not share the same mental culpability, principals and accomplices were treated the same for sentencing under NERA.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed because NERA did apply to unarmed accomplices. For NERA purposes, principals and accomplices were treated the same. Therefore, the sentencing was proper.

State v. Johnson


A-83 September Term 1999


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


166 N.J. 523; 766 A.2d 1126; 2001 N.J. LEXIS 179


October 11, 2000, Argued  
February 28, 2001, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 325 N.J. Super. 78, 737 A.2d 1140 (1999).
State v. Johnson, 325 N.J. Super. 78, 737 A.2d 1140, 1999 N.J. Super. LEXIS 326 (App.Div., 1999)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), upholding enhanced sentences under the No Early Release Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-7.2, for defendant's convictions of first-degree robbery, second-degree possession of a firearm with intent to use it unlawfully against a person, and third-degree possession of a firearm without a permit.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted in trial court for first-degree robbery, second-degree possession of a firearm with intent to use it unlawfully against a person, and third-degree possession of a firearm without a permit. The trial court imposed an enhanced sentence under the No Early Release Act (NERA), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-7.2, due to a violent crime finding. Defendant appealed, claiming that the mandatory-minimum sentence mandated by NERA and imposed by the trial court constituted cruel and unusual punishment. The appellate court affirmed. The court granted certiorari and affirmed, holding that the trial court's imposition of an enhanced sentence under NERA required defendant's jury to find, pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-7.2(e), beyond a reasonable doubt, that defendant committed a violent crime. The court further held that defendant's jury found the violent crime predicate beyond a reasonable doubt in reaching its verdict.


OUTCOME: Convictions and sentence were affirmed were because defendant's jury found the statutory violent crime predicate beyond a reasonable doubt in reaching its verdict, and, thus, defendant's enhanced sentence was proper.

Trantino v. New Jersey State Parole Bd.


A-136/A-137 September Term 1999


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


166 N.J. 113; 764 A.2d 940; 2001 N.J. LEXIS 4


September 25, 2000, Argued  
January 18, 2001, Decided


NOTICE:

 [***1] [EDITOR'S NOTE: PART 1 OF 2. THIS DOCUMENT HAS BEEN SPLIT INTO MULTIPLE PARTS ON LEXIS TO ACCOMMODATE ITS LARGE SIZE. EACH PART CONTAINS THE SAME LEXIS CITE.]


PRIOR HISTORY: On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 331 N.J. Super. 577 (2000).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant New Jersey State Parole Board sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Appellate Division, which reversed the board's decision denying parole to respondent prisoner.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, serving a life sentence for two murder convictions committed in 1963, became eligible for parole in 1979. Following several rejections, respondent again applied for parole in 1994. Appellant parole board again denied the application, based in part on the refusal of respondent state department of corrections to place respondent prisoner in an approved halfway house program. After a series of appeals and remands, the appellate division reversed in part, ordering appellant to grant parole. The supreme court affirmed in part, holding that (1) appellant's reliance on respondent's inadequate recollection of the details of his crimes from 35 to 40 years earlier constituted a clear abuse of discretion; (2) respondent's failure to comply with previous parole conditions from 1961 to 1963 was irrelevant to his fitness for parole in 1999; and (3) appellant ignored almost completely the 35 psychological evaluations of respondent from 1979 to 1997, the vast majority of which were supportive of parole. The court modified in part, ordering that respondent be placed in a halfway house for 12 months.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate division's decision in part, ordering that respondent prisoner be granted parole, but modified the decision to require that respondent be placed in an approved halfway house program for 12 months. Appellant's selective reliance on a small portion of the extensive record of psychological tests and opinions was arbitrary and capricious.

Cambria v. Soaries


A-24 September Term 2000


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


169 N.J. 1; 776 A.2d 754; 2000 N.J. LEXIS 1743


October 23, 2000, Argued  
October 25, 2000, Decided  
July 19, 2001, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 334 N.J. Super. 437, 759 A.2d 1227 (2000).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, an individual and a voter league, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of New Jersey, Appellate Division, which affirmed the trial court's denial of plaintiffs' request for injunctive relief and granted the motion to dismiss of defendants, the secretary of state and the attorney general, in an action seeking a declaratory judgment that a tax resolution violated N.J. Const. art. IX, para. 5.


OVERVIEW: A resolution was filed with the secretary of state for certification and submission to the division of elections calling for a proposed amendment to be placed on the ballot. Plaintiffs filed a complaint seeking a declaratory judgment that the resolution violated the New Jersey Constitution. Defendants moved to dismiss the complaint, which the trial court granted. The dismissal was affirmed by the appellate court. On appeal, plaintiffs contended the resolution violated N.J. Const. art. IX, para. 5, because it made more than one change to the constitution but allowed for only one vote. Specifically, plaintiffs argued the separate vote requirement prohibited the dedication of two types of state revenues to a state trust fund in one amendment. The supreme court held that to meet the separate vote requirement of N.J. Const. art. IX, para. 5, any proposed amendment could not make two or more changes to the constitution unless they were closely related to one another. Accordingly, the supreme court found that under that standard, the separate vote requirement was not violated because the amendment effected two discrete changes by the dedication of two new revenue streams to the fund.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court.

PLANNED PARENTHOOD OF CENT. NEW JERSEY v. FARMER


A-52 September Term 1999


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


165 N.J. 609; 762 A.2d 620; 2000 N.J. LEXIS 1024


January 19, 2000, Argued  
August 15, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Chancery Division, Bergen County.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The matter came before the court pursuant to its order on certification to the Superior Court, Chancery Division, Bergen County (New Jersey), in action brought by plaintiffs seeking a declaratory judgment and preliminary injunction precluding enforcement of the Parental Notification for Abortion Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 9:17A-1.1 to -1.12.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs brought an action seeking a declaratory judgment and preliminary injunction precluding enforcement of the Parental Notification for Abortion Act (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 9:17A-1.1 to -1.12. The trial court sustained the Act against plaintiffs' facial challenge. On appeal, under equal protection principles, plaintiffs challenged the Act because it conditioned a minor's right to obtain an abortion on parental notification, unless a judicial waiver was obtained, but did not impose corresponding limitations on a minor who sought medical and surgical care otherwise related to her pregnancy. The court reversed the judgment, deciding that the classification created by the legislature burdened the fundamental right of a woman to control her body and destiny, without adequate justification. The court found that the State failed to demonstrate a substantial need for the Act. Further, the court determined that plaintiffs presented compelling evidence that neither the interests of parents nor the interests of minors were advanced by the Act, and that there was no principled basis for imposing special burdens only on that class of minors seeking an abortion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment because the classification created by the legislature under the Parental Notification for Abortion Act burdened the fundamental right of a woman to control her body and destiny, without adequate justification. The State did not offer adequate justification for distinguishing between minors seeking an abortion and minors seeking medical and surgical care relating to their pregnancies.

In re Grant of the Charter Sch. Application of Englewood on the Palisades Charter Sch.


A-36/A-37/A-38 September Term 1999


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


164 N.J. 316; 753 A.2d 687; 2000 N.J. LEXIS 666


February 29, 2000, Argued  
June 28, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 320 N.J. Super. 174, 727 A.2d 15 (1999).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Essentially disagreeing with the state legislature's decision to allow charter schools to become part of the provision of public education, appellants, three school districts, challenged the facial validity of the Charter School Program Act of 1995 (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 18A:36A-1 to -18. The Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), upheld the constitutionality of the Act's provisions and appellants sought review.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, three school districts, challenged the grant of charters to newly-created charter schools within their respective districts, contending that the Charter School Program Act of 1995 (Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 18A:36A-1 to -18, was unconstitutional because it violated equal protection and due process, contravened the prohibition against donation of public funds for private purposes, and constituted an improper delegation of legislative power to a private body. The appellate court upheld the Act and appellants sought review. The state supreme court affirmed, holding that the Commissioner of Education was required to assess the racial impact that a charter school would have on the district of its residence. The court also held that the state constitutional obligation to prevent segregation in public schools must inform the Commissioner's review of a charter school application and that the Commissioner must consider the economic impact that a charter school would have on its district of residence when a district made a preliminary showing that satisfaction of the thorough-and-efficient education requirements would be jeopardized.


OUTCOME: Despite affirming the Charter School Program Act's constitutionality, the supreme court modified the lower court's judgment by holding that a state's duty to prevent segregation required an assessment of the racial impact that a charter school would have on a school district.

State v. Loyal


A-29 September Term 1999


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


164 N.J. 418; 753 A.2d 1073; 2000 N.J. LEXIS 663


March 13, 2000, Argued  
June 27, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), affirmed defendant's murder conviction and sentence, and defendant's certification petition was granted, defendant appealed the judgment, arguing that it was an abuse of discretion for the trial court to have declared a mistrial after discovering that defendant's counsel had previously represented a significant State witness.


OVERVIEW: Defendant killed his victim over a drug sale, allegedly at the drug-dealer's instruction. Witnesses included a friend of the buyer and the dealer's girlfriend, both of whom were called as State witnesses at defendant's murder trial. The buyer's friend identified defendant but the dealer's girlfriend recanted her identification. After learning defendant's counsel had recently represented the girlfriend on drug-related charges, the trial court sua sponte declared a mistrial, over the parties' waivers and objections, because of the appearance of impropriety. Defendant sought dismissal based on double jeopardy, which was denied. The girlfriend did not testify at the retrial. Defendant was convicted. He renewed his motion to dismiss based on double jeopardy or because there was no manifest necessity for mistrial. The motion was denied. The appellate court affirmed. The court granted certification and affirmed, holding the interests of both parties and the public could have been disserved by defense counsel's continued representation and there was manifest necessary for the mistrial.


OUTCOME: Judgment was affirmed, as trial court correctly found defense counsel's representation of defendant in drug-related homicide created unacceptable appearance of impropriety, justifying declaration of mistrial to vindicate public interest in fair trial, as counsel had recently represented recanting State's witness on drug charges. Defendant's retrial did not violate double jeopardy.

In re Passaic County Utils. Auth.


A-19 September Term 1999


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


164 N.J. 270; 753 A.2d 661; 2000 N.J. LEXIS 662; 30 ELR 20658


February 2, 2000, Argued  
June 22, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 321 N.J. Super. 186, 728 A.2d 323 (1999).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After plaintiff cities' petition for certification was granted, they appealed a New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, decision holding that the Municipal and County Utilities Authorities Law, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 40:14B-22.1, authorized defendant utility authority to impose on municipalities and commercial waste generators not using its facilities an environmental investment charge (EIC) to raise funds to pay obligations on bonded debt.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs and intervenors, municipalities and solid waste generators that no longer used defendant authority's facilities to dispose of solid waste, sued defendant authority and others over imposition of an environmental investment charge (EIC). Federal litigation created the perceived need for EICs, by holding New Jersey's statutory and regulatory scheme controlling disposal of state-generated solid waste discriminated against out-of-state solid waste facilities, violating the Commerce Clause. This permitted waste generators to bypass local facilities for cheaper out-of-state disposal. Counties and authorities experienced a drastic reduction in revenue, threatening substantial default on public debt. Authorization and imposition of EICs on plaintiffs and others was a direct consequence of the unprecedented federal dismantling of New Jersey's solid waste regulatory system. Plaintiffs unsuccessfully challenged the EIC in the lower court. On certiorari from the appellate court, the court reversed, holding the EIC was not statutorily authorized.


OUTCOME: Judgment was reversed but stayed to provide opportunity for legislative action. Charge imposed by defendant authority was not statutorily authorized because statute did not contemplate imposition of unprecedented charges on non-users of defendant's facilities to liquidate debt incurred in reliance on regulatory system declared unconstitutional by federal courts.

Green Party v. Hartz Mt. Indus.


A-59 September Term 1999


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


164 N.J. 127; 752 A.2d 315; 2000 N.J. LEXIS 656


March 28, 2000, Argued  
June 13, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 324 N.J. Super. 192, 735 A.2d 9 (1999).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, party and party organizer, challenged the judgment of the New Jersey Superior Court, Appellate Division, which reversed the judgment of the Chancery Division, by holding that a "reasonable business judgment" standard granted appellee mall broad powers to regulate the free speech right to leaflet, and upheld three regulations as a good faith means of protecting appellee's private property interests.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, party and organizer, sought access to respondent mall to get signatures for a candidate. The Supreme Court of New Jersey reviewed what standard governed reasonableness of a mall's regulations of time, place, and manner of expressive activity, and how that standard applied. Respondent had regulations requiring non-profit organizations to provide insurance, sign a "hold harmless" clause, and limit access to "one day, or a few days a year." Finding the business judgment rule was an improper standard, the court balanced individual free speech and assembly rights with the private property rights of mall owners. Noting that appellee accepted the risk of a general invitation to the public, and exploring the additional risk created by appellants' occasional presence, the court adopted a cost analysis to determine additional risk. Because appellee's inadequate proof failed to show real economic costs were an unfair burden, and that the insurance requirement was reasonably required for legitimate business objectives while preserving expressive rights, the court invalidated the regulations, reversed the appellate judgment, and reinstated the chancery judgment, as modified.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed and modified chancery judgment reinstated, because business judgment rule was improper standard, appellee accepted risk of general public invitation, and appellee's proof failed to show real economic costs of additional risk of appellants' occasional presence was an unfair economic burden and that insurance requirement was reasonably required for legitimate business objectives while preserving appellants' free speech rights.

OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs purchased land where they planned to build six two-story homes. Under a resolution which was not in the subdivision approval file, the land was to be used only as a buffer zone, and plaintiffs' applications for construction permits were denied. In re Adoption of a Child by W.P.


A-124 September Term 1998


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


163 N.J. 158; 748 A.2d 515; 2000 N.J. LEXIS 360


October 26, 1999, Argued  
April 6, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appeal from the Superior Court, Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Child V's adoptive parents appealed decision of Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey) affirming decision that New Jersey Adoption Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 9:3-37 to -56, and Grandparent Visitation Statute, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 9:2-7.1, did not conflict and scheduling hearing on biological grandparent's visitation request over parent's objections.


OVERVIEW: Child V was adopted by nonrelative persons pursuant to the New Jersey Adoption Act (Adoption Act), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 9:3-37 to -56. Child V's biological grandparents subsequently sought continued visitation with child V pursuant to the Grandparent Visitation Statute (Visitation Statute), N.J. Stat. Ann. § 9:2-7.1. The court found that, in the case of biological grandparents seeking visitation over the objection of nonrelative adoptive parents, there was a conflict between the Adoption Act and the Visitation Statute, and the Adoption Act would prevail to bar visitation by the biological grandparents.


OUTCOME: Appellate court's decision was vacated and trial court's order was reversed. Court held that, in case of biological grandparents seeking visitation with child V over objection of nonrelative adoptive parents, adoption statute was prevailing provision and it barred visitation by the biological grandparents.

Schneider v. Simonini


A-84 September Term 1998


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


163 N.J. 336; 749 A.2d 336; 2000 N.J. LEXIS 142


November 8, 1999, Argued  
March 6, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied February 20, 2001, Reported at: 531 U.S. 1146, 121 S. Ct. 1083, 148 L. Ed. 2d 959, 2001 U.S. LEXIS 1143.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Schneider v. Simonini, 531 U.S. 1146, 121 S. Ct. 1083, 148 L. Ed. 2d 959, 2001 U.S. LEXIS 1143 (2001)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 314 N.J. Super. 583, 715 A.2d 1018 (1998).
Schneider v. Simonini, 314 N.J. Super. 583, 715 A.2d 1018, 1998 N.J. Super. LEXIS 392 (App.Div., 1998)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs sought review of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), decision which reversed, on qualified immunity grounds, the trial court's judgment for plaintiffs in their claim for a constitutional violation brought under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, which arose from the erroneous arrest of plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff was arrested for suspicion of hijacking as part of an investigation by defendants, but was released when his mistaken identity was learned. Plaintiffs filed suit under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, and after a jury trial, judgment was entered against defendants, an investigator and his supervisor. The appellate court reversed, on qualified immunity grounds, and plaintiffs appealed; a split court affirmed. Three judges believed that the arrest warrant affidavit was deficient, and no exigent circumstances existed; therefore, probable cause did not exist. However, three judges agreed with the concurring/dissenting judge that, even if probable cause was lacking, defendant investigator was entitled to qualified immunity because he could have reasonably believed in the existence of probable cause. Defendant supervisor was not liable because he did not act with recklessness or deliberate indifference in supervising defendant investigator, and his level of personal involvement in plaintiff's arrest was insufficient to find him liable under § 1983.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Although probable cause might not have existed to arrest plaintiff, defendant investigator could reasonably have believed in its existence, so he was entitled to qualified immunity; defendant supervisor did not act with recklessness or deliberate indifference, and his level of personal involvement in the arrest was insufficient to result in § 1983 liability.

State v. Oliver


A-72 September Term 1998


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


162 N.J. 580; 745 A.2d 1165; 2000 N.J. LEXIS 138


October 25, 1999, Argued  
February 28, 2000, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Oliver, 180 N.J. 453, 852 A.2d 190, 2004 N.J. LEXIS 781 (2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 316 N.J. Super. 592, 720 A.2d 1001 (1998).
State v. Oliver, 316 N.J. Super. 592, 720 A.2d 1001, 1998 N.J. Super. LEXIS 501 (App.Div., 1998)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the decision of the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), which affirmed appellant's sentencing in the Superior Court, Law Division, under New Jersey's "three strikes" law, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-7.1a.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of first-degree robbery and aggravated assault. Defendant was sentenced to life under the "three-strikes" law, N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:43-7.1a. The court affirmed the two lower courts. There was no double jeopardy violation, as the enhanced sentence was a stiffened penalty for the latest crime. Separation of powers was not violated, as the legislature's power to enact such a law was only part of its power to enact mandatory sentencing laws generally. There was no ex post facto violation as the statute was on the books at the time of defendant's triggering offense. There was no equal protection violation, as the law was mandatory once defendant fell within the scope of the act, and there was no showing that the law had a disparate impact on minorities. The trial court properly determined the nature of defendant's previous convictions by at least a preponderance of the evidence.


OUTCOME: Sentence affirmed; there were no violations of double jeopardy, separation of powers, ex post facto, or equal protection; defendant was accorded due process as his previous convictions were determined at least by a preponderance of the evidence.

Wildoner v. Borough of Ramsey


A-74 September Term 1998


SUPREME COURT OF NEW JERSEY


162 N.J. 375; 744 A.2d 1146; 2000 N.J. LEXIS 18


October 13, 1999, Argued  
January 31, 2000, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On certification to the Superior Court, Appellate Division, whose opinion is reported at 316 N.J. Super. 487, 720 A.2d 645 (1998).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a grant of certification, defendants, a police department and officers, challenged a reversal, by the Superior Court, Appellate Division (New Jersey), of a summary judgment dismissing plaintiff senior citizen's action for false arrest and related torts. They argued that the circumstances justified the officers' belief that there was probable cause for arrest under the Prevention of Domestic Violence Act, N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 2C:25-17 to -33.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff and wife lived in senior citizens' home. Plaintiff's neighbor reported to the home's manager, who called the police, that plaintiff was being loud and abusive and threatening to throw knives at wife. Police officers arrived and arrested plaintiff. A criminal complaint was signed by police officers against plaintiff; plaintiff's wife refused to sign. Plaintiff sued defendants, the borough, the police department, and the officers. Defendants moved for summary judgment which the court below granted, dismissing plaintiff's complaint and holding defendants were immune from liability under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 and N.J. Stat. Ann. §§ 59:2-1, 3-3, and 9-2(d). Plaintiff appealed and the Appellate Division reversed, concluding plaintiff raised genuine issues of fact regarding probable cause for arrest and whether defendants were protected by good faith immunity. On further appeal, judgment was reversed and the order reinstated dismissing the complaint, as the court found the government officials were immune from prosecution.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed and the order of the trial court dismissing the complaint reinstated; defendants were performing discretionary functions and thus were shielded from liability for civil damages insofar as their conduct did not violate clearly established statutory or constitutional rights of which reasonable persons would have known.

Communications Workers of America v. New Jersey Civil Service Com'n


DOCKET NO. A-4912-13T3, A-3041-14T3, A-0230-15T3, A-0232-15T3, A-0274-15T3, A-0275-15T3


SUPERIOR COURT OF NEW JERSEY, APPELLATE DIVISION


447 N.J. Super. 584; 149 A.3d 844; 2016 N.J. Super. LEXIS 146


November 9, 2016, Argued 
December 1, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Approved for Publication December 1, 2016.
Certification granted by Communications Workers of Am. v. N.J. Civ. Serv. Comm'n, 2017 N.J. LEXIS 284 (N.J., Mar. 14, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the New Jersey Civil Service Commission, Docket Nos. 2016-561, 2016-778, and 2016-779.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The legislature and others challenged decisions of the Civil Service Commission (CSC) pertaining to a Job Banding Rule, N.J.A.C. 4A:3-3.2A, it adopted after the legislature invoked its veto power under N.J. Const. art. V, § 4, para. 6 (the Legislative Review Clause (LRC)), finding that the Rule conflicted with the Civil Service Act, which incorporated N.J. Const. art. VII, § 1, para. 2; while the Legislature was entitled to deference when it invalidated an administrative rule under the LRC, the appellate court could reverse if it had not complied with the procedural requirements of the LRC, it violated protections afforded by the U.S. or New Jersey Constitution, or its resolution was a patently erroneous interpretation of the language of the statute the rule implemented; [2]-As Legislature validly exercised its authority under the LRC, the court set aside the Rule.


OUTCOME: Appellate court reversed the CSC's decisions and set aside the Rule, in all of its amended forms.

State v. Ravi


DOCKET NO. A-4667-11T1, A-4787-11T1


SUPERIOR COURT OF NEW JERSEY, APPELLATE DIVISION


447 N.J. Super. 261; 147 A.3d 455; 2016 N.J. Super. LEXIS 122


February 3, 2016, Argued 
September 9, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Approved for Publication September 9, 2016.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Middlesex County, Indictment No. 11-04-00596.
State v. Ravi, 2012 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 1757 (App.Div., May 21, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In a case involving college roommates wherein defendant was convicted of multiple counts of invasion of privacy, bias intimidation, hindering prosecution, and tampering with evidence as a result of using a webcam to observe his roommate and another engage in homosexual activity, the bias intimidation convictions were reversed because they were predicated on N.J.S.A. § 2C:16-1(a)(3), which had been held unconstitutional in the New Jersey Supreme Court's holding in State v. Pomianek, thus, those convictions were void as a matter of law; [2]-The court further held that the evidence the State presented to prove the bias intimidation charges under N.J.S.A. § 2C:16-1(a)(3) permeated the entire case against defendant, rendering any attempt to salvage the convictions under the remaining charges futile.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed; case remanded to trial court for retrial on the remaining counts against defendant.

DOCKET NO. A-3323-15T1


SUPERIOR COURT OF NEW JERSEY, APPELLATE DIVISION

July 11, 2016, Decided

Appeal granted by, Stay granted by In re Declaratory Judgment Actions Filed by Various Municipalities, 2016 N.J. LEXIS 897 (N.J., Sept. 7, 2016)
Affirmed by, Modified by In re Declaratory Judgment Actions Filed By Various Municipalities, 2017 N.J. LEXIS 13 (N.J., Jan. 18, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from an interlocutory order of Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Ocean County, Docket No. L-2640-15.

In re Declaratory Judgment Actions Filed by Various Municipalities, 2016 N.J. Super. Unpub. LEXIS 356 (Law Div., Feb. 18, 2016)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Applying the Mount Laurel doctrine and the plain language of the Fair Housing Act of 1985 (FHA), N.J.S.A. §§ 52:27D-301 to 52:27D-329, including its unambiguous definition of prospective need, a forward projection of housing needs based on development and growth which is reasonably likely to occur in a region or a municipality, and following the New Jersey Supreme Court's admonition not to become an alternative administrative decision maker for unresolved policy issues surrounding the Third Round Rules, the court held that the FHA does not require a municipality to retroactively calculate a new separate and discrete affordable housing obligation arising during the gap period; [2]-The court acknowledged that the identifiable housing need that arose during the gap period would be captured by a town's present need obligation.

PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Hudson County, Indictment No. 05-11-02091.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Order directing defendant's individual registration record be included on the sex offender Internet registry, pursuant to N.J. Stat. Ann. § 2C:7-13(e) was affirmed because § 2C:7-13(e) required that the information of a moderate or low risk sex offender appear on the registry if the offender's conduct was characterized by a pattern of repetitive, compulsive behavior, which the trial court judge so found; [2]-The court held that § 2C:7-13(e) required that the information of a moderate or low risk sex offender appear on the registry if the offender's conduct was characterized by a pattern of repetitive, compulsive behavior, and the decision depended on the nature of his sexual offenses at the time he committed them, and not on his mental condition at the time of the tier hearing.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed.

PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Bergen County, Docket No. L-8850-11.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In medical negligence cases, when a plaintiff's counsel timely serves an affidavit of merit (AOM) and reasonably relies on the AOM and expert's curriculum vitae, which erroneously reflects that the witness is actively practicing medicine, and, through no fault of the plaintiff's counsel, the error is first discovered after the expiration of the 120-day deadline imposed under the AOM statute, N.J.S.A. §§ 2A:53A-26 to 2A:53A-29, exceptional circumstances exist requiring the judge to allow a plaintiff sufficient time to retain a different expert witness who is qualified under the New Jersey Medical Care Access and Responsibility and Patients First Act, N.J.S.A. §§ 2A:53A-37 to 2A:53A-42, issue a new AOM, and serve a corresponding expert report; [2]-As a result, the court held that the trial court erred by dismissing plaintiff's complaint with prejudice.

A.T. ex rel. T.T. v. Cohen


DOCKET NO. A-0589-14T1


SUPERIOR COURT OF NEW JERSEY, APPELLATE DIVISION


445 N.J. Super. 300; 137 A.3d 1218; 2016 N.J. Super. LEXIS 62


January 20, 2016, Argued 
April 27, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Approved for Publication April 27, 2016.


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Essex County, Docket No. L-3796-13.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether a minor plaintiff could take a voluntary dismissal without prejudice under R. 4:37-1(b) to avoid dismissal with prejudice of her medical malpractice complaint for counsel's failure to timely provide an affidavit of merit (AOM) under N.J.S.A. § 2A:53A-27. HOLDINGS: [1]-R. 4:37-1(b) could not be used to circumvent the time strictures in the AOM statute even if the statute of limitations had not expired; the failure of plaintiff's counsel to file an AOM within 120 days of the filing of the answer was not due to extraordinary circumstances but an "oversight"; [2]-After defendants moved for summary judgment, plaintiff's counsel requested leave to take a voluntary dismissal, though the statute of limitations would not expire for years as plaintiff was a minor; permitting a voluntary dismissal in these circumstances would render § 2A:53A-27 and its purpose meaningless.


OUTCOME: The orders were affirmed.

Ardan v. Board of Review


DOCKET NO. A-5826-13T2


SUPERIOR COURT OF NEW JERSEY, APPELLATE DIVISION


444 N.J. Super. 576; 134 A.3d 1018; 2016 N.J. Super. LEXIS 58


March 7, 2016, Argued 
April 25, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Approved for Publication April 25, 2016.
Certification granted by Ardan v. Board of Review, 2017 N.J. LEXIS 151 (N.J., Jan. 31, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Board of Review, Department of Labor and Workforce Development, Docket No. 412,283.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Appellant claimed she left her employment because of a medical condition that was aggravated by her working conditions and there was no other suitable work available, and the Board of Review determined that she was disqualified for benefits because she left work voluntarily without good cause attributable to the work, having accepted the appeal tribunal's findings that she left her job to accept other employment, never advised her employer she was leaving for medical reasons, and never afforded it an opportunity to provide an accommodation; the court held that the Board reasonably interpreted N.J.A.C. 12:17-9.3(b) to require an employee to notify an employer of a medical condition that was aggravated by the working conditions, request an accommodation, and afford the employer an opportunity to address the matter to determine whether there was other suitable work available.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

Certification granted by State v. Burkert, 227 N.J. 377, 151 A.3d 977, 2016 N.J. LEXIS 1013 (Sept. 20, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Union County, Municipal Appeal No. 6070.
State v. Burkert, 2016 N.J. LEXIS 1406 (N.J., Mar. 7, 2016)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The court reversed defendant's conviction for harassment under N.J.S.A. § 2C:33-4(c) because the State's evidence showed defendant engaged in protected speech and did not prove he engaged in harassing conduct, which is a required element of the offense, as a result of creating a flyer and adding uncouth commentary to the victim's wedding photograph; [2]-The court noted that § 2C:33-4 has not been enacted to proscribe mere speech, use of language, or other forms of expression as a conveyed opinion, even if stated in crude language, is not harassment and, consequently, proscribed speech must be uttered with the specific intention of harassing the listener.


OUTCOME: Judgment reversed.

State v. Gonzalez


DOCKET NO. A-0768-13T2


SUPERIOR COURT OF NEW JERSEY, APPELLATE DIVISION


444 N.J. Super. 62; 130 A.3d 1250; 2016 N.J. Super. LEXIS 16


October 27, 2015, Argued 
January 25, 2016, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Approved for Publication January 25, 2016.
Certification denied by State v. Gonzalez, 2016 N.J. LEXIS 589 (N.J., June 21, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from the Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Camden County, Indictment No. 12-02-0465.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Though defendant did not object to the jury instructions, the appellate court reversed his conviction for robbery and aggravated assault, concluding the instructions, which repeatedly used the ambiguous phrase "and/or" in guiding the jury as to the findings required, were clearly capable of producing an unjust result, as they were so confusing as to cause doubt as to whether the jury's verdict was unanimous or whether it convicted defendant by finding the presence of less than all the elements the State was required to prove; [2]-The trial court erred by instructing that defendant could be convicted of second-degree aggravated assault (N.J.S.A. § 2C:12-1(b)(1)) if the jury found he attempted to knowingly or recklessly cause seriously bodily injury; to be convicted of an attempted aggravated assault, it had to be shown he purposely attempted to cause serious bodily injury.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded for a new trial.

State v. Fitzpatrick


DOCKET NOS. A-2477-14T3, A-2478-14T3


SUPERIOR COURT OF NEW JERSEY, APPELLATE DIVISION

PRIOR HISTORY: On appeal from Superior Court of New Jersey, Law Division, Middlesex County, Indictment Nos. 14-02-00175 (A-2477-14) and 14-05-00620 (A-2478-14).


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]- In separate cases consolidated for appeal, at sentencing, the State requested drug offender restraining orders in accordance with N.J.S.A. § 2C:35-5.7(h), the sentencing court denied those applications, and the State appealed; the appellate court held that the governing statute, § 2C:35-5.7(k), required such appeals to be filed within 10 days of the date of sentencing, and because the State failed to file its notices of appeal in these matters within the 10-day period, the appellate court dismissed both appeals for lack of jurisdiction.


OUTCOME: The appeals were dismissed.
